# Commentary on Heidegger, *Plato: Sophist* (GA 19)

*Marburg lecture course, Winter Semester 1924/25. Edited by Ingeborg Schüßler. Citations follow the marginal pagination of the Klostermann Gesamtausgabe (GA 19), which the edition prints throughout; passages of the dialogue are additionally keyed to Stephanus numbers as Heidegger gives them.*

## Orientation: the traps this course sets

Before the text, the recurring misreadings to guard against. They are not incidental; the whole course turns on refusing them.

First, *truth is not correctness of judgment*. Heidegger reads Greek ἀλήθεια as un-concealment (*Un-verborgenheit*), a determination of beings-as-disclosed and of the Dasein that discloses them, not a property that propositions have when they match facts. The entire "Introductory Part" exists to win this back from the later logical tradition, and every later move in the *Sophist* interpretation presupposes it. Reading ἀλήθεια as propositional truth collapses the course at the root.

Second, *Aristotle is the way into Plato, not a falling-away from him*. Heidegger's hermeneutic principle — proceed from the clear to the obscure, hence from Aristotle back to Plato — is not a claim that Aristotle improves on Plato. It is a claim that Aristotle's conceptuality makes explicit what Plato does but leaves implicit. Treating the long Aristotle sections as mere preface misses that they are supplying the categories in which the *Sophist* will be read.

Third, *the "definitions" of the sophist are not definitions*. They are successive descriptions that fix the sophist in a shifting field of appearances; Heidegger says so directly. Reading the seven δι-αιρέσεις as failed attempts at a genus-species formula imports exactly the logic the course is trying to get behind.

Fourth, *"non-being" is not nothing, and its solution is not a logical trick*. The μὴ ὄν that the sophist hides in is otherness (ἕτερον), a positive structural moment of beings, not sheer negation. The κοινωνία τῶν γενῶν is an ontological result about how kinds are present with one another, not a device for licensing the copula.

Fifth, *λόγος is not "the proposition"*. Heidegger consistently refuses to let the assertoric sentence (λόγος ἀποφαντικός) count as the primary home of truth or the primary form of λόγος. λόγος is first speech that lets something be seen (δηλοῦν, ἀπο-φαίνεσθαι); the sentence-form and its "synthesis/diairesis" are derivative. The habit of hearing "logic" in every λόγος is the standing danger.

Sixth, *ontology here is read off Dasein*. Being means presence (*Anwesenheit*), and the Greeks read that sense of being off the world of production and use; Heidegger recovers the ontology by way of an analytic of the Dasein for whom beings are present. Constitution-in-Dasein is not psychologism, and presence is not a timeless logical status — it is a temporal determination the course will keep exposing.

Terms held in Greek or German throughout, with the English fixed on first use: ἀλήθεια (un-concealment), ἀληθεύειν (disclosing, being-in-the-truth), λόγος (speech/discourse), δηλοῦν (making-manifest), οὐσία (being, in the Greek sense of presence/standing-presence), Dasein (the human being in its being), Sorge (care). Ordinary Greek and German is rendered in English.

## Obituary for Paul Natorp

The course opens not with Plato but with the dead. A Plato lecture in Marburg must honor Natorp, who had died over the summer and whose last teaching had itself been a Plato seminar preparing a revision of his *Plato's Theory of Ideas*. Heidegger's tribute is exact about what he is praising: not the correctness of Natorp's Plato but its *level* — the height of philosophical understanding it demanded and carried through with, in Heidegger's word, unmatched one-sidedness. He insists the one-sidedness is not a fault but the mark of real penetration. What the book taught was a conscience: that command of the material is not yet understanding, and that understanding cannot be assembled out of average philosophical erudition. The proof of the work's worth is that it provoked contradiction, i.e. forced others to think — and that its level has not since been reached.

Heidegger then places Natorp historically with unusual care, because the placing is itself philosophical. Natorp's hermeneutic situation was fixed by Kant and the Marburg school, i.e. by epistemology and theory of science; he read the history of Greek philosophy within the horizon and the limits of Marburg Neo-Kantianism. Against Aristotle he took a sharp line, treating him as a realist and the medieval reception as dogmatism — yet Heidegger stresses that this was not ignorance of Aristotle. On the contrary, Natorp anticipated results only now being recovered. The double-edged compliment discloses Heidegger's own program: the very Aristotle whom Marburg subordinated is about to become the key to Plato.

The tribute widens to Natorp's range — social philosophy, pedagogy, finally philosophy of religion — and to his founding place in the Marburg question of psychology: how the reigning natural-scientific psychology could be built into philosophy, a question routed through Descartes and predetermined by Brentano's *Psychology from an Empirical Standpoint*. Heidegger notes that Natorp was, of all people, best prepared for the confrontation with Husserl, and that his impulses were decisive for Husserl himself. Beneath the eulogy runs a map of the present: the new philosophy grew out of three books and standpoints at once — Cohen's *Kant's Theory of Experience*, Brentano's *Psychology*, Dilthey's *Life of Schleiermacher*. Marburg's mission was twofold: to preserve and retake the tradition, and to cultivate rigor of conceptual work.

The close turns personal and generational. Going beyond Kant became possible only because Marburg first forced philosophy back to him. Natorp was among the very few German professors who understood what the German youth wanted in 1913 on the Hohe Meißner — to shape their lives out of inner truthfulness and self-responsibility. Many of those best have fallen; Heidegger says our Dasein is being slowly shifted onto new foundations, and the young have their share in that task. He honors Natorp's silence under a late unjust attack ("I will keep silent") and the thoroughness and breadth of a learning no longer attained. The final note is the one that will govern the whole course: Natorp had learned from a real understanding of Greek philosophy that there is still no cause to be especially proud of philosophy's progress. The obituary thus does covert methodological work — it sets the demand (level, not erudition), names the target (Marburg's epistemological framing of the Greeks), and points the way (through Aristotle, through the Greeks' own understanding).

## PRELIMINARY CONSIDERATIONS

### §1. The necessity of a twofold preparation for interpreting Plato's dialogues

The course will interpret two late dialogues of Plato (in the event, only the *Sophist* was completed; the *Philebus* was the intended second). Heidegger justifies the narrow focus: the content transacted in these conversations demands an unusual intensity of understanding, and the matters at issue must be appropriated in a way that keeps re-presenting them freshly. His examples are the pairs the *Sophist* lives on — being and non-being, truth and semblance, knowledge and opinion, assertion and concept, value and disvalue. These are basic concepts one thinks one understands at first hearing, and precisely that false familiarity is the enemy: one takes them for self-evident, as though nothing further were to be settled about them. The interpretation is meant to make us at home in what these concepts actually mean.

Hence the twofold preparation. First, an orientation about *how* such peculiar objects — being and non-being, truth and semblance — become visible at all, i.e. where one must look to have anything to say about them. Second, a preparation for grasping the past that meets us in Plato in the right way, so that we do not read arbitrary standpoints into it. The two preparations answer to two things: the *kind* of objects under discussion, and the *ground* from which we reach the historical past. The first is a matter of method — phenomenology — and Heidegger warns that what he offers now is only a short directive; the point is to work into this manner of research slowly, in traffic with the matters themselves, across the course.

#### a) Philosophical-phenomenological preparation. The method and aim of phenomenology

Phenomenology is unpacked from its Greek components: φαινόμενον, what shows itself, and λέγειν, to address. So stated, it would be indistinguishable from any science — botany too describes what shows itself. What distinguishes the phenomenological way of looking is the definite respect in which it sets what shows itself and follows it out: the primary respect is the question of the *being* of this being. What shows itself, taken in that respect, Heidegger names *phenomenon* — and warns at once against confusing "phenomenon" with "semblance" (*Schein*) or "appearance" (*Erscheinung*). Phenomenon means the being itself as it shows itself, in the varying possibilities of its being-disclosed. This way of looking is basically the obvious one, no technique, alive in any originally philosophizing work; hence it can be learned from the simple, originary looking of the Greeks. In the present, it first won through in Husserl's *Logical Investigations*, whose theme is certain phenomena from the domain of consciousness or lived experience — acts of knowing and judging, described as to their structure. The lineage is deliberately drawn: phenomenology is not a modern invention but the recovery of the Greek way of seeing, and Husserl is its contemporary breakthrough — which is why the course can move between Husserl and the Greeks without anachronism.

#### b) Historical-hermeneutic preparation. The principle of hermeneutics: from the clear into the obscure. From Aristotle to Plato

Understanding history can mean nothing but understanding ourselves — not in the sense of taking stock of how things stand with us, but of learning what we are to do. To appropriate a past is to know oneself in debt to it. Heidegger states the ethical charge of the hermeneutic outright: philosophy is guilty of an omission, a not-taking, whenever it imagines it can start fresh, take the easy road, and let itself be casually stimulated by some philosopher. History so understood raises rather than lowers the difficulty of understanding the past. To enter Plato's actual philosophical work we must be assured of the right access from the start, i.e. must strike precisely on what is *not* written there. This requires a guiding thread.

Here Heidegger announces the course's methodological reversal and defends it. The usual way reads Plato forward from Socrates and the pre-Socratics; he will go backward, from Aristotle to Plato. The warrant is the old hermeneutic maxim: interpret from the clear into the obscure. The presupposition is that Aristotle understood Plato — no bold claim, given the level of Aristotle's work. Heidegger generalizes: later thinkers always understand their predecessors better than the predecessors understood themselves, and this is the very mark of creative research, that in what is decisive it does not understand itself. Reading Plato by way of Aristotle carries *no value-judgment* against Plato — the guard the misreading-orientation flagged. What Aristotle says is what Plato handed him, only developed more radically and more scientifically. The concrete preparation will be the question of λόγος as ἀληθεύειν across the different domains of beings — the eternal (ἀεί), and what can-be-otherwise (ἐνδέχεται ἄλλως). Since no greater thinker followed Aristotle, one must leap directly into his work to get oriented; the course gives only a schematic sketch restricted to basic questions. Plato is cited by Stephanus; the interpretation confines itself to *Sophist* and *Philebus*, drawing on the *Parmenides* for ontology and the *Theaetetus* for the phenomenology of knowledge when difficulties demand it.

#### c) First indication of the theme of the *Sophist*. The sophist. The philosopher. The being of beings

In the *Sophist* Plato regards human Dasein in one of its most extreme possibilities: philosophical existence. He shows *indirectly* what the philosopher is by laying out what the sophist is — and he does this not by drawing up an empty program of what one must do to be a philosopher, but by philosophizing. One can concretely say what the sophist, the genuine non-philosopher, is only if one oneself lives in philosophy. Hence the dialogue's peculiar entanglement: precisely by way of reflection on the being of beings does Plato win the right ground for interpreting the sophist *in his being*. So the course will first look to Aristotle for what he says about beings and being. The point folds the two preparations together: the "definition of the philosopher" is inseparable from an ontology, because to fix the sophist one must already be moving in the question of being.

### §2. Orientation toward Plato's *Sophist* by way of Aristotle

#### a) The theme: the being of beings

"Beings" are taken at first wholly indeterminately — the beings of the world in which Dasein is, and the beings of Dasein itself. These are at first disclosed only within a certain circuit: the human being lives in an environing world (*Umwelt*) opened up only within limits. Out of this natural orientation grows something like science, an elaboration of the existing world and of one's own Dasein in definite respects. But what is there at first is not yet *known* in the sense of a science; consciousness has of it a certain view, δόξα, taking the world as it at first seems and shows itself, δοκεῖ. In natural Dasein definite views and opinions about life and its sense first form — and the sophist and the rhetor move within them. Insofar as scientific research sets out from this natural Dasein, it must press *through* these opinions, these provisional determinations, and seek the way to the matters themselves, so that they become more determinate and the concepts are won from them. This is not obvious for everyday Dasein and it is hard for it to strike beings in their being — hardest for a people like the Greeks, whose daily life hung on speech. Every dialogue shows Plato underway: the breaking-up of fixed sentences and the coming-to-understanding of the phenomena, and at once how Plato must halt and fails to break through. To see Plato at work and re-enact it rightly needs the right standpoint, which Aristotle supplies by telling us which beings he — and thereby Plato and the Greeks — had in view, and what the modes of access to them are. The interpretation has no other task than to talk the conversation through once more, as originarily as possible.

#### b) The mode of access: knowledge and truth, ἀλήθεια

The mode of access and dealing that discloses beings as beings-of-such-a-kind, and takes what is so disclosed into possession, is customarily called *knowing*. The knowing that unlocks beings is "true"; a cognition that has grasped beings expresses itself and settles into a sentence, an assertion, which one then determines as a truth. So it is at the concept — or better the *phenomenon* — of truth, as the Greeks determined it, that we will gain light on what knowing was for the Greeks and what it is "in its relation" to beings. For presumably they laid out the concept of "truth" as a "property" of knowing with a view to the knowing alive in their own Dasein. Heidegger will not run through the history of Greek logic but seeks the orientation where, within it, the determination of truth reached its peak: in Aristotle. From the still-living logical tradition one knows that truth is expressly determined by appeal to Aristotle — who first stressed that a *judgment* is what is true, that "true" and "false" are found primarily in judgments, that truth is "judgment-truth." The course flags in advance that this determination is in a certain way correct but superficial: the phenomenon of truth is to be discussed and *grounded* beneath judgment-truth, not settled by it.

### §3. First characterization of ἀλήθεια

#### a) The meaning of the word ἀλήθεια. ἀλήθεια and Dasein

The Greeks have a telling word for truth: ἀλήθεια, whose α is privative. They use a *negative* expression for something we understand positively — as German "*Un*-vollkommenheit" (im-perfection) is negative for a matter we take positively. The negation is of a special kind: what we call imperfect is not simply unrelated to perfection but oriented *toward* it — it is, with respect to perfection, not as it could be. Such privation is often hidden in words: "blind" is a negative expression, yet only what *can* see can be blind, only what can speak can keep silent. So ἀλήθεια means un-concealment, being-uncovered; and the privative form betrays that the Greeks understood the unconcealment of the world as something that must first be *wrested*, that at first and for the most part is not available. The world is at first, if not wholly, closed off; disclosive knowing has at first not yet penetrated; the world is disclosed only in the nearest circuit of the environing world, so far as natural needs demand. And precisely what was perhaps originarily disclosed in natural consciousness is for the most part re-covered and distorted by *speech*: opinions harden into concepts and sentences that get repeated, so that what was originarily disclosed is buried again. Everyday Dasein thus moves in a *double* concealment — first mere ignorance, then the far more dangerous concealment in which what was uncovered turns to un-truth through idle talk (*Gerede*). Against this double concealment philosophy faces a double task: to break through positively to the matters for the first time, and simultaneously to take up the fight against idle talk. Both drives are the real motors of the work of Socrates, Plato, and Aristotle — their war on rhetoric and sophistry is the proof. The clarity of Greek philosophy was thus not a gift of some serene "cheerfulness" handed to the Greeks in their sleep; closer study shows the exertion it took to press through to being *and* through idle talk at once. We should not expect to get the matters more cheaply, burdened as we are by a rich and tangled tradition.

Heidegger then fixes the ontological status of ἀλήθεια with care, because getting it wrong is the standing trap. Un-concealment is a determination of *beings insofar as they are encountered*. It does not belong to being as though beings could not be without it: nature is present-at-hand even before it is uncovered. ἀλήθεια is a peculiar being-character of beings insofar as they stand in relation to a looking-at, to a disclosing that looks around within beings, to a knowing. Yet ἀληθές is *also* in the ὄν, a character of being itself — insofar as being is presence (*Anwesenheit*) and this presence is appropriated in λόγος, "is" in it. The disclosing to which ἀλήθεια is relative is *itself* a being — not of the world that gets disclosed, but a way of being of the being we call human Dasein. Insofar as disclosing and knowing have ἀλήθεια as their aim, the Greeks name this, by what it accomplishes, ἀληθεύειν — left untranslated: to be disclosive, to take the world out of closure and concealment. And this is a way of being of human Dasein. It shows itself first in speaking, in talking-with-one-another, in λέγειν.

#### b) ἀλήθεια and language (λόγος). ἀλήθεια as a way of being of the human (ζῷον λόγον ἔχον), i.e. as a way of life (ψυχή)

ἀληθεύειν shows itself first in λέγειν, and speaking is the basic constitution of human Dasein: in speaking Dasein expresses itself, and it does so by speaking *about* something, about the world. This λέγειν was so obtrusive and everyday for the Greeks that they won the definition of the human being from it — ζῷον λόγον ἔχον. Alongside runs the definition of the human as the being that *reckons*, ἀριθμεῖν — reckoning here not counting but counting-on something, being-calculative; number itself is a later development from this original sense. Aristotle determines λόγος — later called *enuntiatio* and judgment — in its basic function as ἀπόφανσις, ἀποφαίνεσθαι, δηλοῦν, making-manifest. Its modes of enactment are κατάφασις and ἀπόφασις, affirming and denying, later called positive and negative judgment. Even denial, the withdrawing of a determination, is a *letting-see*, an uncovering: I can deny a determination of a thing only insofar as I exhibit the thing. In all these modes speaking, φάναι, is a way of being of life. As vocalization it is no mere noise, ψόφος, but ψόφος σημαντικός, a noise that indicates — φωνή and ἑρμηνεία. Heidegger cites *De Anima* B 8, 420b5: φωνή is a noise proper only to a living being; only animals can utter sounds. The ψυχή is the οὐσία ζωῆς, what makes up the genuine being of a living thing; *De Anima* B 1, 412a27 defines it ontologically as the first entelechy of a natural body potentially having life — the soul is what constitutes the living thing's genuine being-present, in a being that is potentially living. Life is here co-defined as *movement* — not merely locomotion but every kind of change, μεταβολή, the being-present of turning-over; so every πρᾶξις, every νοεῖν, is movement. Speaking is thus φωνή, a vocalizing bearing an ἑρμηνεία within it, saying something about the world that can be understood; and as such it is a way of being of the living, a mode of ψυχή. Aristotle gathers this way of being as ἀληθεύειν: human life in its being, ψυχή, is speaking, interpreting, i.e. it enacts ἀληθεύειν. Aristotle not only grounded this ontologically in *De Anima* but on that ground first saw and interpreted the *manifold* of ἀληθεύειν, its different possibilities — carried out in *Nicomachean Ethics* VI, chs. 2–6 (1138b35 ff.), to which the course now turns.

## INTRODUCTORY PART: Winning ἀλήθεια as the ground of Platonic ontology. Interpretations of Aristotle (*Nicomachean Ethics* VI and X, chs. 6–8; *Metaphysics* I, chs. 1–2)

### First Chapter: The preparatory survey of the modes of ἀληθεύειν (ἐπιστήμη, τέχνη, φρόνησις, σοφία, νοῦς) (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 2–6)

#### §4. The significance of ἀληθεύειν in Aristotle for Platonic ontology

##### a) The five modes of ἀληθεύειν (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 3). ἀληθεύειν as the ground of ontology. ἀλήθεια as a determination of the being of Dasein (ἀληθεύει ἡ ψυχή)

Aristotle opens the real inquiry at VI, 3 (1139b15) with a programmatic list of the modes in which the soul discloses beings by affirming or denying: five in number — τέχνη, ἐπιστήμη, φρόνησις, σοφία, νοῦς. Heidegger renders them as knowing-one's-way-around in producing and handling; science; circumspection or insight; genuine understanding; and perceptive-taking that apprehends what it takes. Aristotle appends ὑπόληψις (holding-something-to-be-something) and δόξα (view, opinion). These two characterize Dasein in its ἐνδέχεται — its can-be-otherwise — because in them Dasein *can deceive itself* (ἐνδέχεται διαψεύδεσθαι): δόξα is not straightforwardly false, but it *can* be false, can distort beings and shove itself in front of them. All these modes stand in connection with λόγος; all but νοῦς are μετὰ λόγου — there is no circumspection and no understanding that is not also speaking. τέχνη is the know-how that guides producing (ποίησις), not the handling itself; ἐπιστήμη is what we call science; φρόνησις is circumspection; σοφία the genuine understanding; νοῦς the apprehending that takes in what it apprehends. Heidegger flags that νοεῖν surfaces already at the decisive beginning of Greek philosophy, where its fate and the West's was decided — in Parmenides: the apprehending and the apprehended are the same.

Following Aristotle on the modes of uncovering yields three things: an orientation about the possible ways open to Greek Dasein for experiencing and interrogating the beings of the world; a preview of the several *regions* of being disclosed in the several modes, together with their characteristic being-determinations; and a first sense of the *limits* within which Greek research held itself. With this threefold gain we reach the ground on which Plato's inquiries into the being of beings-as-world and the being of beings-as-human-Dasein — philosophical-scientific existence — move, so that we can re-enact the possible paths of Platonic research.

The load-bearing point is what Aristotle says *before* listing the modes: ἀληθεύει ἡ ψυχή. Truth is indeed a character of beings insofar as they are encountered, but in the proper sense it is a *determination of the being of human Dasein itself*. Every effort at knowledge must win through against the concealment of beings, which is threefold — ignorance, reigning opinion, error. So it is human Dasein that is properly true; it *is in the truth* (if we translate ἀλήθεια as truth). Being-true, being-in-the-truth, as a determination of Dasein, means: to have the beings one deals with available *un-concealed*. What Aristotle grasps more sharply, Plato already saw — the soul that of itself sets out toward truth (ἡ ἐπ' ἀλήθειαν ὁρμωμένη ψυχή, *Soph.* 228c), while the many are said to stand far from the unconcealment of things (τῶν πραγμάτων τῆς ἀληθείας ἀφεστῶτας, *Soph.* 234c). The soul, the being of the human, strictly speaking, is what is in the truth.

Heidegger then blocks two ways of misconstruing this truth-as-unconcealment. It amounts to *matter-directedness* (*Sachlichkeit*), the comportment in which beings are there as they are in themselves — rightly understood "objectivity." But it does *not* contain objectivity-as-universal-validity: universal bindingness has nothing to do with truth, since something can be universally valid and binding yet false — most prejudices and self-evidences are precisely universal validities that *distort* beings — while what is binding for only a single person can be true. Nor does truth-as-uncovering prejudge that genuine uncovering must be theoretical knowing, or some privileged form of it like science or mathematics, as though the strictest science were the truest and only what meets mathematics' ideal of evidence counted as true. Unconcealment measures itself by the beings themselves, not by a concept of scientificity. Yet Heidegger notes the historical irony: it was this very Greek interpretation of truth that led to seeing theoretical knowing as the proper ideal and orienting all cognition to it — the root of a fateful possibility.

##### b) The history of the concept of truth

ἀληθές means, by the sense of the word, un-concealed. Uncovered first of all are the things, the πράγματα — τὸ πρᾶγμα ἀληθές. This uncoveredness accrues to the thing not insofar as it *is* but insofar as it is *encountered*, is the object of dealings; so uncoveredness is a specific accomplishment of Dasein, whose being lies in the soul — ἀληθεύει ἡ ψυχή. The nearest mode of uncovering is speaking about things, so λόγος primarily takes over the function of ἀληθεύειν: ἀληθεύει ὁ λόγος, and specifically λόγος *qua* λέγειν. But since every λόγος is a speaking-out, a communicating, λόγος takes on at once the sense of the λεγόμενον, the spoken. λόγος thus means both the speaking (λέγειν) and the spoken (λεγόμενον); and since it is λόγος that ἀληθεύει, λόγος *qua* λεγόμενον can be called ἀληθής — though strictly it is not. Insofar as speaking is a being-spoken-out, gaining its own existence in the sentence so that a cognition is *stored* in it, the λόγος *qua* λεγόμενον counts as true; and this is the way truth is *at first* there. In nearest talking-together one holds to what is spoken, and hearing it does not necessarily re-enact the original cognition: understanding a sentence, I need not re-perform each of its steps. I can say "it rained a few days ago" without making the rain present; I can recite and understand sentences without any original relation to the beings I speak of. In this peculiar blurredness all sentences are repeated and understood; the sentences come to a peculiar existence of their own, one orients oneself by them, they become correctnesses, so-called "truths," without the original function of ἀληθεύειν ever being carried out. One goes along with the sentences on trust; λέγειν wins a peculiar free-standingness over against the πράγματα; one holds oneself in idle talk (*Gerede*), which has its own bindingness for anyone who wants to orient himself in the world without appropriating everything originally.

This free-standing sentence is what later reflection — having lost the original position — took to be the bearer of truth and falsity. Since such a sentence can be true or false, and is taken as true without knowing whether it *is*, the question arises: in what does its truth consist? How can a sentence, a judgment, a determination of the psychical, agree with the things? Take ψυχή as subject and λόγος and λέγειν as lived experiences, and one has the problem: how can subjective experiences agree with the object? — whence truth as *correspondence* of judgment with object. Heidegger then dispatches two positions this breeds. One says the correspondence-theory is absurd: to say a thing agrees with the judgment I must already have known the thing, must already have uncovered the objective in order to measure the subjective against it — so the truth of *having-known* is presupposed for the truth of *knowing*, and the theory collapses. A newer epistemology goes a step further: knowing is judging, judging is affirming, affirming is acknowledging, what is acknowledged is a value, a value is present as an *ought*, hence the object of knowledge is really an ought and truth is a *value* — a structure then stretched over all regions until one says "God is a value." Both, Heidegger implies, are artifacts of fastening onto the free-standing affirmative judgment while ignoring beings in their being.

This history is not accidental; it is grounded in Dasein itself, insofar as Dasein moves in its nearest everyday manner of knowing — λόγος — and, in falling to the world, falls to the λεγόμενον. As λόγος becomes mere λεγόμενον, one no longer understands that the "problem" lies *in λόγος itself and its mode of being*. One could have learned precisely from Aristotle and Plato that this spoken-out λόγος is the *externalized* one. Heidegger fixes the results: ἀληθές is the πρᾶγμα; ἀληθεύειν is a determination of the being of life, ascribed especially to λόγος; Aristotle distinguishes the five modes primarily with a view to λέγειν — they are μετὰ λόγου. The μετά does not make speech an arbitrary annex: bound to μέσον, the middle, it means that λέγειν is *within* them, in their midst. Knowing and regarding is always a speaking, voiced or not; all disclosive comportment, not only everyday self-orienting but scientific knowing too, is enacted in speaking. λέγειν primarily takes over the function of ἀληθεύειν, and λέγειν is for the Greeks the basic determination of the human — ζῷον λόγον ἔχον. It is on this ground, in the field of the λόγον ἔχον, that Aristotle wins the first articulation of the five modes.

#### §5. The first articulation of the five modes of ἀληθεύειν (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 2)

##### a) The two basic kinds of λόγον ἔχον: ἐπιστημονικόν and λογιστικόν

Aristotle lays down (VI, 2; 1139a6) that the λόγον ἔχον has two basic kinds. The first is the ἐπιστημονικόν — the λόγος that helps develop *knowledge*. The second is the λογιστικόν — the λόγος that helps develop βουλεύεσθαι, circumspective considering, deliberation. With a view to these Aristotle sorts the modes: under the ἐπιστημονικόν fall ἐπιστήμη and σοφία; under the λογιστικόν fall τέχνη and φρόνησις. νοῦς at first seems to fall outside — but all four modes *are* present *in* νοεῖν; they are a determinate mode of its enactment, namely διανοεῖν, thinking-through.

The division of ἐπιστημονικόν and λογιστικόν is won from *the beings themselves* that come to appropriation in ἀληθεύειν. The one is that with which we look at beings whose ἀρχαί cannot be otherwise (a6 ff.), beings with the character of ἀίδιον, always-being (b23). The other is that with which we look at beings that *can* be otherwise (ἐνδεχόμενον ἄλλως ἔχειν, 1140a1) — the beings τέχνη and φρόνησις have to do with. τέχνη deals with things still to be made, not yet what they will be; φρόνησις makes the *situation* accessible, and the circumstances of every action are always again other; whereas ἐπιστήμη and σοφία bear on what is always already there, not first produced.

The decisive claim: this first and most primitive *ontological* distinction does not first arise in philosophical reflection but belongs to *natural Dasein itself* — not constructed, but lying in the horizon within which natural Dasein's ἀληθεύειν already moves. In its natural way of being, Dasein is occupied with the things that are objects of making and of nearest everyday concern; and this whole environing world is not sealed in itself but a definite cut-out of the world as such. House and yard have their being under the sky, under the sun that runs its course each day, appearing and vanishing evenly again and again. This nature-world, always as it is, is the background from which what-can-be-otherwise stands off. The distinction is wholly originary — so it is wrong to say two *regions* of being are laid side by side in theoretical regard; rather this distinction *is* the world and its first ontological articulation as such. Hence Aristotle at once ties the principle of the distinction to the beings: because these are different in kind, the parts of the soul naturally directed to each are different in kind too — since knowing (γνῶσις, meaning not theoretical cognition but ἀληθεύειν in the widest sense) belongs to them by a certain likeness and kinship, being as it were *at home* with the beings they uncover (1139a8 ff.). Against the diversity of beings, each way of soul-comportment must differ in its being-structure.

##### b) The task and first outline of the inquiry

Aristotle now interrogates the two basic kinds more closely as to which is the μάλιστα ἀληθεύειν, which most takes beings out of concealment — ληπτέον ἄρα ἑκατέρου τούτων τίς ἡ βελτίστη ἕξις (a15). For each, the βελτίστη ἕξις is the most proper possibility of uncovering beings *at* themselves and keeping them as uncovered, i.e. of *being-toward* them as *being-alongside*. For the ἐπιστημονικόν this highest possibility lies in σοφία; for the λογιστικόν, in φρόνησις. There are thus differences and *degrees* of disclosive access and safekeeping: the world is not evenly uncovered for Dasein, and Dasein, though it has in itself the possibility of disclosing world and itself, is not in an even disclosedness. Aristotle's closer analysis does not begin from the highest modes but from those first seen in Dasein — ἐπιστήμη (ch. 3) and τέχνη (ch. 4) — advancing by *showing that these are not the highest*. Throughout he takes up the *ordinary* understanding of the modes: this is no invented concept of knowing and know-how, but the sharpening of what one already means by them. And the manner of regard is the one already alive in the basic distinction: oriented by the respective beings that are in each case disclosed.

#### §6. The essential determination of ἐπιστήμη (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 3)

Aristotle begins the closer regard with ἐπιστήμη. The word has a looser sense in which it means as much as τέχνη, any knowing-one's-way-about — a sense Aristotle also uses — but here it carries the sharpened sense of *scientific* knowing. He opens by insisting one must look sharply at the phenomenon itself and not merely illustrate it from what it is not, δεῖ ἀκριβολογεῖσθαι καὶ μὴ ἀκολουθεῖν ταῖς ὁμοιότησιν (1139b18). The general guide for the whole analysis is a double question: what do the beings look like that each mode uncovers, and does the mode also disclose the *ἀρχή* of those beings? Why the second question matters is not yet evident — and Heidegger lets that suspense stand, because the answer is the whole point of the chapter.

##### a) The object of ἐπιστήμη: the always-being (ἀίδιον). ἐπιστήμη as a ἕξις of ἀληθεύειν. The interpretation of being out of time (ἀίδιον, ἀεί, αἰών)

Aristotle starts from how beings are understood in the strictest sense of knowing: ὑπολαμβάνομεν, ὃ ἐπιστάμεθα, μὴ ἐνδέχεσθαι ἄλλως ἔχειν (b20) — what we know cannot be otherwise, must always be so. Only what always is can be known; what can be otherwise is not known in the strict sense. For if what can also be otherwise comes to stand *outside* the regarding (ἔξω τοῦ θεωρεῖν, b21) — if I am not currently alongside it — it may meanwhile change, while I hold my view fast, and my view has become false. Knowing is distinguished precisely by this: the being I know I *still* know even outside the actual looking, because the object of knowing always *is*. And so, if it is known, this knowing as ἀληθεύειν is itself *always*. Knowing is a having-uncovered, a safekeeping of the uncoveredness of the known; a being-positioned toward the beings of the world that *disposes over* their look. ἐπιστήμη is a ἕξις of ἀληθεύειν (b31), in which the look of beings is kept. Such a being can never be concealed, never in concealment finally turn out otherwise, so that the knowing would no longer be knowing; hence it *can* pass out of the actual looking and yet be known. Knowing need not be constantly enacted; it is a being-alongside-beings, a disposing-over-their-uncoveredness even when I do not stand before them. Because the being always is, my cognition is secured and I need not keep returning to it. Of what-can-be-otherwise I have no knowing — which is why one says the historical, strictly, cannot be known.

Now the striking result: this being is determined *in its being* by a moment of *time*. The always-enduring characterizes it. The ὄντα are ἀίδια (b23), and ἀίδιον shares its stem with ἀεί and αἰών. καὶ γὰρ τὸ ἀεὶ συνεχές (*Phys.* Θ, 6; 259a16): the always is what holds together with itself, is never interrupted. αἰών means lifetime understood as full being-present — τὸν ἅπαντα αἰῶνα (*De Caelo* A, 9; 279b22); every living thing has its αἰών, its allotted span of being-present, the measure of presence it disposes over, while in a wider sense αἰών means the duration of the world as such, which for Aristotle is eternal, ungenerated, imperishable. The heaven (οὐρανός) allots the living thing its αἰών, its being-present. Further, the ἀίδια are πρότερα τῇ οὐσίᾳ than the perishable (*Met.* Θ, 8; 1050b7) — earlier *as regards presence* than what has once come to be and so was not present; hence they are ἀρχή, the beginning for all other beings. They are what properly *is* — for the Greeks being means being-present, being-in-the-now — so what is always in the now is the being that properly is and the origin of the rest. Every determination of a being, if it is to be, gets led back to and understood from an always-being.

Heidegger then handles an apparent contradiction sharpening this into a thesis about time. Aristotle also holds that the always-being is *not in time*: τὰ ἀεὶ ὄντα, ᾗ ἀεὶ ὄντα, οὐκ ἔστιν ἐν χρόνῳ (*Phys.* Δ, 12; 221b3), it suffers nothing from time, is unchangeable — yet he calls the heaven eternal, αἰών. The resolution is Aristotle's own precise sense of "being-in-time": to be in time is τὸ μετρεῖσθαι τὸ εἶναι ὑπὸ τοῦ χρόνου (b5), to be measured, as regards one's being, *by* time. Something is measured by time insofar as its nows are determined — now and now in succession. But what always is, is constantly in the now, and its nows are countless, limitless, ἄπειρον; since the infinite nows of the ἀίδιον are not measurable, the always-being is *not in time*. It is therefore *not* "supra-temporal" in our sense: what is not "in time" is, for Aristotle, still *temporal*, i.e. determined *out of* time — the ἀίδιον not-in-time being determined precisely by the ἄπειρον of its nows. The point to hold fast, and it governs the entire course: beings are interpreted *in their being out of time*. The object of ἐπιστήμη is the ἀεὶ ὄν — the first determination of the ἐπιστητόν, and the seed of *Being and Time*'s thesis that the traditional sense of being is unadmittedly temporal (presence).

##### b) The place of the ἀρχή in ἐπιστήμη (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 3; *An. Post.* I, 1). The teachability of ἐπιστήμη. ἀπόδειξις and ἐπαγωγή. The presupposition of the ἀρχή

The second determination of the ἐπιστητόν (given fully at VI, 6): it is an ἀποδεικτόν, something demonstrable. Here at VI, 3 this appears as: ἐπιστήμη is διδακτή, teachable, and the ἐπιστητόν is μαθητόν, learnable (1139b25). To knowing belongs its being *teachable* — imparted and communicated — a constitutive determination it shares with τέχνη; and scientific knowing is above all ἐπιστήμη μαθηματική, with mathematics teachable in an exceptional sense. This teachability shows what knowing turns on: a being-positioned toward beings that disposes over their uncoveredness *without constantly being alongside*, hence communicable without any actual uncovering having to take place.

Teachable and learnable too are the λόγοι. Aristotle thinks first of natural speech, where there are two ways. The rhetors before a court or assembly appeal to the common understanding everyone has; they bring about *conviction*, not scientific proof, διὰ παραδειγμάτων — by adducing a striking example, δεικνύντες τὸ καθόλου διὰ τὸ δῆλον εἶναι τὸ καθ' ἕκαστον (a8): they show the universal, meant to bind the others, by way of the evident particular case. This path is ἐπαγωγή (a6), a plain leading-toward, not real arguing. Alternatively one takes the binding and universal λαμβάνοντες ὡς παρὰ ξυνιέντων (a7), from the natural understanding, from what one knows and agrees on, reckoning with certain knowledge the hearers dispose over and that is not further discussed, and from it proves something by συλλογισμός (a5). συλλογισμός and ἐπαγωγή are the two ways of imparting knowledge; inference ἐκ προγιγνωσκομένων, from what is known beforehand, is the mode of communication proper to ἐπιστήμη. One can thus impart a science to someone who has not himself seen all the facts, if only he disposes over certain presuppositions — a μάθησις purest in mathematics, whose axioms are such προγιγνωσκόμενα: the mathematician does not discuss the axioms but *works with* them. Even modern axiomatics, Heidegger notes, is pursued *mathematically* — trying to prove the axioms by deduction and relation-theory, a path grounded in the axioms themselves, so that the axioms are not thereby clarified. Clarifying what is already familiar beforehand is rather the business of *ἐπαγωγή*, the mode of clarification of plain looking. ἐπαγωγή is therefore what discloses the ἀρχή; it is the more original, not ἐπιστήμη — it leads originally *to* the καθόλου, whereas ἐπιστήμη and συλλογισμός proceed *from* the καθόλου (1139b29).

Every case therefore needs ἐπαγωγή, whether it stops there or a real proof follows. Every ἐπιστήμη is διδασκαλία and so *always presupposes what it cannot as ἐπιστήμη itself clarify*; it is ἀπόδειξις, exhibiting *from* something already familiar, always drawing on an ἐπαγωγή it does not itself properly enact, being beforehand sufficiently familiar with the "from-where." What ἐπιστήμη presupposes is precisely the ἀρχή — and this it does not properly disclose itself. Because ἐπιστήμη cannot exhibit what lies before it, its ἀληθεύειν shows a *defect*: it does not suffice to exhibit beings as such, since it does not disclose the ἀρχή. Hence ἐπιστήμη is *not* the βελτίστη ἕξις of ἀληθεύειν; the highest possibility within the ἐπιστημονικόν is rather σοφία. Still, genuine knowing is always more than mere acquaintance with results: whoever merely disposes over the συμπεράσματα, the conclusions, and then talks on, has no knowing — he has the ἐπιστήμη only κατὰ συμβεβηκός (*An. Post.* I, 2; 71b10), from outside; it falls to him, who is and remains properly ignorant. To knowing itself belongs disposing over the συλλογισμός, being able to run through the grounding-nexus in which a conclusion hangs. ἐπιστήμη is thus an ἀληθεύειν that does *not* properly make its beings — the always-being — available, since these remain concealed from it precisely in their ἀρχαί.

The double question announced at the outset now shows its cutting edge: the *second* question — whether a mode discloses the ἀρχή — is always the criterion of whether an ἀληθεύειν is *genuine*. The same test governs τέχνη, an ἀληθεύειν within the λογιστικόν. And as ἐπιστήμη, the *nearest* ἀληθεύειν within the ἐπιστημονικόν, proved not the genuine one, so τέχνη, the more *familiar* ἀληθεύειν within the λογιστικόν, will prove un-genuine. τέχνη discloses beings that can be otherwise (VI, 4; 1140a1) — as does φρόνησις; within the ἐνδεχόμενον there is a difference, between a ποιητόν and a πρακτόν, the theme of a producing (ποίησις) or of an acting (πρᾶξις).

##### c) πρᾶξις and ποίησις as the nearest modes of enacting ἀληθεύειν. ἐπιστήμη as a self-standing "πρᾶξις" of ἀληθεύειν

A phenomenon lying more or less explicitly in *all* modes of ἀληθεύειν has not yet been properly seen in ἐπιστήμη. Insofar as ἐπιστήμη is *enacted as a task*, it is itself a πρᾶξις — though one aimed not, like producing, at some result, but solely at winning beings *as ἀληθές*. In knowing the ἀληθές lie the task and goal of ἐπιστήμη. But at first and for the most part, knowing stands *in the service of* fabrication; ἀληθεύειν co-constitutes the enactment of a ποίησις or πρᾶξις. For ἀληθεύειν is not the sole determination of the ψυχή — only one, though constitutive, possibility of a being with the character of life (ψυχή): the being distinguished by *speaking*. Aristotle characterizes the two basic possibilities of the soul quite generally as κρίνειν and κινεῖν. Already the αἴσθησις of the animal has the character of κρίσις: in natural perceiving too something is set off against something. The second determination is κινεῖν, "bestirring-oneself"; to it corresponds the higher determination of the human's being — πρᾶξις, κινεῖν in the sense of κρίνειν, of speaking discrimination. The ζωή of the human is πρακτικὴ μετὰ λόγου, characterized by πρᾶξις καὶ ἀλήθεια (VI, 2; 1139a18) — by acting and by the uncoveredness of Dasein itself and of the beings the acting bears on. These two can, with a view to sight and its possibilities, be formulated as αἴσθησις, νοῦς, ὄρεξις: the κύρια, the governing possibilities of every human comportment, τρία δ' ἐστὶν ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ τὰ κύρια πράξεως καὶ ἀληθείας, αἴσθησις νοῦς ὄρεξις (a17).

Every comportment of Dasein is thus determined as πρᾶξις καὶ ἀλήθεια. In ἐπιστήμη the character of πρᾶξις did not step forth expressly because knowing in science is *self-standing* and as such already *is itself* the πρᾶξις and ὄρεξις. In τέχνη, by contrast, the ἀληθεύειν is that of a ποίησις: τέχνη is a διάνοια ποιητική (a27), a thinking-through of beings that contributes to producing, to *how* something is to be made. Hence in τέχνη as ποίησις, and in every πρᾶξις, the ἀληθεύειν is a λέγειν that speaks ὁμολόγως ἔχον τῇ ὀρέξει (a30), exactly as the ὄρεξις wills — no theoretical speculation about beings but a speaking-out that gives the right directive for correctly carrying through what is to be produced. So in τέχνη and φρόνησις the ἀληθεύειν is oriented to ποίησις and πρᾶξις. The distinction between a knowing that is *for its own sake* and a knowing that serves production is the axis on which the ranking of all five modes will turn, and it is already the distinction that will let Heidegger read σοφία as the mode that discloses for no further use.

#### §7. The analysis of τέχνη (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 4)

##### a) The object of τέχνη: the being-that-will-be (ἐσόμενον)

As with ἐπιστήμη, the first task is to determine the beings τέχνη bears on. In τέχνη the know-how is directed to the ποιητόν, to what is first to be produced and so is *not yet*. Therein lies that it can also be otherwise: what is not yet is not always — ἔστιν δὲ τέχνη πᾶσα περὶ γένεσιν (1140a10). All know-how, as the guidance of a producing, moves in the circuit of a being that is *in becoming*, underway to its being; and the τεχνάζειν is indeed a regarding (θεωρεῖν) — but not one that is absorbed in regarding, but one oriented to the ὅπως, to *that* something come about thus and so, i.e. be rightly carried out. The dealing that τέχνη guides is always a *making-ready-for*; its θεωρεῖν is no speculation but guides dealing under the orientation toward a for-this and to-this. So the being of τέχνη is an ἐσόμενον, one that only *will* be.

##### b) The place of the ἀρχή in τέχνη (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 4; *Met.* VII, 7). The twofold relation of τέχνη to its ἀρχή. εἶδος and ἔργον. The παρά-character of the ἔργον

The second question: how far can τέχνη itself disclose the ἀρχή of the being it bears on? For τέχνη the ἀρχή is ἐν τῷ ποιοῦντι (a13) — the point from which fabrication sets to work lies "in the producer himself." Before all producing, the *for-which*, the ποιητόν, must be considered: the ποιητόν is there beforehand for the producer, since through τεχνάζειν he must have made clear to himself how the work should look. The εἶδος of what is to be produced — say the building-plan — is fixed *before* the producing, and from this plan the builder proceeds to execution. So the ἀρχή of the being of τέχνη, the εἶδος, is in the soul, in the producer — ἀλλὰ μὴ ἐν τῷ ποιουμένῳ (a13), *not* in what is to be produced, the ἔργον. This becomes clearest by contrast with beings that are also produced but produce *themselves*, the φύσει ὄντα: these have the ἀρχή in themselves, ἐν αὑτοῖς γὰρ ἔχουσι ταῦτα τὴν ἀρχήν (a15). In τέχνη, by contrast, the ἔργον lies *παρά*, "beside" the handling: precisely *as* finished work it is no longer object of the ποίησις — the shoe's being-finished means precisely that the cobbler *hands it over*. Since the τέλος co-constitutes the ἀρχή, in τέχνη the ἀρχή is, so to speak, *not at its disposal*: it prefigures that τέχνη is an *un-genuine* ἀληθεύειν.

The object of τέχνη is the ποιητόν, the ἔργον that springs out of a producing — and this ἔργον is an ἕνεκά τινος (VI, 2; 1139b1), "for the sake of something," with a reference to something else; it is οὐ τέλος ἁπλῶς (b2), no end simply, but a πρός τι καί τινος (b2), "to something, for someone" — the shoe made for wearing, for another. The ἔργον of ποίησις is thus a produced-thing *for further use* by the human. τέχνη therefore has the ἔργον as object of ἀληθεύειν only so long as it is *not yet finished*; once finished, the work falls out of τέχνη's domain and becomes object of the relevant *use* — which Aristotle expresses by saying the ἔργον is "παρά" (*Nic. Eth.* I, 1; 1094a4). τέχνη bears on a being *insofar as it is in becoming*: ἔστιν δὲ τέχνη πᾶσα περὶ γένεσιν.

Within the being determined by becoming Aristotle distinguishes three possibilities (*Met.* VII, 7; 1032a12): what becomes φύσει (able to produce itself), what becomes by τέχνη, and what happens ἀπὸ ταὐτομάτου, by chance — under the chance Aristotle thinks above all of malformations and the like, what is properly *against* nature yet in a sense also comes about of itself. The modes of becoming that are not nature's he calls ποιήσεις (a26); by such ποίησις comes about ὅσων τὸ εἶδος ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ (b1), all that whose look is in the soul. This must be examined more closely to see how τέχνη in a certain way *has* the ἀρχή and in a certain way does *not*. In medical τέχνη health, in building the house, is the εἶδος ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ. When a house is to be built, the basic enactment of the deliberating has the structure: since the house is to look thus and so, it is necessary that such-and-such be present for it. In this principled deliberation there is ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ an ἀληθεύειν — here (b6) *νοεῖν* — an ἀποφαίνεσθαι, a letting-see of what is to be produced; and what is here uncovered and present in the soul is the εἶδος of the house, its look, its "face" as it shall one day stand there, which makes up its proper *presence*. This is anticipated ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ in a προαίρεσις, since the house to be produced is not yet there. The phrase τὸ εἶδος ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ means this anticipation of the εἶδος in the soul — in good German, *the look is made present* (*vergegenwärtigt*): the house that shall one day become present is beforehand made present *as it is to look*. This making-present is an uncovering of the εἶδος ἄνευ ὕλης (b12), without matter. The wood is not yet there. In a definite way the ὕλη is of course also there in the deliberating — one deliberates precisely about the material too — but "ἄνευ ὕλης" is to be understood *ontologically*: the ὕλη is not present in the proper sense in τέχνη. It is properly there only as the *out-of-which* of the finished house's subsisting in its being-finished, making up the finished house's proper *presence*. It is τὸ ἔσχατον καθ' αὑτό, what is not first to be produced but is already available *for*, and available in such a way that it is what properly brings the ποιούμενον into presence — ἐνυπάρχει γὰρ καὶ γίγνεται αὕτη (b32), for the ὕλη is what "is co-present within" and what "becomes."

The εἶδος as εἶδος ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ is the *anticipated presence* of the house; and insofar as the human makes it present, he carries through, in constant regard *to* this εἶδος, the whole elaboration of the plan. τὸ δὴ ποιοῦν καὶ ὅθεν ἄρχεται ἡ κίνησις τοῦ ὑγιαίνειν, ἐὰν μὲν ἀπὸ τέχνης, τὸ εἶδός ἐστι τὸ ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ (b21): the properly producing, the whence the movement begins, is the εἶδος in the soul. The εἶδος is thus the ἀρχή, from which the κίνησις begins — first the κίνησις of νόησις, the deliberation, then of ποίησις, the acting that springs from it. Insofar as the εἶδος is thus the ἀρχή of the whole movement of producing and is ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ, the ἀρχή of the ποιητόν is ἐν τῷ ποιοῦντι (VI, 4; 1140a13), i.e. is the affair of τέχνη itself. But the ποιούμενον, the finished house, is no longer object of τέχνη: as finished it falls out. And the τέλος, taken in its ontological character, is πέρας — πέρας λέγεται τὸ τέλος ἑκάστου (*Met.* V, 17; 1022a4); the ἀρχή too is in a certain way πέρας, ἡ μὲν ἀρχὴ πέρας τι (a12). Since τέλος has the same ontological character as ἀρχή — namely πέρας — yet the τέλος is precisely *not* kept safe in τέχνη, τέχνη stands in exactly the same relation to its being as ἐπιστήμη. And because τέχνη no longer reaches the ἔργον, which falls out of it, it stands in a certain way *like τύχη*, chance: τρόπον τινὰ περὶ τὰ αὐτά ἐστιν ἡ τύχη καὶ ἡ τέχνη (VI, 4; 1140a18). Chance's mark is that it does not have in hand what springs out — and so with τέχνη: however finely elaborated, it does not dispose with absolute certainty over the work's succeeding. τέχνη ultimately does not have the ἔργον in hand — a fundamental *defect* of its ἀληθεύειν.

##### c) The εἶδος as ἀρχή of the κίνησις of τέχνη as a whole (*Met.* VII, 7). νόησις and ποίησις. τέχνη as the ground of the interpretation of being through the εἶδος

The εἶδος comes into play as ἀρχή in τέχνη: the εἶδος ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ is the ἀρχή of the κίνησις that is first νόησις and then ποίησις. Aristotle exhibits the connection of νόησις and ποίησις in *Metaphysics* VII, 7, at health and building. He says: ἡ δὲ ὑγίεια ὁ ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ λόγος (1032b5) — health is the λόγος in the soul, where λόγος means λεγόμενον, the spoken. Correspondingly ἡ δὲ τέχνη λόγος τοῦ ἔργου ὁ ἄνευ ὕλης ἐστίν (*De Part. An.* A, 1; 640a31), where λόγος means λέγειν, the discussing making-present. But the λόγος *qua* λεγόμενον is the εἶδος. Heidegger flags this as an echo of Platonic diction: the εἶδος is nothing but the *Idea*. Hence Aristotle can say in shortened form ἡ οἰκοδομικὴ τὸ εἶδος τῆς οἰκίας (VII, 7; 1032b13): the *ability to build* is the *look* of the house. τέχνη is λόγος *qua* λέγειν of the λεγόμενον, i.e. of the εἶδος; building keeps uncovered and safe the look of the house. (Heidegger notes this is decisive too for νόησις νοήσεως in *Met.* XII, 9–10, where the proper being is νοῦς as the θειότατον, to which life and duration belong.)

Following the movement from the εἶδος ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ: γίγνεται δὴ τὸ ὑγιὲς νοήσαντος οὕτως· ἐπειδὴ τοδὶ ὑγίεια, ἀνάγκη, εἰ ὑγιὲς ἔσται, τοδὶ ὑπάρξαι […] καὶ οὕτως ἀεὶ νοεῖ, ἕως ἂν ἀγάγῃ εἰς τοῦτο ὃ αὐτὸς δύναται ἔσχατον ποιεῖν (1032b6). The healthy comes about by uncovering thus: since health is such-and-such, it is necessary, if there is to be health, that such-and-such be present for it — and one keeps uncovering so, until one has led back to what one can *oneself* produce as the *last thing*. This ἔσχατον is also τὸ τελευταῖον τῆς νοήσεως (b17), the last of the circumspective uncovering: in technical circumspection is set forth what stands there as the *outermost-first* for the directing grasping. Circumspection runs through no theoretical steps but sets forth *that* at which the accomplishing, the bringing-into-being, ποιεῖν, sets on. The νόησις here is a τεχνάζειν (VI, 4; 1140a11), the outward-facing uncovering of the ὑπάρχοντα.

The philosophical yield Heidegger is driving toward: τέχνη — the pre-theoretical world of production and use — is the *ground* on which the Greeks read off the very sense of being *as εἶδος*, i.e. as the anticipated *look* under which a thing stands present. Being = presence (*Anwesenheit*), and presence gets its paradigm from the produced thing standing finished and available. This is why the Platonic *Idea* is not a free-floating abstraction but the ontological residue of the producer's foreseen εἶδος; and why the whole *Sophist*'s question about the being of beings will keep circling back to production, look, and use. The two "defects" established here — that neither ἐπιστήμη nor τέχνη discloses its own ἀρχή, and that τέχνη does not have its ἔργον in hand — are the negative results that clear the way for φρόνησις and σοφία as the genuine modes of ἀληθεύειν.

The movement completes: ἡ ἀπὸ τούτου κίνησις ποίησις (1032b10), the movement issuing from this learning of the νόησις is the ποίησις — the properly bringing-forth directing, while νόησις has the character of *illumination*; the two belong together and make up the full movedness of dealing. συμβαίνει τρόπον τινὰ τὴν ὑγίειαν ἐξ ὑγιείας γίγνεσθαι (b11): health comes about, in a way, *out of* health — out of the εἶδος of health ἐν τῇ ψυχῇ. The εἶδος is the ἀρχή of the whole nexus of νόησις and ποίησις; hence ἡ οἰκοδομικὴ τὸ εἶδος τῆς οἰκίας.

On the ground of τέχνη, then, the being of the house is understood as being-made, as answering to a "look." *Presence-at-hand is genuinely and solely referred to the modes of becoming and producing*; all other determinations are κατὰ συμβεβηκός — and τὸ κατὰ συμβεβηκὸς οὐθὲν μέλει τῇ τέχνῃ (V, 15; 1138b2), τέχνη does not concern itself with the incidental. Correspondingly φύσις too is understood as a becoming-being, a bringing-oneself-from-oneself to shape and look. Heidegger names the stakes: this way of seeing has its ground in *Platonic* philosophy, the εἶδος being nothing but the name for the Platonic Idea. He warns against the standard habit of centering Plato's philosophy on the "theory of Ideas" and taking it as the guiding thread — for it may be that one understands an author precisely *not* through what he himself names most important, but through what he passes over in silence. Without pre-empting the discussion of the Ideas, he flags the orientation: the genesis, primary sense, and even the opacity of Plato's Ideas become intelligible only by holding fast to where the εἶδος first arises quite naturally, in which ἀληθεύειν it explicitly steps forth — namely τέχνη. From here it becomes intelligible why Plato says the Idea is the being that properly is. τέχνη was thus not treated idly: *in it the εἶδος first becomes present*.

Heidegger closes the survey of the two nearest modes: ἐπιστήμη and τέχνη, the basic possibilities of the ἐπιστημονικόν and the λογιστικόν, are *not* the highest — but one must not simply assume that the other two modes (σοφία, φρόνησις) must therefore be the proper possibility and cultivation, the ἀρετή, of each kind. The aim is less such systematics than concrete understanding of the phenomena themselves. Each ἀληθεύειν has the sense of setting Dasein *in order against its falling to the λεγόμενον*, so that in this being it cannot be deceived. Aristotle turns next to φρόνησις.

#### §8. The analysis of φρόνησις (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 5)

The analysis again first determines what φρόνησις bears on, then bounds it off against the two already analyzed: against ἐπιστήμη, φρόνησις comes out as δόξα; against τέχνη, as ἀρετή — the closed nexus of ch. 5.

##### a) The object of φρόνησις: Dasein itself. The determination of the τέλος of φρόνησις in contrast to the τέλος of τέχνη. Its relation to ἀληθεύειν: prior identity in φρόνησις, difference (παρά) in τέχνη

Aristotle begins from what natural Dasein means by φρόνησις, whom one calls a φρόνιμος: δοκεῖ δὴ φρονίμου εἶναι τὸ δύνασθαι καλῶς βουλεύσασθαι περὶ τὰ αὑτῷ ἀγαθὰ καὶ συμφέροντα, οὐ κατὰ μέρος […], ἀλλὰ ποῖα πρὸς τὸ εὖ ζῆν ὅλως (1140a25). A φρόνιμος is evidently one who can deliberate well and fittingly, the βουλευτικός — and specifically about what is good (constituting being-finished) and advantageous *for the deliberator himself*. So the object of φρόνησις is indeed something that can be otherwise, but it has from the outset a *reference to the deliberator himself*. τέχνη's deliberation bears only on what is contributory to producing something else, the ἔργon (e.g. the house); φρόνησις' deliberation bears on that ἔργον only insofar as it is contributory *for the deliberator himself*. The ἀληθεύειν of φρόνησις thus has in itself the direction of *reference back to the discloser*. And we call φρόνιμος not the one who deliberates rightly κατὰ μέρος, about particular advantages in some respect (health, bodily strength), but the one who deliberates ποῖα πρὸς τὸ εὖ ζῆν ὅλως — about what is advantageous for the right way of Dasein's being as such, as a whole. The βουλεύεσθαι of φρόνησις concerns *the being of Dasein itself*, the εὖ ζῆν, that Dasein be a right one. Hence φρόνησις has the reference πρὸς τέλος τι σπουδαῖον (1140a29), toward a τέλος that is a matter of *seriousness*, and specifically ὧν μὴ ἔστιν τέχνη (a30), with respect to beings that are *not* the theme of a producing. The τέλος of φρόνησις is therefore no *παρά* over against the being of the deliberating itself, as the ἔργον of τέχνη is; rather the object of the deliberating is ζωή itself, and the τέλος is of the *same being-character* as φρόνησις. τῆς μὲν γὰρ ποιήσεως ἕτερον τὸ τέλος, τῆς δὲ πράξεως οὐκ ἂν εἴη· ἔστιν γὰρ αὐτὴ ἡ εὐπραξία τέλος (1140b6): in ποίησις the τέλος is another; in πρᾶξις it is not — the εὐπραξία itself is the τέλος. In φρόνησις the πρακτόν is of the same being-character as the ἀληθεύειν itself, and here the τέλος is in fact uncovered and retained: it is the being of the deliberator himself. The φρόνιμος does not coincide with the τεχνίτης, whose ἀληθεύειν bears on *another* being.

Aristotle sharpens: βουλεύεται δ' οὐθεὶς περὶ τῶν ἀδυνάτων ἄλλως ἔχειν (1140a31) — no one deliberates about what cannot be otherwise (as with the τεχνίτης); but the βουλευτικός also does not deliberate οὐδὲ τῶν μὴ ἐνδεχομένων αὐτῷ πρᾶξαι (a32), about what he cannot himself carry out. In φρόνησις' deliberating, then, one looks *to oneself and one's own acting*, whereas τέχνη is a devising of what I need not myself will and be able to execute. Further, φρόνησις' deliberating is a certain *inferring*: if such-and-such is to happen, if I am to comport and be thus, then… — and the whence and the constant regard *to which* I deliberate, the οὗ ἕνεκα, is in each case *other*. So it is a talking-through, a λογίζεσθαι, but no ἀπόδειξις, no ἐπιστήμη; conversely, what is necessarily as it is cannot be a possible object of deliberation. Like τέχνη's, φρόνησις' deliberating bears on what can be otherwise and has a certain likeness to τέχνη's ("if I am to act thus, then such-and-such must happen" / "if such-and-such is to *become*, then…"). Yet φρόνησις differs from τέχνη, because in τέχνη the πρακτόν is a τέλος that is *παρά*. The τέλος of φρόνησις stands otherwise: φρόνησις is ἕξις ἀληθὴς μετὰ λόγου πρακτικὴ περὶ τὰ ἀνθρώπῳ ἀγαθά (1140b5), a being-positioned of human Dasein such that it *disposes over the transparency of itself*. Its τέλος is no πρός τι and no ἕνεκά τινος; it is the ἄνθρωπος himself — αὐτὴ ἡ εὐπραξία τέλος (b7); the right being of the human is τέλος, and this is ζωὴ πρακτικὴ μετὰ λόγου. The τέλος of φρόνησις is a τέλος ἁπλῶς and an οὗ ἕνεκα, a for-the-sake-of-which. And insofar as Dasein is uncovered *as* the οὗ ἕνεκα, what is *for* its sake and to be taken care of is itself prefigured; so with Dasein as the οὗ ἕνεκα the ἀρχή of φρόνησις' deliberating is seized at one stroke — αἱ μὲν γὰρ ἀρχαὶ τῶν πρακτῶν τὸ οὗ ἕνεκα τὰ πρακτά (1140b16). These ἀρχαί *are Dasein itself*, which finds itself, stands to itself thus or so. Dasein is the ἀρχή of φρόνησις' deliberating; and what φρόνησις deliberates is not that *at which* a πρᾶξις comes to an end — a *result* is not constitutive of the being of acting, but only the εὖ, the *how*. In φρόνησις the τέλος is the ἄνθρωπος himself; in ποίησις it is another, a worldly being over against Dasein; in πρᾶξις it is not.

##### b) φρόνησις as ἀ-ληθεύειν. ἡδονή and λύπη. σωφροσύνη. φρόνησις as struggle against the self-concealing tendency lying in Dasein. φρόνησις as un-self-standing ἀληθεύειν in the service of πρᾶξις

If the human himself is the object of φρόνησις' uncovering, then it must stand with the human such that he *is concealed to himself*, does not see himself — so that an ἀ-ληθεύειν is expressly needed to become transparent to oneself. And indeed: διαφθείρει καὶ διαστρέφει τὸ ἡδὺ καὶ τὸ λυπηρὸν τὴν ὑπόληψιν (1140b13). What gives pleasure and what drags the mood down can *destroy* or *derange* the ὑπόληψις. A mood can conceal the human from himself; he can come to care about incidental things, be besotted with himself, so that he does not properly see himself. Hence φρόνησις must be *rescued* again and again: circumspection toward oneself, insight into oneself, must be wrested from the danger of διαφθείρειν and διαστρέφειν. It is not at all self-evident that Dasein is uncovered *for itself* in the genuineness of its being; here too ἀλήθεια must be wrung out. So Aristotle, with Plato, invokes a telling etymology — σωφροσύνη σῴζει τὴν φρόνησιν (b11), *sound-mindedness* is what "saves" φρόνησις, preserves it against the possibility of concealment (cf. *Cratylus* 411e: σωφροσύνη as σωτηρία φρονήσεως). But only *certain* modes of ἀληθεύειν are endangered by ἡδονή and λύπη: οὐ γὰρ ἅπασαν ὑπόληψιν διαφθείρει […] ἀλλὰ τὰς περὶ τὸ πρακτόν (b13) — not every ὑπόληψις, but those bearing on the πρακτόν. Since ἡδονή and λύπη belong to the basic determination of the human, the human is *constantly in danger* of being concealed from himself by himself. φρόνησις is therefore nothing self-evident but a *task* to be seized in a προαίρεσις; in it the sense of ἀ-ληθεύειν — uncovering of something *concealed* — shows itself in an exceptional way. τῷ δὲ διεφθαρμένῳ δι' ἡδονὴν ἢ λύπην εὐθὺς οὐ φαίνεται ἡ ἀρχή (b17): when Dasein is corrupted by pleasure or pain, the ἀρχή *does not show itself* — the right οὗ ἕνεκα no longer appears, is concealed, and must be uncovered through λόγος. So φρόνησις, once enacted, stands in a *constant struggle* against the concealing tendency lying in Dasein itself: ἔστι γὰρ ἡ κακία φθαρτικὴ ἀρχῆς (b19), the bad condition is what destroys the ἀρχή, lets the right οὗ ἕνεκα not become visible. The danger and resistance for φρόνησις lie in *Dasein itself*. Aristotle sums up: ὥστ' ἀνάγκη τὴν φρόνησιν ἕξιν εἶναι μετὰ λόγου ἀληθῆ περὶ τὰ ἀνθρώπινα ἀγαθὰ πρακτικήν (b20) — φρόνησις is a ἕξις of ἀληθεύειν, a being-positioned of human Dasein in which one disposes over the transparency of oneself (its theme being the ἀνθρώπινα ἀγαθά), a ἕξις that is πρακτική, moving *within* acting. Hence it is εὖ insofar as it comports ὁμολόγως to the ὄρεξις, to the πρᾶξις, the deliberating measuring itself to the for-the-sake-of-which of the acting. φρόνησις is thus itself an ἀληθεύειν, but *not a self-standing one* — an ἀληθεύειν in the service of πρᾶξις, one that makes an action transparent in itself. Insofar as an action's transparency is constitutive *for* it, φρόνησις is co-constitutive of the proper enactment of the acting; it is ἀληθεύειν as the *guidance of the action*.

Heidegger's stakes here are large and mostly unstated: φρόνησις is the mode of disclosure whose "object" is the discloser's own existence in its situation, and whose truth must be won against a *self-concealing tendency native to Dasein*. This is, in nuce, the structure that *Being and Time* will call conscience (*Gewissen*) and the falling (*Verfallen*) it discloses Dasein against. The point that a result is not constitutive of acting — only the εὖ, the *how* — is the ethical correlate of refusing the production-model: πρᾶξις is not measured by an ἔργον left standing παρά, but is its own τέλος.

##### c) The delimitation of φρόνησις against τέχνη and ἐπιστήμη. φρόνησις as ἀρετή. φρόνησις as "un-forgettable" conscience. σοφία as ἀρετή of τέχνη

The delimitation is drawn first against τέχνη.

Since φrόνησις, exactly like τέχνη, is directed to a being that can be otherwise, but τέχνη does *not* have the ἔργον while φρόνησις does, one might suppose φρόνησις is the *ἀρετή of τέχνη*. The being-character of ἀρετή is τελείωσις: it makes up the being-finished of something, brings to completion what has the *possibility* of it, i.e. can also be *without* it. The question is whether φρόνησις can be the τελείωσις of τέχνη. ἀλλὰ μὴν τέχνης μέν ἐστιν ἀρετή, φρονήσεως δ' οὐκ ἔστιν (1140b21): for τέχνη there is an ἀρετή, a possible τελείωσις; for φρόνησις there is *none*. How is an ἀρετή possible for τέχνη? In a deliberating know-how there are degrees of cultivation; τέχνη admits of *more and less*, and to it belongs *trying out*. One understands in it whether it works or whether it goes another way; τέχνη proceeds the more surely for having risked a failed attempt — security forms precisely *on the path of erring*. Whoever does not stiffen into a fixed "technique," a set routine, but keeps trying anew and *breaks* the fixed procedure, brings himself into the right possibility of know-how and disposes better over the uncovering proper to τέχνη: καὶ ἐν μὲν τέχνῃ ὁ ἑκὼν ἁμαρτάνων αἱρετώτερος (b22). *Being able to err* is an advantage belonging to τέχνη itself, which is τελειοτέρα precisely on the ground of this possibility; the possibility of being-able-to-err is *constitutive* of τέχνη's cultivation.

With φρόνησις, whose theme is Dasein's own being, it is otherwise: every erring is a *self*-erring, and self-erring is no higher possibility, no τελείωσις of φρόνησις, but downright *ruin*. Beside the possibility of erring, φρόνησις has only the genuine possibility of *hitting the mark*; it is not oriented to trying-out — I cannot experiment with myself in ethical acting. φρόνησις' deliberating stands under the *either–or*; by its sense it is στοχαστική, having in itself a fixed orientation, going toward the goal, the μεσότης. There is no more-or-less, no both-and as in τέχνη, but only the *seriousness* of the determinate decision, hitting or missing. Insofar as φρόνησις is στοχαστική, it has *no* possibility of being more complete; it has no ἀρετή but *is in itself ἀρετή*. So the mode of enacting ἀληθεύειν is other in φρόνησις than in τέχνη, though both bear on beings that can be otherwise. δῆλον οὖν ὅτι ἀρετή τίς ἐστιν καὶ οὐ τέχνη (b24): φρόνησις is an ἀρετή but not a τέχνη. And because φρόνησις directs itself to ἀρχή *and* τέλος at once and keeps both, it is the βελτίστη ἕξις of ἀληθεύειν within the being that can be otherwise.

How does φρόνησις stand to ἐπιστήμη? Since φρόνησις is not the ἀρετή of τέχνη, can it be the ἀρετή of ἐπιστήμη, the ἐπιστημονικόν? It seems φρόνησις might be the ἀρετή of ἐπιστήμη in a *preliminary stage* — for within knowing there is indeed a mode of uncovering that, like φρόνησις, bears on what can be otherwise: δόξα. ἥ τε γὰρ δόξα περὶ τὸ ἐνδεχόμενον ἄλλως ἔχειν καὶ ἡ φρόνησις (b27). δόξα has, in a sense, mere *acquaintance*-character; it is a "thematic" opinion, a view that has nothing to say for a determinate acting. About everyday things that happen and change, natural Dasein has definite views and opinions; and one might think that for δόξα — no genuine mode of ἀληθεύειν — φρόνησις is in fact the ἀρετή. Aristotle raises this (not for completeness: such views had actually arisen) but *cuts it off*: ἀλλὰ μὴν οὐδ' ἕξις μετὰ λόγου μόνον (b28) — φρόνησις is no self-standing ἕξις of ἀληθεύειν existing *merely for the sake of uncovering*; it is a ἕξις that is πρακτική, and because that belongs to its structure it cannot from the outset count as the τελείωσις of δόξα, which aims solely at *having-a-view*.

Then a decisive further mark: the ἀληθεύειν of δόξα, μάθησις, ἐπιστήμη has a peculiar character of *falling* — what I have experienced, noted, learned, I can *forget*; this possibility of ἀληθεύειν stands under λήθη (whose stem is in λανθάνειν), can sink back into concealment. *Being-able-to-be-forgotten* is a specific possibility of the ἀληθεύειν that has the character of θεωρεῖν, since the ἕξις μετὰ λόγου is an ἀληθεύειν into which Dasein *expressly brings itself*. With φρόνησις it stands otherwise: I can experience, note, learn what is *already* experienced, noted, learned, whereas φρόνησις is *each time new*. So of φρόνησις there is no λήθη: σημεῖον δ' ὅτι λήθη τῆς μὲν τοιαύτης ἕξεώς ἐστιν, φρονήσεως δ' οὐκ ἔστιν (b28). φρόνησις has *not* the falling-possibility of forgetting. Aristotle's explication here is very terse, but Heidegger judges it no over-reading to say Aristotle has here *struck upon the phenomenon of conscience*: φρόνησις is nothing other than *conscience set in motion*, making an action transparent. One cannot *forget* conscience — though one can, through ἡδονή and λύπη, through passions, *distort* what conscience uncovers and render it ineffective. Conscience announces itself again and again. Because φρόνησις has no possibility of λήθη, it is no mode of ἀληθεύειν addressable as theoretical knowing, and so cannot come into question as the ἀρετή of ἐπιστήμη or τέχνη.

The *frappant* result, which sets up §9: Aristotle calls *σοφία* the ἀρετή of τέχνη (VI, 7; 1141a12). The highest mode of ἀληθεύειν — philosophical regarding, for Aristotle the human's highest way of existing — is at once the τελείωσις of τέχνη. This must strike one the more strangely since τέχνη has for its theme the being that *can be otherwise*, while σοφία has, in the exceptional sense, the *always-being*. (Heidegger, following the editor's note, reads the link through the ἀρχὴ τῆς κινήσεως and the doctrine of the four causes: σοφία completes τέχνη as the knowing of first origins toward which τέχνη's foreseeing of the εἶδος already tends.)

#### §9. The analysis of σοφία (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 6–7)

##### a) The dia-noetic relation of ἐπιστήμη, φρόνησις, and σοφία to the ἀρχαί (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 6)

To understand σοφία, Heidegger first re-presents the guiding connection under which the whole interpretation runs: the modes of ἀληθεύειν are analyzed with a view to the ἀρχαί — their uncovering and safekeeping. ἐπιστήμη is *grounded in* the ἀρχαί, using them in its axioms, the self-evident propositions from which it infers; in it ἀρχή and τέλος, εἶδος and ὕλη of beings are unexpressly co-meant, but it does not make the ἀρχαί *thematic* — it only carries through its deliberation *along the guiding thread* of the εἶδος. τέχνη takes over only the ἀρχή, the εἶδος; the τέλος is not even co-meant in it; and it too does not thematize the εἶδος but proceeds along its thread, which gives it the direction for its λογίζεσθαι. In φρόνησις the οὗ ἕνεκα — and with it both ἀρχή and τέλος, the εὐπραξία — is given (for the ἀρχή *is* the τέλος itself), but again *not* in thematic regard; ἀρχή and τέλος do not come to regard *as* ἀρχή and τέλος. φρόνησις is no speculation about the ἀρχή and τέλος of acting as such: it is no ethics and no science, no ἕξις μετὰ λόγου μόνον (VI, 5; 1140b28), but in its proper sense what it can be only *as* the sight of a concrete action and decision. Yet even σοφία, which ultimately bears on the *last principles* of beings, is an ἀληθεύειν that does *not* exclusively and properly have the ἀρχαί for its theme. Rather ἀρχή-research is what it is only insofar as it seeks the principles *for the beings that stand under the principles*: τοῦ γὰρ σοφοῦ περὶ ἐνίων ἔχειν ἀπόδειξίν ἐστιν (VI, 6; 1141a2). So σοφία too is not the ἀληθεύειν in which the ἀρχή becomes thematic *as* ἀρχή.

Aristotle draws the conclusion by elimination: εἰ δὴ οἷς ἀληθεύομεν καὶ μηδέποτε διαψευδόμεθα περὶ τὰ μὴ ἐνδεχόμενα ἢ καὶ ἐνδεχόμενα ἄλλως ἔχειν, ἐπιστήμη καὶ φρόνησίς ἐστιν καὶ σοφία καὶ νοῦς, τούτων δὲ τῶν τριῶν μηδὲν ἐνδέχεται εἶναι (λέγω δὲ τρία φρόνησιν ἐπιστήμην σοφίαν), λείπεται νοῦν εἶναι τῶν ἀρχῶν (a3). If the modes by which we truly uncover beings and do *not* distort them are ἐπιστήμη, φρόνησις, σοφία, and νοῦς — and if the first three do not properly have the ἀρχαί for their theme — then it remains that *νοῦς* is the ἀληθεύειν that uncovers the ἀρχαί *as* ἀρχαί. τέχνη is here *left out*: the modes meant are those in which we are *secured and do not deceive ourselves*, whereas in τέχνη one *does* go astray — ἁμαρτάνειν is constitutive of it.

##### b) νοῦς as the ἀληθεύειν of the ἀρχαί (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 7). σοφία as νοῦς καὶ ἐπιστήμη

Aristotle says nothing more precise about νοῦς here. On the whole little about νοῦς is handed down from him: it is the phenomenon that gave him the *most difficulty* — one he perhaps clarified as far as was possible within Greek interpretation of being. VI, 6 gives a fore-indication by recalling that ἐπιστήμη, like φρόνησις and σοφία, is μετὰ λόγου (1140b33). Heidegger's fore-pointing thesis: the ἀληθεύειν of νοῦς is in fact *ἄνευ λόγου*, if one understands λόγος as κατάφασις and ἀπόφασις — pure νοῦς, if one wants to grasp it μετὰ λόγου, has a wholly peculiar λόγος that is *no* affirming and denying. And, pointing further ahead: νοῦς *as such* is not a possibility-of-being of the human. But since human Dasein *is* characterized by a "meaning" and apprehending, νοῦς is nonetheless *present* in human Dasein — the νοῦς Aristotle calls ὁ καλούμενος τῆς ψυχῆς νοῦς (*De An.* III, 4; 429a22), the "so-called" νοῦς, meaning the *un-genuine* νοῦς. This νοῦς in the human soul is not a νοεῖν, a sheer seeing, but a *διανοεῖν*, because the human soul is determined by λόγος: νοεῖν becomes, on the ground of λόγος, of the addressing of something *as* something, a διανοεῖν. Apart from νοῦς no mode of ἀληθεύειν remains that is in the proper sense an ἀληθεύειν *of the ἀρχαί*.

Because σοφία takes into regard *also* the concrete beings *for which* the ἀρχαί are ἀρχαί, while aiming *most of all* at the ἀρχαί, Aristotle can characterize it as *νοῦς καὶ ἐπιστήμη* — an ἀληθεύειν that on the one hand in a certain way takes over the ἀληθεύειν of νοῦς, and on the other has the science-character of ἐπιστήμη: ὥστε εἴη ἂν ἡ σοφία νοῦς καὶ ἐπιστήμη (1141a19).
