This composite definition is the key result: σοφία is not a *separate* faculty gazing at first principles in isolation, but the *unity* of the highest apprehending (νοῦς, which alone reaches the ἀρχαί as ἀρχαί) with the demonstrative knowing (ἐπιστήμη) of what follows from them. The stage is now set for the ranking that will occupy §§10–26: σοφία as the μάλιστα ἀληθεύειν, disclosing the always-being for its own sake, against φρόνησις as the highest disclosure of the being that can be otherwise — Dasein itself.

##### c) The further outline of the inquiry. φρόνησις and σοφία as the highest modes of ἀληθεύειν. The priority of σοφία. Its origin in the natural Greek understanding of Dasein. The phenomenology of Dasein as method. θεωρία: word-explanation and conceptual history

Even at the preliminary survey Heidegger extracts a threefold result without pre-empting the interpretation of the highest modes. First, the comparative interpretation made clear that ἀληθεύειν is ultimately placed under the respect of *the uncovering and safekeeping of the ἀρχαί*. Second, this very respect toward the ἀρχαί is what brings the discussion of the two highest modes, φρόνησις and σοφία, to *decision*. Third, therefore a real understanding of the modes is won only when one exhibits *how it comes about* that precisely the question of the ἀρχή furnishes the guiding thread for distinguishing and fixing them. In chs. 6–13 of *Nic. Eth.* VI the regard moves only within φρόνησις and σοφία, and the question is which has *simply the priority*.

Heidegger notes in passing that what Aristotle works out here on a materially difficult ground of phenomena, under the titles φρόνησις and σοφία, is *the same* that later stands in philosophy under the titles *practical and theoretical reason* — though the newer discussion of the "faculties of reason" has passed through and been permeated by manifold influences, so that its original ground is scarcely recognizable without the guidance of Aristotle's work; and conversely one *cannot* seek an understanding of φρόνησις and σοφία along the thread of the Kantian distinction of practical and theoretical reason. The warning cuts both ways: Aristotle illuminates the buried origin of the later distinction, but the later distinction *distorts* Aristotle if read back into him.

Anticipating the result: Aristotle shows (1) that σοφία is the *other* highest possibility of ἀληθεύειν, the second βελτίστη ἕξις alongside φρόνησις, and (2) that it has *even a priority* over φρόνησις, such that *this* ἀληθεύειν makes up an own and the proper possibility of Dasein — the βίος θεωρητικός, the existence of the scientific human. The result is the more astonishing since σοφία has for its theme the *always-being*, while φρόνησις aims precisely at the ἐνδεχόμενον ἄλλως ἔχειν — the being of human Dasein — and makes it transparent. To render intelligible *why* σοφία is nonetheless the highest possibility, σοφία must be (1) worked out against φρόνησις in its own structure and *pushed through* as the proper mode of ἀληθεύειν, the highest possibility-of-being of Dasein — whereby φρόνησις will step out more concretely; (2) shown *not* dogmatically imposed by Aristotle on the Greek Dasein of the day — Aristotle wills nothing unheard-of and new, but makes σοφία intelligible *out of the being of Greek Dasein itself*, thinking radically to the end what the natural understanding of life among the Greeks *strove toward*; (3) and by following this rootedness of σοφία's priority in Dasein, an understanding will come of why not φρόνησις but *σοφία* is the ἀρετή of τέχνη — σοφία as the ἀρετή of ἐπιστήμη, as ἀκριβεστάτη τῶν ἐπιστημῶν (VI, 7; 1141a17), the "strictest of all sciences."

Heidegger then states the *method* of the whole course, and it is worth marking because it governs everything that follows. The interpretation is grounded in a *phenomenology of Dasein* that cannot here be expressly presented; only a short methodical remark is given. Methodological speculation has little sense with no matter behind it, so the course will drive *concrete* interpretation first and defer the "method-question" — which is then more than the word says, being *itself* again inquiry into the matters (*Sachforschung*). Methodically, the interpretation is *not* about adducing hitherto unnoticed texts of Aristotle — he has stood available for 2000 years — but about the *rich hermeneutics already lying in the preparation for the interpretation* (which does not mean uncritical interpreting-around relative to other standpoints). The presupposition of the interpretation is that *Dasein is in the theme*; and if the interpretation "reads something into" Aristotle, its concern is to *regain and understand what is actually going on with him*. One thing is to aim at a philosophical system parcelled into disciplines; another is to make the matters sharper, the tendencies more explicit, and not to fall short of them. This is Heidegger's standing answer to the charge of "reading Heidegger into Aristotle": the violence is in the service of the matter Aristotle was after but did not fully thematize.

Finally the word θεωρία. σοφία has the mode of enactment of *pure knowing, pure seeing*, θεωρεῖν; it is the βίος θεωρητικός. The word θεωρεῖν was known before Aristotle, but Aristotle first used θεωρητικός. θεωρεῖν / θεωρία comes from θεωρός, compounded of θέα (look, sight — what *gives* something to see, how something takes itself out, akin in sense to εἶδος) and ὁράω (to see). θεωρός is then one who *looks at* something in its look, who looks at what there is to see: the θεωρός is the festival-goer, present as *spectator* at the great games and feasts — whence our "theater." "Seeing" is thus expressed *twice*. Heidegger sketches the word's later fortunes: shortly before Plotinus (2nd–3rd c.) θεωρία was etymologized so that θέο- was heard as θεῖον, θεός — θεωρεῖν as looking upon the *divine* (as in Alexander of Aphrodisias), a re-interpretation with some ground in certain Aristotelian passages but not hitting the word's proper sense. θεωρία was rendered in Latin as *speculatio*, pure regarding — so "speculative" means the same as "theoretical." The word then played a great role in theology, opposed to ἀλληγορία: θεωρία is the regard that sets out the historical facts *before* all allegory, as they are, becoming identical with ἱστορία, and finally with biblical theology and theology as such — so that the later *theologia speculativa* comes to stand precisely *against* exegetical theology, one of the peculiar accidents of conceptual history. Heidegger's point in the excursus is not antiquarian: the drift by which θεωρία becomes "looking at the divine" and then "speculation" is exactly the drift that detaches the highest ἀληθεύειν from its root in the Greeks' concrete seeing of the world — the root the course means to recover.

The chapter closes by naming where σοφία is treated (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 6–13; *Nic. Eth.* X, 6–10, with εὐδαιμονία; *Metaphysics* I, 1–2) and by re-affirming the program: to go with Aristotle the way of *Met.* I, 1–2 and see how in Greek Dasein itself a tendency toward σοφία and its preliminary stages is *laid out*.

### Second Chapter: The genesis of σοφία within the natural Dasein of the Greeks (αἴσθησις, ἐμπειρία, τέχνη, ἐπιστήμη, σοφία) (*Met.* I, 1–2)

#### §10. Introductory characterization of the inquiry. Its guiding thread: the self-expressing of Dasein itself. Its course: the five stages of εἰδέναι. Its goal: σοφία as μάλιστα ἀληθεύειν

Heidegger opens with a brisk dismissal of a chronological worry. *Metaphysics* I is supposed to be "early," yet it already mentions the *Ethics* (I, 1; 981b25), which is demonstrably late — which would refute the assumption, unless the citation is interpolated. He holds a *chronology* of the Aristotelian writings to be impossible, and pushes back on Werner Jaeger, who called *Met.* I a generous "improvisation" and wanted the "inconvenient" reference to the *Ethics* struck out on the ground that the doctrine of causes is clearly worked out at I, 3. Heidegger finds no real ground here — the less so as nothing materially different is said. Set the *confusion* in which the fundamental concepts τέχνη, ἐπιστήμη, σοφία, φρόνησις and their relations still lie *in Plato* beside the transparently superior presentation in *Met.* I, 1–2, and one may not speak of "improvisation," however generous. In Aristotle these concepts are clear from the start. *Met.* I, 1–2 is seen from the very same horizon as *Nic. Eth.* VI — only ἀληθεύειν is *not* here made explicit as such: at 981b5 Aristotle says, in its stead, λόγον ἔχειν, αἰτίας γνωρίζειν, and finally to know the ἀρχή as such. σοφία is thus to be determined as a mode of *λόγον ἔχειν* — which goes together with the determination of Dasein, of the human, as λόγον ἔχον.

What is the nearest and most originary phenomenon of natural Dasein addressable as a *preliminary stage* of σοφία? Such questions need a guiding thread, and Aristotle's is to fetch "information" *from Dasein itself* — from what Dasein, expressing itself, *means* when it uses the words σοφία, σοφός. He fixes two things in view. First, in the *everyday use* of these words there must betray itself the understanding natural Dasein has of them. Everyday Dasein has no sharp, scientific concept of them — as the nearest self-expression is, as nearest, indeterminate and never has univocally fixed concepts — but this does not conflict with Dasein's *going surely* in the understanding of itself. As in all everyday talk, Dasein moves here in the indeterminacy of the "more or less": one does not speak of σοφός but of *μᾶλλον* and *ἧττον* σοφός; one cannot define it, yet knows *this* is σοφώτερον than *that*. This *comparative* manner of speaking is characteristic of the everyday; the task is only to take it up and *hear out* of it what the μάλιστα of this μᾶλλον is — the method of *Met.* I, 1. Second, Aristotle orients himself by what Dasein says *directly and expressly* about the σοφός — the method of I, 2.

In the comparative manner various *stages of understanding* show themselves, present and known in natural Dasein itself. In the μᾶλλον and ἧττον lies the *tendency toward the μάλιστα*, and τέχνη is already μᾶλλον σοφός than ἐμπειρία; the τελείωσις points in the direction of ἐπιστήμη and θεωρεῖν. Aristotle thus shows that his interpretation of σοφία and θεωρεῖν is nothing but the *clear, self-clarifying explication of Dasein*. Heidegger lays out the five stages Aristotle lifts out: (1) the κοιναὶ αἰσθήσεις (981b14), the orientation about the world that everyone has; (2) ἐμπειρία, "experience," being-broken-in to a determinate handling; (3) τέχνη — the τεχνίτης or χειροτέχνης, the handworker who works *with the hand* along the thread of τέχνη's determinate orientation; (4) the ἀρχιτέκτων, the master-builder, who does not himself work and lay hands on the building but moves only in the know-how, whose main task lies in *designing the plan*, thinking through the εἶδος — yet still a ποίησις, since it aims at producing the house; (5) simple θεωρεῖν, the regarding exhibiting where χρῆσις, use, no longer matters. Each stage shows, over against the prior, a μᾶλλον of the σοφόν. (Heidegger notes he began the count with the human; Aristotle sets *before* it the life of the animals, which already have "a little of experience.")

The mode of enactment of σοφία is θεωρεῖν, a way of being of human Dasein that encloses in itself a διαγωγή: tarrying, leisure, doing-nothing. διαγωγή as doing-nothing means: to act nothing, accomplish nothing — every ποίησις falls away. Insofar as θεωρεῖν is determined by διαγωγή, it is no ποίησις but a *mere regarding, a tarrying alongside the object itself*. This brings to sharper expression what Plato often said — e.g. *Sophist* 254a: the philosopher, keeping himself always alongside, in looking upon being, and in this looking speaking about it, running through a comprehending of it. In Plato the *same* scientific bearing is alive that Aristotle then explicates — only not yet ontologically-theoretically grounded. Since delimiting σοφία against φρόνησις requires clarifying the *genesis* of σοφία's comportment, and since from this genesis the horizon is also won for understanding *why* σοφία is at once ἀρετή for τέχνη *and* ἐπιστήμη, it must show *why τέχνη*, which properly aims at a ποίησις, presents on the ground of its ownmost structure a preliminary stage to σοφία. Aristotle expressly: οὐθὲν ἄλλο σημαίνοντες τὴν σοφίαν ἢ ὅτι ἀρετὴ τέχνης ἐστίν (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 7; 1141a11) — genuine understanding, σοφία, is the completion, ἀρετή, τελείωσις, of know-how at an accomplishing. And: ὥστε δῆλον ὅτι ἡ ἀκριβεστάτη ἂν τῶν ἐπιστημῶν εἴη ἡ σοφία (a16), σοφία is the *strictest* of the sciences. Heidegger reads ἀ-κριβής as the same formation as ἀ-ληθής — α-privative plus κρυπτόν: *un-concealed* — so that ἀκρίβεια names a character of knowing *as uncovering*. Because σοφία is the strictest science, i.e. the one that most properly uncovers beings, Aristotle can say: δεῖ ἄρα τὸν σοφὸν μὴ μόνον τὰ ἐκ τῶν ἀρχῶν εἰδέναι, ἀλλὰ καὶ περὶ τὰς ἀρχὰς ἀληθεύειν. ὥστε εἴη ἂν ἡ σοφία νοῦς καὶ ἐπιστήμη, ὥσπερ κεφαλὴν ἔχουσα ἐπιστήμη τῶν τιμιωτάτων (a17). The σοφός must not only know beings *from* the ἀρχαί but also uncover *in the circuit of* the ἀρχαί, so that σοφία is νοῦς καὶ ἐπιστήμη and, as it were *having the head*, is the ἐπιστήμη of the τιμιώτατα, the most-honored objects of knowing — namely the always-being, ἀεί — and uncovers *in* this the ἀρχαί. Hence it "has the head," takes first place, is the μάλιστα ἀληθεύειν.

A methodical reminder closes the section, restating the course's ground: ἀληθεύειν is a way of being of Dasein, and specifically insofar as Dasein *comports itself* toward a being — toward world, or toward itself. The being that in the Greek sense properly *is* is the world, the ἀεί. And since being-alongside is determined *in its being from the whereat* (*Wobei*), the ways of being of Dasein are to be interpreted *from the comportment toward it*. This is the hinge on which the genesis-analysis turns: the stages of understanding will be read off the *beings* they disclose and the *comportment* (handling, using, regarding) in which they stand.

#### §11. The first three stages of εἰδέναι: αἴσθησις–ἐμπειρία–τέχνη (*Met.* I, 1)

##### a) αἴσθησις. The priority of ὁρᾶν. ἀκούειν as the condition of learning. μνήμη and φρόνησις

In σοφία everything turns on Dasein's *being-oriented*, on the uncovered- and visible-being of beings; and since σοφία is determined as pure θεωρεῖν, Aristotle begins the *Metaphysics* from *this* Dasein: πάντες ἄνθρωποι τοῦ εἰδέναι ὀρέγονται φύσει (980a1), all humans by nature *strive to see*. To Dasein belongs "seeing," apprehending in the widest sense — and more: to it belongs the *ὄρεξις*, the being-out-for seeing, for being-acquainted-with. σημεῖον δ' ἡ τῶν αἰσθήσεων ἀγάπησις (a1): the sign is the *love* of perceiving. In connection with the εἰδέναι Dasein strives after, Aristotle lifts out the priority of *one* αἴσθησις over all others, namely *seeing*: we give ὁρᾶν precedence over all the senses. The guiding viewpoint is the possibility of experiencing something about the world *through* a sense, i.e. the *extent* to which the beings of the world are uncovered by it: αἴτιον δ' ὅτι μάλιστα ποιεῖ γνωρίζειν ἡμᾶς αὕτη τῶν αἰσθήσεων καὶ πολλὰς δηλοῖ διαφοράς (a26) — δηλοῦν meaning here *to let see, make manifest*. Seeing is distinguished by "letting many differences be seen": it gives the greatest possibility of *distinguishing* things in their manifoldness and orienting oneself among them.

This priority of ὁρᾶν is the more remarkable since at 980b23 Aristotle stresses that *ἀκούειν, hearing, is the highest αἴσθησις*. There is no contradiction. *Hearing is the basic constitution of the human who speaks*: with speaking, hearing belongs to his possibility, and because the human can *hear*, he can *learn*. The two senses have a priority in different directions: hearing makes possible *communication*, being-understood by others; seeing has the priority of the *primary disclosing* of the world, so that the seen can be *talked over* and appropriated more fully in λόγος. Aristotle then gives an anticipatory determination of the human's being: τὸ δὲ τῶν ἀνθρώπων γένος ζῇ καὶ τέχνῃ καὶ λογισμοῖς (980b27) — the human race lives *by τέχνη and reasonings*, which are, gathered together, the two modes of λόγον ἔχον already known from the *Ethics*: the ἐπιστημονικόν and the λογιστικόν. This says the human disposes over a *higher* mode of orientation than the animals — an orientation itself having stages. φύσει μὲν οὖν αἴσθησιν ἔχοντα γίγνεται τὰ ζῷα, ἐκ δὲ ταύτης τοῖς μὲν αὐτῶν οὐκ ἐγγίγνεται μνήμη, τοῖς δ' ἐγγίγνεται (980a27): animals have first a mere αἴσθησις, some also μνήμη, retaining — where μνήμη means not *remembering* but *keeping something in mind* in the widest sense, requiring no λόγος or νοεῖν. καὶ διὰ τοῦτο ταῦτα φρονιμώτερα καὶ μαθητικώτερα τῶν μὴ δυναμένων μνημονεύειν (b1): on the ground of this retaining, living things have a certain φρόνησις — φρόνησις in the wider sense, a certain *security of orientation*; and those animals that can *hear* have at once the possibility, in a sense, of *learning* — they can be trained. μνήμη, understood in this wide form already present in animals, plays a fundamental role in the formation of τέχνη as a human mode of orientation. On quite definite paths, perceiving forms an ἐμπειρία: ἐκ μνήμης.

##### b) ἐμπειρία. The referential nexus: as-soon-as–then. Its temporal character

γίγνεται δ' ἐκ τῆς μνήμης ἐμπειρία τοῖς ἀνθρώποις· αἱ γὰρ πολλαὶ μνῆμαι τοῦ αὐτοῦ πράγματος μιᾶς ἐμπειρίας δύναμιν ἀποτελοῦσιν (980b28): out of μνήμη arises ἐμπειρία in humans — many μνῆμαι of the *same* state of affairs make up the capacity for a *single* ἐμπειρία, a single *procedure*. The essential in ἐμπειρία is the *holding-present of a determinate nexus of occurrences in the same matter*. Aristotle later (981a7) gives a medical example, which Heidegger draws forward: when everyday experience invents definite *means* for the state of health, these means at first stand *outside* any real insight into the causal connection of the means with what it is to remove. What is understood is only a *nexus of the presence of definite occurrences*. Schematically: *as soon as* such-and-such a state sets in, *then* such-and-such a means must be applied — as-soon-as, then. What the state is, what the means is, and *how* the state is removed, does not come to insight at all; all that matters is *relieving the ill*. This nexus is manifestly a *temporal* one, and at first a *purely* temporal one: as-soon-as… then… — a peculiar nexus of the *temporal being* of Dasein. Dasein's *making-present*, which expresses itself in the "now," appears here as an "as-soon-as": as soon as this…, then that…

This nexus can form itself, in the course of *time*, into experience: πλῆθος γὰρ χρόνου ποιεῖ τὴν ἐμπειρίαν (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 8; 1142a15). Then Dasein disposes over a determinate orientation, in which only *this* as-soon-as–then nexus is set off. Heidegger names this temporal structure the *nexus of making-present* (*Zusammenhang des Gegenwärtigens*): as soon as this is present, then that must be procured, made present too. In αἴσθησις, Dasein's nearest self-orienting, the circumstances and things are *contingent*, as it just happens to give itself; over against contingent, arbitrary *trying-out*, ἐμπειρία already has a *determinate security* — here the "as soon as this, then that," the determinate nexus *as* determinate, is already set off. So ἐμπειρία already has present a μία ὑπόληψις, one holding-to-be (ἔχει ὑπόληψιν, 981a7): Dasein is *familiar* with the nexus, moves in a holding-to-be — but is still *without insight into the nexus as such*; there is here no looking-at the *what*, because Dasein is still wholly absorbed in *accomplishing*; a quite primitive making-present. Yet even so ἐμπειρία has a priority over mere perceiving: within mere accomplishing it is already a δύναμις, a first oriented being-positioned-toward — for over against the *multiplicity* of αἴσθησις, the *unity* of a determinate matter-nexus is placed at Dasein's disposal in ἐμπειρία. As δύναμις, ἐμπειρία is a determinate *prefiguring* of comportment, measured to the respective occurrence or absence of…; in it lies a certain *readiness* for the chances and circumstances as they can arise — an orientation that is *sure* but bears *no insight* in itself.

The "more" that comes into view with ἐμπειρία Aristotle describes: οἱ γὰρ ἔμπειροι περὶ ἕκαστα κρίνουσιν ὀρθῶς τὰ ἔργα, καὶ δι' ὧν ἢ πῶς ἐπιτελεῖται συνιᾶσιν, καὶ ποῖα ποίοις συνᾴδει· τοῖς δ' ἀπείροις ἀγαπητὸν τὸ μὴ διαλανθάνειν εἰ εὖ ἢ κακῶς πεποίηται τὸ ἔργον (*Nic. Eth.* X, 10; 1181a19). The experienced — those broken-in to a determinate direction — can decide περὶ ἕκαστα, about each step, understand *through what* and *how* the ἔργα are brought to their end and which matters go together with which; while the ἄπειροι, the inexperienced, though they too have some acquaintance with the work, must content themselves with its "not being wholly concealed" from them (διαλανθάνειν — λανθάνειν — *ἀ-ληθές*!) whether what comes out is well or badly made. Heidegger seizes the etymological glint: even the inexperienced person's poverty is described with a word of *un-concealment*. The ἄπειρος has a judgment only over the naked *result*; to the ἔμπειρος even the *εἶδος* is no longer concealed. Although this transparency is present in ἐμπειρία, the matter-nexus *as such* does not yet come into view. Out of this ἐμπειρία τέχνη can form itself.

##### c) τέχνη. The modifications of the referential nexus. The setting-off of the εἶδος. When–so. Because–therefore. τέχνη and ἐμπειρία. καθόλου and καθ' ἕκαστον

γίγνεται δὲ τέχνη ὅταν ἐκ πολλῶν τῆς ἐμπειρίας ἐννοημάτων μία καθόλου γένηται περὶ τῶν ὁμοίων ὑπόληψις (981a5): τέχνη arises when out of many ἐμπειρία-notions *one* ὑπόληψις arises that has for its object the *καθόλου*, the universal. In ἐμπειρία there is a security about the *referential nexus*; when ἐμπειρία *hardens*, out of a manifold looking-at what is at issue there forms a ὑπόληψις μία καθόλου. In the many single cases toward which one comported in ἐμπειρία in the manner *as-soon-as-this–then-that*, there comes, in the *repetition* in which one comports *always as-soon-as-this–then-that*, the *same* — and with it the *what* — to setting-off, to understanding (ἐννοεῖσθαι). Over against the purely temporal nexus the respective *what* is *uncovered*: τὸ εἶδος ἀφορίζεται (a10), the εἶδος is *set off*, the matter now understood κατ' εἶδος ἕν, with a view to a *self-maintaining, constantly recurring look*.

This modifies what in ἐμπειρία was given in a wholly preliminary understanding: the *as-soon-as–then* becomes a *when-this–then-that*, a *when–so*. And this "when" has at first a quite remarkable, indifferent sense: it means no longer merely a bare *as-soon-as* but already a certain *because* — *when*, i.e. in a way *because*, such-and-such shows itself, I must arrange such-and-such. So the referential nexus modifies itself in that the *understanding becomes more genuine* — and it becomes more genuine insofar as what is at issue is *set off in its look*. Understanding is now grounded no longer in the making-present of the execution-nexus, in the retaining of *succession*, but in the *presentation of the look of the matter* on whose treatment the concern is directed. Therefore we say that the one who disposes over τέχνη is σοφώτερος, more a σοφός, than the one who has only ἐμπειρία: καὶ σοφωτέρους τοὺς τεχνίτας τῶν ἐμπείρων ὑπολαμβάνομεν (a25). The new phenomenon that permits addressing τέχνη as σοφωτέρα lies in the direction of *seeing*, not of *execution* — which remains untouched. On the contrary: execution as such can even turn out *better* in ἐμπειρία than in τέχνη: πρὸς μὲν οὖν τὸ πράττειν ἐμπειρία τέχνης οὐδὲν δοκεῖ διαφέρειν, ἀλλὰ καὶ μᾶλλον ἐπιτυγχάνοντας ὁρῶμεν τοὺς ἐμπείρους τῶν ἄνευ τῆς ἐμπειρίας λόγον ἐχόντων (a12). As regards *doing*, ἐμπειρία seems to differ in nothing from τέχνη; indeed we see the experienced *reach their goal better* than those who without ἐμπειρία have only the λόγος — i.e. have the *look*, the structural nexus of the being-produced, uncovered at their disposal. The one rightly broken-in, who grasps, has mostly the advantage as regards *success* over the one who disposes over the know-how.

The ground: αἴτιον δ' ὅτι ἡ μὲν ἐμπειρία τῶν καθ' ἕκαστόν ἐστι γνῶσις ἡ δὲ τέχνη τῶν καθόλου, αἱ δὲ πράξεις καὶ αἱ γενέσεις πᾶσαι περὶ τὸ καθ' ἕκαστόν εἰσιν (a15) — ἐμπειρία is knowledge of the *particular* (καθ' ἕκαστον), τέχνη of the *universal* (καθόλου), while all πράξεις and γενέσεις bear on the *particular*. This is the pivot: τέχνη is "wiser" not because it acts better but because it *sees the what*, the recurring look, and so has ascended from the as-soon-as of mere succession to the *because* of the εἶδος — the first appearance, at the level of everyday production, of the move toward the καθόλου that σοφία will complete. That this same τέχνη is *less* effective in execution than experience prepares the paradox the chapter is built to resolve: the direction of *increasing σοφία* is the direction of *withdrawal from use and doing* toward pure seeing — the very διαγωγή that defines θεωρεῖν. The step from καθ' ἕκαστον to καθόλου, and the question of which is "first" and "more knowable," is exactly what the excursus of §12 will take up.

Heidegger draws the ontological consequence before Aristotle's text states it. What in the referential nexus was at first αἴτιον — "to blame" for something, motive for something — becomes more and more *ἀρχή*. The *why* is then no longer what *guides the accomplishing* but only what *uncovers the being*. The nexus-of-origin *in the structure of the being itself*, and thereby the being itself, is more and more uncovered and understood. In the tendency toward the merely *uncovering regarding* of the being with respect to the ἀρχή lies the σοφώτερον. So in τέχνη the *pre-sketch* for σοφία is given. The temporal reading is made explicit: in ἐμπειρία the *as-soon-as-this–then-that* expresses a making-present procuring, a *pro-ducing*; as ἐμπειρία hardens it becomes *always-then-this–as-soon-as-that*, and in repetition *when-this–so-that*, *because-this–therefore-that*, whereby the *what*-nexus is set off; and the making-present of that nexus is ultimately *preliminary* for the uncovering of the being *out of its ἀρχή* — the ἀρχή being the *from-whence* that is *always already there*. What the making-present of that nexus is ultimately preliminary *for* is: to *make the being available in its presence (οὐσία)* in the uncovering *going-back* to what is already there, the ἀρχή.

Heidegger then states his hermeneutic openly, and it is the fullest statement of method in the course. This structure is *not expressly there in Aristotle*; interpretation *goes beyond* what at first stands in the text — but this is *no reading-in* (*Hineindeuten*); the point is to uncover what was *unexpressly present* among the Greeks. Going beyond what the primitive understanding first sees carries a certain danger of ascribing *too much* to Aristotle and the Greeks — yet on closer acquaintance one notices they *deserve* this "too much." If it must come to an exact reckoning, one need only make *deductions* after having first gone beyond the text; such reckoning is enough if precisely thereby *what alone stands there* becomes more intelligible. On such a hermeneutic everything turns. And the guiding decision — to orient the Greek concept of being *at time* — is no mere notion but has its quite definite grounding, which Plato will show.

#### §12. Excursus: καθόλου and καθ' ἕκαστον. The way of philosophy (esp. *Met.* V, 26; *Top.* VI, 4; *Phys.* I, 1)

*(The editor notes that no notes for this excursus survive in Heidegger's hand; it is reconstructed from the transcripts of Jonas, Schalk, and Weiß.)* καθόλου is compounded of κατά and ὅλον, so the being of the καθόλου must be clarified from the concept of *ὅλον*, whole — for which Aristotle gives the orientation in *Met.* V, 26.

##### a) The manifold meanings of ὅλον. The καθόλου as ὅλον λεγόμενον (*Met.* V, 26)

Aristotle distinguishes several senses of ὅλον. (1) ὅλον λέγεται οὗ τε μηθὲν ἄπεστι μέρος ἐξ ὧν λέγεται ὅλον φύσει (1023b26): a ὅλον is that from which no part, no belonging component, is *absent* — positively, the *full presence* of the being in what belongs to its being. Heidegger renders it "completeness" (*Vollständigkeit*): the being in its *full stand*. Notably Aristotle gives the *same* definition for τέλειον (V, 16; 1021b12): τέλειον is that outside which not even a single component can be taken. So ὅλον means first the full presence of the components that make up a being's being-finished. (2) καὶ τὸ περιέχον τὰ περιεχόμενα ὥστε ἓν τι εἶναι ἐκεῖνα (1023b27): the ὅλον is the *encompassing* such that the encompassed are a kind of *one*. This second sense splits: the ὅλον is περιέχον either (a) ᾗ γὰρ ὡς ἕκαστον ἕν — such that *each* of the encompassed is *itself* this ὅλον — or (b) ἢ ὡς ἐκ τούτων τὸ ἕν — such that the one *consists of* the encompassed. The example of (a) is precisely the καθόλου: τὸ μὲν γὰρ καθόλου καὶ τὸ ὅλως λεγόμενον ὡς ὅλον τι ὄν, οὕτως ἐστὶ καθόλου ὡς πολλὰ περιέχον τῷ κατηγορεῖσθαι καθ' ἑκάστου καὶ ἓν ἅπαντα εἶναι ὡς ἕκαστον, οἷον ἄνθρωπον, ἵππον, θεόν, διότι ἅπαντα ζῷα (b29). *Living-being* (ζῷον) is a ὅλον; human, horse, god are ἕκαστα; and ζῷον unites these ἕκαστα into a single whole such that *each single one, as single, is living-being*. This peculiar trait — that of many singles each single *is* the whole — is possible only τῷ κατηγορεῖσθαι καθ' ἑκάστου, "in that the ὅλον is *said of* each ἕκαστον." The determination is already indicated in the word καθόλου itself, insofar as the κατά points to *λέγειν* as κατάφασις. The καθόλου belongs to Dasein *insofar as Dasein is uncovering in the manner of λέγειν*: the καθόλου is a *ὅλον λεγόμενον*, a wholeness that shows itself *only in λέγειν*, distinguished in that its being is determined by *accessibility in λόγος*. That the καθόλου is a whole in relation to its one is visible only in the κατηγορεῖσθαι. Within the kinds of ὅλον, the καθόλου has this distinguished position: that in it λέγειν *comes into function*.

The second kind of ὅλον περιέχον, (b), is given in all that one calls *συνεχές*, continuous: τὸ δὲ συνεχὲς καὶ πεπερασμένον, ὅταν ἕν τι ἐκ πλειόνων ᾖ, ἐνυπαρχόντων μάλιστα μὲν δυνάμει, εἰ δὲ μή, ἐνεργείᾳ (b32). A stretch is a ὅλον, but such that it consists ἐκ πλειόνων, of many single points; not each single point *is* the ὅλον, the line — rather all the points *together* first constitute the ἕν. Mostly the ἐνυπάρχοντα, the components-within, are there only δυνάμει, potentially (in seeing a stretch the single points are not expressly set off); when set off, they are there ἐνεργείᾳ. *Before* this sense of ὅλον as συνεχές stands the primarily *ontological* sense in which ὅλον is identical with τέλειον, complete — the full belonging-together of the determinations that make up a being. Then a third kind: (3) the *totality*, πᾶν. ἔτι τοῦ ποσοῦ ἔχοντος δὲ ἀρχὴν καὶ μέσον καὶ ἔσχατον, ὅσων μὲν μὴ ποιεῖ ἡ θέσις διαφοράν, πᾶν λέγεται, ὅσων δὲ ποιεῖ, ὅλον (1024a1). Where the *arrangement* (θέσις) of the parts makes *no* difference, it is a πᾶν, a *totality* (the totality of points is other than the whole stretch — the concept of a *set*, where no point has precedence over another); where the θέσις *does* make a difference, it is a ὅλον. Something can be πᾶν and ὅλον at once: ἔστι δὲ ταῦτα ὅσων ἡ μὲν φύσις ἡ αὐτὴ μένει τῇ μεταθέσει, ἡ δὲ μορφὴ οὔ, οἷον κηρὸς καὶ ἱμάτιον (a3) — where the φύσις stays the same under rearrangement but the *μορφή*, the shape, does not (a garment folded or worn differently keeps its φύσις but alters its μορφή). Finally: number is called πᾶν but not ὅλος (a7), the ἀριθμός being called πᾶν, totality, not ὅλον; and πάντα, "all," names not the whole but the separated units. This analysis, Heidegger stresses, has *fundamental* significance for Aristotle for the structure of beings and for the *λόγος that uncovers beings in their structure* — and from it the difference between καθόλου and καθ' ἕκαστον emerges, which lies in the *mode of access* to beings and thereby in the *degree of uncoveredness (ἀλήθεια)* of beings.

##### b) The mode of access as distinguishing καθ' ἕκαστον from καθόλου. αἴσθησις and λόγος. πρὸς ἡμᾶς γνωριμώτερον and ἁπλῶς γνωριμώτερον. The way of philosophy (per *Top.* VI, 4 and *Met.* VII, 3): from καθ' ἕκαστον to καθόλου

The καθόλου is a determinate ὅλον whose distinction is that its being is determined by *accessibility through λόγος*: it is a ὅλον λεγόμενον. The καθόλου can *never* be uncovered in an αἴσθησις that holds to bare eye-sight; to see the καθόλου I must *speak*, address something *as* something. In this difference between λόγος and αἴσθησις lies also the difference between καθόλου and καθ' ἕκαστον. The καθ' ἕκαστον is the being as it offers itself *at first*, i.e. in αἴσθησις; the καθόλου is what shows itself *first and only in λέγειν*. This distinction concerns the fundamental question *in what manner and in what sequence of stages the being becomes accessible in the genuineness of its being*. Dasein has two *outermost possibilities* in which it is uncovering, pre-sketched by this distinction: καθ' ἕκαστον and καθόλου. Heidegger marks the grammatical clue: καθ' ἕκαστον has κατά with the *accusative*, καθόλου with the *genitive*. With the accusative κατά means *extending over* something; with the genitive it means that what a comportment extends over is *expressly taken into view* (as in τοξεύειν κατά τινος, to shoot down *at* someone from the tree). So the ὅλον in καθόλου is, per the genitive, distinguished in that it shows itself *only insofar as it expressly becomes theme*, while the καθ' ἕκαστον in αἴσθησις shows itself *of itself*, without becoming theme.

This is also caught in the distinction between ἁπλῶς γνωριμώτερον and πρὸς ἡμᾶς γνωριμώτερον. (1) πρὸς ἡμᾶς γνωριμώτερον — *more familiar with reference to us* — is the being uncovered in our *nearest* comportment: the καθ' ἕκαστον that shows itself in αἴσθησις. The being understood *in its proper being* — what is *always already there* at the being and from which all else is determined — is at first *concealed* to us. (2) ἁπλῶς γνωριμώτερον — *more familiar simply*, without reference to us, with a view to the being *at itself* — is what is *simply there* at the being such that it *gives all else its presence*: the καθόλου, most accessible through λόγος or νοῦς, while under αἴσθησις falls at first the καθ' ἕκαστον. Aristotle's series: ἁπλῶς μὲν οὖν γνωριμώτερον τὸ πρότερον τοῦ ὑστέρου, οἷον στιγμὴ γραμμῆς καὶ γραμμὴ ἐπιπέδου καὶ ἐπίπεδον στερεοῦ (point prior to line, line to plane, plane to solid), while ἡμῖν δ' ἀνάπαλιν — *for us the reverse* — since μάλιστα γὰρ τὸ στερεὸν ὑπὸ τὴν αἴσθησιν πίπτει, the *solid* most falls under perception.

The decisive philosophical point Heidegger extracts: the being, even when it is there for the nearest regarding, is *not yet ἀλήθεια, not yet the being as uncovered* — and it is precisely ἀλήθεια *about which* one philosophizes. This does not mean one *speculates about "truth"*; the equating of ὄν and ἀλήθεια becomes clear only when one is clear about ἀλήθεια itself. And though in αἴσθησις a "badly familiar uncovered" is present, one must *take one's departure from it* — for this badly-uncovered is nonetheless "familiar to oneself," is the *ground one disposes over*. One must appropriate this ground expressly, and *not* leap over the reality that a theory posits as "bad" to some *super-being* (*Übersein*) — *as Plato did*. It will not do to posit the at-first-familiar, badly-uncovered, as *μὴ ὄν*; one must start from it and, μεταβαίνων, "striding *through* this badly-uncovered itself, see the simply and properly familiar." For Plato it was so that he won a certain sense of being — though also not as radically as Aristotle later — and it then "happened" to him to address *this being as the being*, so that he had to posit *what is properly the being as non-being*. This peculiar failing Aristotle *completely saw through* — for a Greek an achievement of which we can perhaps form no conception. This is Heidegger's clearest early statement of his critique of Plato: the χωρισμός and the hypostatizing of the Ideas into a super-being are a *failure to hold to the phenomenal ground*, corrected by Aristotle's insistence on μετάβασις *through* the perceptually given.

Precisely the καθ' ἕκαστον of αἴσθησις one must *get a grip on*, taking up at it the *first fact* of the being — which even Aristotle managed only within certain limits, so that despite the radical tendency he did not press through to the *last originality* of the being of the world. (An interpretation is possible that itself tries to see the being of the world *detached from the Greek concept of being* — but that shall not happen here in the lecture.) So the *way* on which the being is uncovered in the genuineness of its being goes *from the καθ' ἕκαστον, striding through it (μεταβαίνων), to the καθόλου* — the καθ' ἕκαστον being the πρὸς ἡμᾶς γνωριμώτερον that shows in αἴσθησις, the καθόλου showing only in λόγος. *De Anima* B 5: τῶν καθ' ἕκαστον ἡ κατ' ἐνέργειαν αἴσθησις, ἡ δ' ἐπιστήμη τῶν καθόλου (417b22).

##### c) The way of philosophy (*Phys.* I, 1). From καθόλου to καθ' ἕκαστον. Resolution of the apparent contradiction between *Top.* VI, 4 and *Phys.* I, 1

But in the introduction to the *Physics* Aristotle stresses that the way leads *from the καθόλου to the καθ' ἕκαστον*: διὸ ἐκ τῶν καθόλου εἰς τὰ καθ' ἕκαστα δεῖ προϊέναι (184a25) — exactly the *reverse* of the way just characterized, an apparent *contradiction*. If it should turn out to be no contradiction in truth, we would thereby win a sharper clarification of both concepts — for these are *not thing-concepts* cut to a determinate being. The difficulty is heightened because the cited sentence is preceded by considerations that *coincide* with what was just said: πέφυκε δὲ ἐκ τῶν γνωριμωτέρων ἡμῖν ἡ ὁδὸς καὶ σαφεστέρων ἐπὶ τὰ σαφέστερα τῇ φύσει καὶ γνωριμώτερα (a16) — the way, per our φύσις, our Dasein, is determined by αἴσθησις: it goes *from the more familiar to us* to *what is by its nature the more familiar*. And even sharpened over the *Topics*: οὐ γὰρ ταὐτὰ ἡμῖν τε γνώριμα καὶ ἁπλῶς (a18), for not the same is familiar-to-us and familiar-simply.

The resolution lies in the sense of the *departure-point*. ἔστι δ' ἡμῖν πρῶτον δῆλα καὶ σαφῆ τὰ συγκεχυμένα μᾶλλον (a21): for us δῆλον at first is what is *more still poured-together* (*zusammengeschüttet*), the un-set-off. A body is at first given as a poured-together; surface, line, point are given only *un-set-off*; one handles the body and sees at first only the body. ὕστερον δ' ἐκ τούτων γίνεται γνώριμα τὰ στοιχεῖα καὶ αἱ ἀρχαὶ διαιροῦσι ταῦτα (a22): out of this συγκεχυμένως-δῆλον there become *later* familiar the στοιχεῖα, the elements — surface, line, point — and the ἀρχαί, the *departure-points* from which the body, per its being-constitution, *comes into being*: the point. The poured-together is set off "in that we *take it apart*." Such *διαιρεῖν* is the basic function of λόγος: λόγος *lays apart* in discussing. The συγκεχυμένα Aristotle characterizes in the same chapter as ἀδιορίστως (184b2), *not yet delimited*; the ἀρχαί are still *concealed*; the *whole* is seen. The συγκεχυμένα are to be taken apart in λόγος and thereby pass from something unclarified to something *delimited*, so that the *boundary* of the single determinations lies fixed and what was at first given συγκεχυμένως can be grasped in the ὁρισμός (b12).

On closer look: with the συγκεχυμένα are *from the outset co-meant* the components of the being that are to be lifted out in the proper regarding — the ἀρχαί. When Aristotle says the being is given συγκεχυμένως, he means it is *already interrogated toward an ἀρχή*. When we make a body present in the nearest regarding, its ἀρχαί are not *expressly* given, but they *are* there, *un-uncovered, in αἴσθησις*. This agrees with *Met.* VII, 3: the being insofar as it is given in αἴσθησις — what is at first known to us — has *little or nothing of the being*. Because the ἀρχαί are in a certain way there, but *poured-together*, therefore the being is *not yet there*; the *presence* is not yet uncovered and grasped *as such*. Accordingly the ἀρχαί — or, identically, the καθόλου — are *in their structure* still concealed; the μέρη are not yet disclosed, not yet laid apart in the διαίρεσις. So it becomes intelligible how Aristotle can say τὸ γὰρ ὅλον κατὰ τὴν αἴσθησιν γνωριμώτερον (a24): "the whole is, with a view to perception, more familiar." I see at first the whole body, and this ὅλον has *potentially* (δυνάμει) in itself the περιεχόμενα.

The ὅλον in the sense of καθόλου thus has, as now emerges, a *double* meaning: (1) the ὅλον λεγόμενον in the already-explicated sense — the whole that shows only in λέγειν, in that in addressing, each encompassed, each καθ' ἕκαστον, shows *itself* as the whole (human, horse, god each for itself ζῷον); (2) the καθόλου means at once that each ζῷον *as such has in itself a structure* — the καθόλου encloses in itself, apart from the single cases it encompasses, determinate *structural moments* not at first expressly given in αἴσθησις. The καθόλου is at first *itself συγκεχυμένως there*.

So *Phys.* I, 1 (184a23) is *no contradiction* to the *Topics*; on the contrary it makes it more explicit: *the way goes from the un-articulated καθόλου to the articulated καθ' ἕκαστον*, so that each single μέρος becomes visible. And the καθ' ἕκαστον now first becomes visible in its *functional* sense: it means here *not a determinate region of beings* but the *manner of being-articulated or not-articulated*. So καθ' ἕκαστον means (1) the at-first-set-off of αἴσθησις, and (2) the *simply* set-off of the *moments that lie in the καθόλου itself*. With this agrees the course of regard Aristotle carries through in the *Physics*, which is from the outset *ἀρχή-research*, the grasping of the ἀρχαί: ἐπιστήμη is always ἐπιστήμη of the καθόλου, and this goes from the *un-articulated* καθόλου to the *articulated*, such that its μέρη are manifest in the ὁρισμός — exactly what the methodical principle ἐκ τῶν καθόλου εἰς τὰ καθ' ἕκαστα δεῖ προϊέναι expresses.

In this principle, seemingly wholly formal, Aristotle grasps at once the *movement-sense of the history of the interrogation of the being of φύσις* that preceded his research and that he laid down in *Physics* I. At first the philosophers, questioning the given of the world, saw what *at first gave itself*, and saw it *un-articulated* — above all the Eleatics, who at first saw *only being*. Aristotle adduces Parmenides' ἓν τὰ πάντα (I, 2; 185a22): being is everywhere being; all that is, is being, being-present, there. With reference to Aristotle's own task — to find a *manifold* of ἀρχαί, a *structure* of this ὄν — the ἓν of the Eleatics presents itself to him such that in them this basic structure is still συγκεχυμένως and not yet set off. Others, not yet so far, took a *determinate being* as ἀρχή and posited it for the whole — Thales water, Anaximander air *(sic; Anaximenes)* — seeing what at first offered itself as what *rules throughout* the being, and positing it as ἀρχή. This history of ἀρχή-research hovers before Aristotle when he utters the sentence ἐκ τῶν καθόλου εἰς τὰ καθ' ἕκαστα at the *Physics'* opening. The excursus thus does triple duty: it fixes the ontological sense of *whole* and *universal*, it establishes the *two-directioned way of philosophy* (perception gives the whole confusedly; λόγος articulates it into its ἀρχαί), and it plants Heidegger's critique of the Platonic χωρισμός — the leap to a super-being — against which Aristotle's μετάβασις-through-the-given is held up as the corrective.

So too becomes positively intelligible what Aristotle says at *Met.* VII, 3 (1029b5): καὶ τοῦτο ἔργον ἐστίν, ὥσπερ ἐν ταῖς πράξεσι τὸ ποιῆσαι ἐκ τῶν ἑκάστῳ ἀγαθῶν τὰ ὅλως ἀγαθὰ ἑκάστῳ ἀγαθά, οὕτως ἐκ τῶν αὐτῷ γνωριμωτέρων τὰ τῇ φύσει γνώριμα αὐτῷ γνώριμα. The task is the same as in acting: just as in acting one starts from what is in each case good *for oneself* and goes *through* it to the ὅλως ἀγαθόν, so that in accomplishing the ὅλως ἀγαθόν the ἑκάστῳ ἀγαθόν is at once accomplished — so in knowing one must start from what is at first the more familiar *for oneself* and go through it to the τῇ φύσει (or ὅλως) familiar, in order *from this to go back again* to the αὐτῷ γνώριμον, so that this becomes transparent *from* that. One must press through from the at-first-familiar to the ἀρχή and appropriate it such that, *from* this appropriation, a *proper* appropriation of the καθ' ἕκαστον takes place — the transparency of the departure-point itself won, the καθ' ἕκαστον understood *out of* the ἀρχαί. Only from here do we understand what it means that the καθόλου is the proper theme of τέχνη and ἐπιστήμη.

#### §13. Continuation: τέχνη and ἐπιστήμη (*Met.* I, 1). The tendency in τέχνη toward an "autonomous" ἐπιστήμη. The further development of ἐπιστήμη

Unlike the ἔμπειρος, the τεχνίτης is the one who ἄνευ τῆς ἐμπειρίας ἔχει τὸν λόγον (981a21) — who, without being broken-in to the mode of treatment, *knows the εἶδος*; who knows the respective being καθόλου, "in its whole," but is unfamiliar with τὸ ἐν τούτῳ καθ' ἕκαστον, "with what the being in each case is for itself," which in this ὅλον is *one among others*. Decisive for τέχνη is the *hearing-toward, the seeing-toward*, i.e. the *uncovering*. Hence: "οἱ ἀρχιτέκτονες" τὰς αἰτίας τῶν ποιουμένων ἴσασιν (981b1), the master-builders *know the causes* of what is produced. This shows at once: the αἰτία, i.e. the καθόλου, is at first *not the theme of a mere regarding*. It is set off as εἶδος, but not so as to be theme of a *special investigation*. The knowing of the αἰτία is there at first only *in the nexus of accomplishing itself* — the αἰτία is there at first only as the *because-therefore* of the so-and-so proceeding; the εἶδος is at first there only *in τέχνη itself*. But because in τέχνη the εἶδος is *already* lifted out, the τεχνῖται are ascribed μᾶλλον εἰδέναι (a31), to know *more* and to be σοφώτεροι than the mere ἔμπειροι. The μᾶλλον is ascribed κατὰ τὸ λόγον ἔχειν (b6), with a view to the *developing of the talking-over* of what is object of the concernful dealing, i.e. with a view to *uncovering*. The λέγειν becomes, *within* accomplishing, more and more *autonomous*, and this the nearest natural Dasein interprets as σοφώτερον. Further, the λόγον ἔχων can make something intelligible in *how it comes to its being*, how the whole hangs together; he knows that *out of which* something consists, *for what* it is contributory, how thereby something becomes so available-present as it is — so he can give *information* about the being in its *origin* (δύναται διδάσκειν, b7). Hence natural Dasein holds τὴν τέχνην τῆς ἐμπειρίας μᾶλλον ἐπιστήμην εἶναι (b8): τέχνη, because it *has the λόγος* and can give information about the being in its origin, is held to be *more ἐπιστήμη* than ἐμπειρία. So within the genesis of σοφία, τέχνη *moves together with* ἐπιστήμη; it is downright *called* ἐπιστήμη.

Two things, then, are called ἐπιστήμη: (1) τέχνη, and (2) the highest science, σοφία, in its determination as νοῦς καὶ ἐπιστήμη (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 7; 1141a19). The first meaning — ἐπιστήμη as much as τέχνη — is the *everyday* one, and in it the concept takes a peculiar *middle position*. τέχνη is called ἐπιστήμη insofar as, unlike ἐμπειρία, it already *draws out the εἶδος*; but this does not yet properly determine what makes up the distinguished character of ἐπιστήμη. τέχνη is ἐπιστήμη although it is properly a ἕξις ποιητική aiming at ποίησις; yet at once it is ἕξις μετὰ λόγου ἀληθοῦς (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 4; 1140a10). *In τέχνη, ἐπιστήμη is properly stretched out in front of the accomplishing-tendency*; in τέχνη lies the tendency to *free itself from handling* and become *autonomously* itself an ἐπιστήμη — and insofar as this tendency lies in it, the nearest natural Dasein ascribes to it being σοφώτερον.

Against this: τῶν αἰσθήσεων οὐδεμίαν ἡγούμεθα εἶναι σοφίαν (981b10) — natural Dasein does *not* ascribe to αἴσθησις the character of σοφία, καίτοι κυριώταταί γ' εἰσὶν αὗται τῶν καθ' ἕκαστα γνώσεις (b11), even though αἴσθησις is the mode of ἀληθεύειν in which the καθ' ἕκαστον, the respective, becomes accessible *as such*. That is why, in the field of what goes to the καθ' ἕκαστον, αἴσθησις is a κύριον beside νοῦς and ὄρεξις (*Nic. Eth.* VI, 2; 1139a18) — indeed Aristotle later (VI, 9; 1142a23) even in a way *equates* αἴσθησις with φρόνησις. But: ἀλλ' οὐ λέγουσι τὸ διὰ τί περὶ οὐδενός (981b11), the αἰσθήσεις give of *no* given that shows itself the *why*; therefore natural Dasein does not ascribe to them the character of σοφία. Uncovering the *why* is the mark of the higher stages.

The tendency in τέχνη to free itself from handling and become autonomous ἐπιστήμη is grounded *in Dasein itself* — shown for Aristotle by the fact that a τεχνίτης who, as we say, "*dis-covers*" something is *admired*: τὸν ὁποιανοῦν εὑρόντα τέχνην παρὰ τὰς κοινὰς αἰσθήσεις θαυμάζεσθαι ὑπὸ τῶν ἀνθρώπων μὴ μόνον διὰ τὸ χρήσιμον εἶναί τι τῶν εὑρεθέντων ἀλλ' ὡς σοφὸν καὶ διαφέροντα τῶν ἄλλων (b13). The τεχνίτης who discovers something *beyond* what everyone sees is admired — esteemed as one who *distinguishes himself*, who makes what one cannot oneself — and *not* because what he invents is of great use, but because he *presses further in grasping the being*, be the discovered matter great or small: because he is σοφώτερος. This *discovering* is the *going-beyond the nearest possibilities* Dasein has. So in the admiration everyday Dasein distributes, it is attested that in Dasein itself a special *esteem for dis-covering* is alive; Dasein has of itself the tendency to *discover* the being, and *purely* to discover it — μὴ πρὸς χρῆσιν (b19), *apart from all use*. Hence too: the *less* the τεχνάζειν and the ἐπιστήμη are oriented πρὸς τἀναγκαῖα and πρὸς διαγωγήν (b18), toward the urgencies of life and toward pastime, the *more* Dasein addresses those who accomplish such things as σοφώτεροι.

The development of ἐπιστήμη goes further so (Aristotle, 981b20): as soon as the τέχναι and ἐπιστῆμαι demanded πρὸς τὰ ἀναγκαῖα, for life's urgencies, and πρὸς τὴν ἡδονήν, for recreation and pleasure, were found, Dasein could — *unburdened* of these urgencies — devote itself *wholly to regarding*. Therefore the first sciences, e.g. mathematics, arose in *Egypt*, because the *priests* were given time to do nothing but regard. So though a tendency toward uncovering is given in Dasein, the *autonomous* pure-uncovering becomes properly possible only where Dasein is *free from dependence on procuring the ἀναγκαῖα*. In such *σχολάζειν* (having-leisure) a *leap* of the accomplishing-tendency takes place: to *abstain* from procuring the ἀναγκαῖα and merely, *seeingly-uncovering*, to tarry alongside. The more the merely-seeing uncovering comes into its right, the more the *why* — the διὰ τί, the αἰτία — and finally more and more the *from-where*, τὸ διὰ τί πρῶτον (*Met.* I, 3; 983a29), τὰ ἐξ ἀρχῆς αἴτια, the ἀρχή — becomes *visible*.

In αἴσθησις and ἐπιστήμη we now have two *end-stations*, without yet having properly understood σοφία. The possibility that first went beyond the merely momentary disclosing of αἴσθησις and made the being more expressly accessible is *retaining*, μνήμη. This *holding-present* as a mode of access to beings *holds through up to σοφία*, in which the making-present relates *expressly to the ἀρχαί*. The whole genesis is thus one continuous intensification of a single structure — making-present — from the animal's bare retention to the philosopher's regard upon the first origins; and the direction of increase is, throughout, the direction of *withdrawal from use* toward pure seeing, freed in σχολή.

#### §14. σοφία (*Met.* I, 2). The four essential moments of σοφία (πάντα, χαλεπώτατα, ἀκριβέστατα, αὐτῆς ἕνεκεν). Explanatory reduction of the first three to the μάλιστα καθόλου

The question now arises: what is σοφία, or the σοφός himself? Aristotle takes it up in *Met.* I, 2, and — again — the decision is not made *dogmatically*; he goes back to natural nearest Dasein: εἰ δὴ λάβοι τις τὰς ὑπολήψεις ἃς ἔχομεν περὶ τοῦ σοφοῦ, τάχ' ἂν ἐκ τούτου φανερὸν γένοιτο μᾶλλον (982a6). The task is to take up the *holdings-to-be* we *in common* have — what is interpreted as σοφός in natural everyday Dasein — and to make this fore-concept of σοφία more explicit, and so the natural self-interpretation of Dasein more transparent. Aristotle counts *four moments* with which natural Dasein at first characterizes what it means by the σοφός.

(1) πρῶτον μὲν ἐπίστασθαι πάντα τὸν σοφὸν ὡς ἐνδέχεται, μὴ καθ' ἕκαστον ἔχοντα ἐπιστήμην αὐτῶν (982a8). The wise one strikes us first as the one who *knows πάντα, all together* — who in a peculiar sense understands everything, *without* having a knowledge that looks at each single thing separately, without having special expertise in each. And yet, when one talks with him about it, he understands everything, and understands it *properly*. Here it is notable that πάντα in natural speech means the whole in the sense of the *totality, the sum*. The σοφός understands τὰ πάντα, the sum, without having won the knowledge καθ' ἕκαστον, from each respective thing; he understands the sum without having *run through* each unit — so that the knowing of the πάντα, given the manifest *lack* of knowledge of the single, becomes *puzzling*.

(2) τὸν τὰ χαλεπὰ γνῶναι δυνάμενον καὶ μὴ ῥᾴδια ἀνθρώπῳ γιγνώσκειν, τοῦτον σοφόν (a10). The σοφός is the one who has the possibility of uncovering what is *hard* to uncover — hard for the human in his nearest Dasein, for the πολλοί. What the σοφός can uncover is thus not only *concealed* but *difficult* to uncover, because it does not disclose itself to nearest everyday Dasein readily, in habit, comfortably.

(3) τὸν ἀκριβέστερον καὶ τὸν διδασκαλικώτερον τῶν αἰτιῶν σοφώτερον εἶναι περὶ πᾶσαν ἐπιστήμην (a12). The σοφός is in every "science" and τέχνη *more thorough*, comes more *to the ground* of matters; hence he has rather the possibility to *teach*, to impart — he can make the matters clearer, give more proper information on how the matter stands, because he sees it not in its nearest aspect but in its proper *whence and why*.

(4) τῶν ἐπιστημῶν δὲ τὴν αὑτῆς ἕνεκεν καὶ τοῦ εἰδέναι χάριν αἱρετὴν οὖσαν μᾶλλον εἶναι σοφίαν ἢ τὴν τῶν ἀποβαινόντων ἕνεκεν (a14). σοφία is an ἐπιστήμη carried out *simply for its own sake* — in it the uncovering of what is uncovered is enacted *solely for its own sake*, not with an eye to what might spring from it, its practical usability. σοφία is the ἐπιστήμη determined *only by the pure tendency toward seeing*, happening solely τοῦ εἰδέναι χάριν, in order to *see and seeingly to know*, and which *as such leads, guides, pre-sketches*.

Aristotle then works through the four moments singly. Anticipating: in all four an *uncovering* is in view that goes *to the first departure-points of the being purely as such*. Conversely, this idea of σοφία — going to the αἴτια as such, and specifically to τὰ ἐξ ἀρχῆς, the ἀρχαί — makes *explicit* what Dasein *unexpressly, still unclarified to itself, strives toward*. The reduction of the first three moments to the *μάλιστα καθόλου* is the argumentative core.

To (1): τὸ μὲν πάντα ἐπίστασθαι τῷ μάλιστα ἔχοντι τὴν καθόλου ἐπιστήμην ἀναγκαῖον ὑπάρχειν (a21). The σοφός knows "all" because he *most* disposes over the uncovering of the *"in general"*. Because σοφία is an εἰδέναι καθόλου, the σοφός necessarily understands πάντα. Note: the nearest understanding sees the whole as *totality*, and therefore that understanding of "all" is the more puzzling to it, since it lacks the knowledge of the single as "whole." Aristotle clarifies this πάντα as a ὅλον in the sense of the *καθόλου*; he puts the ὅλον in place of the πάντα. Now it is not that the σοφός sees the whole as the *sum* of singles; rather he understands *what each single ultimately is with the other*. The πάντα he disposes over is *grounded in the ὅλον as the καθόλου* — the proper πᾶν the σοφός aims at. In such "all"-understanding what matters is the καθόλου, which is a *ὅλον λεγόμενον*; i.e. what matters is a distinguished λεγόμενον: λόγον ἔχειν. Hence: ἀνάγεται γὰρ τὸ διὰ τί εἰς τὸν λόγον ἔσχατον (*Met.* I, 3; 983a28) — in σοφία the *why*, the αἴτιον, is led back to the *outermost λόγος*, the outermost addressing of the being in its being. Uncovering the καθόλου does *not* need the running-through of each particular in express cognizance, and is not the *sum* of these; the peculiar thing is that it is a *whole* without each case being registered as a case — and yet, indeed *precisely therefore*, the respective is understood in the *genuineness of its being-present*. This is grounded in the σοφός's *leaping ahead* from the outset to the proper whole, whence he has the orientation to *discuss each single concrete thing*. So, without having expertise, he can ultimately *speak along* in the proper matter. Thus Aristotle leads the general talk of πάντα ἐπίστασθαι back to the ὅλον as καθόλου.

To (2): from this reduction it becomes clear at once why everyday interpretation says the σοφός aims at what is *hard* (χαλεπόν) to know. χαλεπώτατα ταῦτα γνωρίζειν τοῖς ἀνθρώποις, τὰ μάλιστα καθόλου· πορρωτάτω γὰρ τῶν αἰσθήσεών ἐστιν (982a24). What the σοφός knows is difficult *because it is the most "in general"* — and this is *farthest removed* from what shows itself in nearest eye-sight, within which everyday regarding holds itself. αἴσθησις is the nearest mode of tarrying and disclosing of the πολλοί; it has nothing of difficulty; everyone moves in it, and one can help another to this everyday orientation and ease it for him. To the φύσις of the human belongs a certain *preference for the nearest-given* in αἴσθησις, the ἀγάπησις τῶν αἰσθήσεων (980a21). And especially when the orientation toward accomplishing-necessity falls away — when everyday Dasein is *rid* of it, when the seeing becomes *free* — precisely then Dasein *loses itself* still more in the *look* of the world, but so that it always *remains in αἴσθησις*. Over against this easy and self-evident moving in eye-sight, the *pressing beyond* it to what properly *is* becomes *hard*. The difficulty lies *not in the matters* but *in Dasein itself*, in a peculiar way of being of Dasein, that of the *"at-first"* (*Zunächst*). Dasein as it at first is has its present in the *now*, in the world; it has the tendency to *cling to the nearest*. But in σοφία it is a matter of pressing into what in nearest Dasein is still *concealed*, into the μάλιστα καθόλου — and this in pressing *against* nearest eye-sight. So σοφία is an uncovering that runs, in relation to nearest Dasein, in a *counter-movement*; σοφία is a *counter-tendency against nearest Dasein* and its tendency to *hang on* at the nearest of eye-sight. *As* σοφία, σοφία is *difficult for Dasein*. Only from here are the matters of σοφία "difficult" as regards their ἀληθεύειν. (Note: relative to αἴσθησις, being σοφώτερον, σοφία, is a μᾶλλον εἰδέναι, a μᾶλλον ἐπαΐειν (981a24); σοφία forms itself in a counter-movement against αἴσθησις — yet αἴσθησις is not *switched off* but *taken as departure-point*, giving the ground, so that the regarding does *not remain in its field*. αἴσθησις is a κύριον, something belonging to Dasein at all, but not something through which the being itself *as being* can be seen.)

To (3): ἀκριβέσταται δὲ τῶν ἐπιστημῶν αἱ μάλιστα τῶν πρώτων εἰσίν (982a25). It is proper to σοφία to be ἀκριβεστάτη — and not because the σοφοί expend special acumen, but because its theme is what *most touches the fundaments of the being in its being*. ἀκριβέστατον, most thorough, is what is μάλιστα τῶν πρώτων, "most presses to the first from-where." These "firsts," the first determinations of the being, are as the most originary not only in themselves *simple* but, in their manifold, most sharply to be grasped, *because they are the fewest*. The peculiarity of the ἀρχαί is that they are *limited in number* and, in this their limited number, transparent among themselves in their relation. In *Physics* I, 2 ff. Aristotle shows there must be *more than one* ἀρχή, but that the ἀρχαί are determined in number by a limit, πέρας; so in a ὁρίζεσθαι it must be *delimited* how many they are, whether two or three, etc. — and only because the ἀρχαί are limited is a determination of the being in its being, an addressing of the being as ὁρίζεσθαι and ὁρισμός, and thereby *science as definitive cognition*, possible and guaranteed. The strictness of science Aristotle illustrates at μαθηματική, at ἀριθμητική and γεωμετρία (982a28): the stricter, more thorough disciplines are those that proceed from *fewer* ἀρχαί, that posit *fewer* originary determinations in the being they thematize — ὅτι γὰρ ἐξ ἐλαττόνων ἀκριβέστεραι τῶν ἐκ προσθέσεως λεγομένων, οἷον ἀριθμητικὴ γεωμετρίας (982a26). Arithmetic differs from geometry: arithmetic has *fewer* ἀρχαί; in geometry, as regards the ἀρχαί, there is a *πρόσθεσις*, an *adding-to*. To understand this, a general orientation about what Aristotle understands by mathematics is needed — given in the following excursus, which is at once a *preparation for our Plato-interpretation*.

The three reductions converge: σοφία's knowing "all," its "difficulty," and its "thoroughness" are one and the same feature seen from three sides — that it is *εἰδέναι of the μάλιστα καθόλου*, the uncovering of the first ἀρχαί as such. The fourth moment (for-its-own-sake) is of a different order — it names not the *object* but the *manner of enactment*, the pure θεωρεῖν freed from use — and it is this fourth that will carry the transition, in §§16 ff., to why σοφία is the *highest* possibility of Dasein and yet, as the *Nicomachean Ethics* X will insist, a possibility in which the human touches on something *more than human*.

#### §15. Excursus: General orientation about the essence of mathematics according to Aristotle

The excursus is not a digression: the ranking of the sciences by *ἀκρίβεια* rests on the claim that arithmetic is stricter than geometry because it proceeds from *fewer* ἀρχαί, and this cannot be understood without seeing what mathematics *is* for Aristotle — and the account doubles as *preparation for the Plato-interpretation*, since the mathematical χωρίζειν is exactly the operation Plato performs, unawares, on the Ideas.

##### a) Fundamentals about mathematics in general (*Phys.* II, 2). χωρίζειν as the basic act of mathematics. Critique of the χωρισμός in Plato's doctrine of Ideas

The μαθηματικαὶ ἐπιστῆμαι have for their theme τὰ ἐξ ἀφαιρέσεως — what shows itself in the *taking-away* of something, namely from the at-first-given. The μαθηματικά are something *taken out* of the φυσικὰ ὄντα, of what at first shows itself. So Aristotle: ὁ μαθηματικὸς χωρίζει (*Phys.* II, 2; 193b31). χωρίζειν, to separate, hangs together with χώρα, *place*; place belongs to the being itself. The μαθηματικός *brings something away from its own place*. The peculiarity is that the mathematical is *not at a place*: οὐκ ἐν τόπῳ. This sounds paradoxical to modern concepts, especially while τόπος is still translated "space" — but a τόπος, place, has only a σῶμα φυσικόν, a physical body. This χωρίζειν, which will meet us in Plato in the doctrine of the χωρισμός of the Ideas — where Plato expressly assigns the Ideas a τόπος, the τόπος οὐρανός — is in Aristotle the manner in which the mathematical *becomes objective at all*.

Aristotle lays this out at *Phys.* II, 2. Mathematical objects — στερεόν, γραμμή — can also be regarded as φυσικά; the natural human sees a surface as πέρας, the *boundary* of a body. The mathematician, by contrast, regards the mathematical objects *purely at themselves*, ἀλλ' οὐχ ᾗ φυσικοῦ σώματος πέρας ἕκαστον (193b32) — not insofar as each (line, surface) is the πέρας, the boundary (the being-finished) of a natural body. This negative determination — that the mathematical is *not* πέρας of the φυσικὸν σῶμα — says nothing other than that the mathematical does not come into consideration *as "place."* Insofar as the φυσικὰ ὄντα are κινούμενα, insofar as being-moved belongs to their being as a basic determination, the mathematical can at first be regarded as belonging to a moved being; but the mathematical *as such* is *taken away* from what is characterized by movement: χωριστὰ γὰρ τῇ νοήσει κινήσεώς ἐστι (193b34), the mathematical is "set-away from the being insofar as it is moved," insofar as it changes, turns over, waxes and wanes — and it is χωριστὰ τῇ νοήσει, set-away *only in a determinate mode of regarding*, in "meaning." (κίνησις itself is at first and mostly κίνησις κατὰ τόπον, change of place — τῆς κινήσεως ἡ κοινὴ μάλιστα καὶ κυριωτάτη κατὰ τόπον ἐστίν, ἣν καλοῦμεν φοράν, *Phys.* IV, 1; 208a31, the most general movement being locomotion, presenting itself in the circuit of the heaven.) When the mathematician takes something out of the φυσικὸν σῶμα, οὐδὲν διαφέρει (193b34), "it makes no difference": nothing in the *content* of what remains the mathematician's theme changes; it becomes nothing other; only the *what* of the πέρας is taken *at itself*, as it looks, as it presents itself in its boundary-content. οὐδὲ γίνεται ψεῦδος χωριζόντων (193b35): "through the taking-out no *deception* arises for the mathematician" — he does not regard something that is *not* what it shows itself as; staying with his special theme, he comes into no danger that it gives itself as other than it is; nothing else is there than what is taken out. *In this χωρισμός everything is in order.*

Then the critical turn: λανθάνουσι δὲ τοῦτο ποιοῦντες καὶ οἱ τὰς ἰδέας λέγοντες (193b35). Those who address the Ideas, who uncover them in λόγος, *also proceed so* — χωρίζοντες, "they take out" — only that they λανθάνουσι, "are concealed" as to *how and that* they do it; they are *not transparent to themselves* in their procedure, its limits and distinctions. λανθάνουσι, "they remain concealed *to themselves* in doing such." (A peculiar use of λανθάνειν — conversely there is then an ἀλήθεια *with respect to Dasein itself*.) Those who speak of Ideas are not clear about which possibilities the χωρισμός carries in itself: it has *legitimate* sense in mathematics, but *not* where it is a matter of determining the *ἀρχαί of the being*. τὰ γὰρ φυσικὰ χωρίζουσιν ἧττον ὄντα χωριστὰ τῶν μαθηματικῶν (193b36): "they set at a place of their own the φύσει ὄντα," i.e. the ἀρχαί proper to them — "which, however, are *less* to be taken from place than the mathematical." For the φύσει ὄντα are κινούμενα; in each category of physical being lies a determinate reference to being-moved. But in their Ideas *as* ἀρχαί they *leave out* precisely the κίνησις that is the basic character of the φύσει ὄντα, so that with these ἀρχαί they posit a *being of their own* — among them finally κίνησις *itself*. One cannot posit the ἀρχαί of the moved φύσει ὄντα as *un-moved*, and κίνησις itself as an Idea — thus as a χωριστόν; in them the κινούμενον *as* κινούμενον must be *co-seen*, and with it fundamentally more — the τόπος itself, by which being and presence are determined. This is Heidegger's precise formulation of the objection to the theory of Ideas: the χωρισμός, valid in mathematics (where taking-away distorts nothing), becomes a *falsification* when applied to the being of the moved world, because it strips away exactly κίνησις and τόπος, the determinations that constitute the presence of φύσει ὄντα.

##### The essence of τόπος (*Phys.* IV, 1–5): boundary (πέρας) and the δύναμις of a being's proper being

Since the difference of geometry and arithmetic turns on θέσις, and θέσις is intelligible only against τόπος, Heidegger inserts an analysis of place. Aristotle determines τόπος at first seemingly naively, from *ἀντιμετάστασις*, mutual replacement (208b1): where now water is, there, when it has gone out as from a vessel, air is again; and when yet another body occupies this same τόπος, the place *was* already (ἦν) — before just water or air is in it. The ἦν does not mean τόπος is something *separated* from what is in it; place is only something *other* than the two that have changed in it. Then comes the characteristic determination: ἔχει τινὰ δύναμιν (208b10), "place has a certain *power*." δύναμις is here understood *strictly ontologically*: place belongs to the being itself, it makes up precisely the *possibility of the proper being-present* of the being in question. This possibility, like every possibility, is *pre-sketched*: each being has its place. Fire (πῦρ) as such has its place ἄνω, above; earth (γῆ) as such has its place κάτω, below. The light has in its being the pre-sketch toward its place, upward; the heavy toward its, downward — nothing arbitrary but φύσει (208b18). Each being has in its being the pre-sketch toward a determinate place, and place is *constitutive for the presence of the being*: each is *borne*, φέρεται, to its place.

ἄνω and κάτω, with the other of the six διαστάσεις (up–down, front–back, right–left), Aristotle calls μέρη or εἴδη of place. And expressly: such determinations are *not only πρὸς ἡμᾶς* — ἔστι δὲ τὰ τοιαῦτα οὐ μόνον πρὸς ἡμᾶς (208b14); for us the above and below is *not always the same*, not properly being, ἀλλὰ κατὰ τὴν θέσιν, ὅπως ἂν στραφῶμεν, γίνεται (208b15), but comes to be *according to the θέσις, as we happen to stand and turn*. Here θέσις comes in, over against τόπος as such. There are thus determinations of τόπος that are in a way *absolute* within the world, and beside them the possibility that some *change* — that for one what is above is for another below; this change is one of θέσις, dependent on how we stand. ἐν δὲ τῇ φύσει διώρισται χωρὶς ἕκαστον (208b18): "in nature itself, however — regarding the things purely in their being — each is set for itself at its place," for the above is nothing arbitrary but *whither fire and the light are borne*, and the below whither the heavy and earthy. So the summarizing characteristic: ὡς οὐ τῇ θέσει διαφέροντα μόνον ἀλλὰ καὶ τῇ δυνάμει (208b21) — they differ not only by θέσις (πρὸς ἡμᾶς) but by δύναμις; this δυνάμει says that place is the *possibility of the proper presence* of the being belonging to it, such that the *direction* to its own place belongs to the being of the being itself, which is itself always δυνάμει.

Place is further specified: τόπος is investigated only because there is κίνησις κατὰ τόπον (211a12) — only because the αἰσθητά meet us *as moved*, because there is such a thing as movement at all, can we strike upon place; in the change of place the place *as such sets itself off*, can be taken by another. οὐ γὰρ πᾶν ἐν τόπῳ, ἀλλὰ τὸ κινητὸν σῶμα (212b28): at a place is only what is κινητόν, moved — hence we believe the *heaven* most of all is at a place, because it is ever in movement (211a13); yet closer regard shows the heaven is *not* at a place — ὁ δ' οὐρανὸς οὐ πού ὅλος οὐδ' ἔν τινι τόπῳ ἐστίν, εἴ γε μηδὲν αὐτὸν περιέχει σῶμα (212b8), since no body encompasses it; rather the heaven *itself is the place for all the beings under it*. And the sharp determination: εἰ τοίνυν μηδὲν τῶν τριῶν ὁ τόπος ἐστί, μήτε τὸ εἶδος μήτε ἡ ὕλη μήτε διάστημά τι, ἀνάγκη τὸν τόπον εἶναι τὸ πέρας τοῦ περιέχοντος σώματος (212a2). Place is the *boundary of the περιέχον*, of what bounds a body — not the boundary of the body itself but that *against which* the body's boundary strikes, so that between these two boundaries there is *no interval*, no διάστημα. This is intelligible only if one holds fast that the world is *absolutely oriented*, that there are distinguished places as such: absolute above, the heaven — the μέσον, the middle of the heaven — and absolute below, the earth. Aristotle himself concedes: δοκεῖ δὲ μέγα τι εἶναι καὶ χαλεπὸν ληφθῆναι ὁ τόπος (212a7), place is *something great and very hard to grasp*, because there always co-appear the ὕλη out of which the body consists and its μορφή, its shape, so that one is tempted to take the extension of the stuff or the boundary of the shape as the place; and because the μετάστασις of the moved happens such that the place itself does *not* move, while the moved has a *priority as regards perceptibility*. Gathered: place has a δύναμις; place is the *possibility of the right belonging-there of a being* — the being-present that accrues to the being as such according to its material constitution — and this δύναμις is *ontological*, the possibility itself being *being*. Place is the *being-able-to-be-present* belonging to the being and co-constituting its being — the *being-able-to-be-there* of a being, such that, being-there, it is *properly there*.

##### b) The difference of geometry and arithmetic. The increasing "abstraction" from the φύσει ὄν: στιγμή = οὐσία θετός; μονάς = οὐσία ἄθετος

Both point and unit are οὐσία — something that *is for itself*. The basic element of arithmetic is the *μονάς*, the unit (to μόνον, only, alone — what simply *remains*, μένειν, what is "alone," "for itself"); the basic element of geometry is the *στιγμή*, the point. The point is distinguished from the unit by a πρόσθεσις, an *adding-to* — in the point lies a *θέσις* in a distinguished sense: μονὰς οὐσία ἄθετος, στιγμὴ δὲ οὐσία θετός· ταύτην ἐκ προσθέσεως (*An. Post.* I, 27; 87a36). Both are ἀδιαίρετον κατὰ τὸ ποσόν, indivisible as to quantity (*Met.* V, 6; 1016b29), but the μονάς is *without* θέσις, the point *with* θέσις. θέσις has the character of the *πρός τι*, the toward-something (like ἕξις, διάθεσις; *Cat.* 7, 6b2): every θέσις is a θέσις τινός, a placing *of* something, an *orientation, situation*, the character of being-toward. So the geometrical object retains a relation to *position* that the arithmetical does not.

##### β) The genesis of geometry and arithmetic starting from τόπος

The geometrical objects make the difference of τόπος and θέσις clear. If one *disregards* the peculiar being-mode of τόπος, which is φύσει-determined, and retains *only the manifold of possible positions*, the orientation-moments, one is put in the position to understand *wherein* the specifically geometrical objects hold themselves. What is lifted out of the αἰσθητά and is then the θετόν, the *posited*, are the *place-moments* — so that the lifted-out geometrical is then *no longer at its place*. The place-moments taken away from the σῶμα in the ἀφαίρεσις are the πέρατα of the physical body; but insofar as they are lifted out of it, they are *no longer* understood mathematically *as boundary of the physical body*; rather through the θέσις they receive, over against the physical body, an *autonomy*. The geometrical objects are indeed not at a place; nonetheless at them I can determine above and below, right and left — at a square I can determine the sides — so I still have the determination-possibility of θέσις, of *analysis situs*, the lifting-out of the diversity of positions as such, although the geometrical objects themselves do not *have* these determinations in what they are. Every geometrical point, element, line, surface is fixed by θέσις; every geometrical object is a οὐσία θετός. The θέσις need be no *determination*, but it belongs to it. The μονάς, by contrast, does *not* carry this orientation in itself; it is οὐσία ἄθετος. In mathematics the θέσις maintains itself *only in geometry*, since geometry has a *greater nearness to the αἰσθητόν* than arithmetic.

##### γ) The connection-structure of the manifold in geometry and arithmetic: συνεχές and ἐφεξής

The geometrical objects retain a kinship with what is in αἰσθάνεσθαι: all that is in perceiving has μέγεθος, *extension*; hence it is οὐκ ἀδιαίρετον — τὸ αἰσθητὸν πᾶν ἐστι μέγεθος καὶ οὐκ ἔστιν ἀδιαίρετον αἰσθητόν (*De Sensu* VII; 449a20). This structure maintains itself in the geometrical, insofar as the geometrical is *continuous, συνεχές*; the point represents only the *last and outermost boundary* of this continuous. τὸ δὲ πάντῃ ἀδιαίρετον καὶ θέσιν ἔχον στιγμή (*Met.* V, 6; 1016b25): what is in every respect no longer resolvable, and has a θέσις, is the point; the γραμμή is μοναχῇ διαιρετόν (resolvable in one dimension), the ἐπίπεδον διχῇ (twofold), the σῶμα πάντῃ καὶ τριχῇ διαιρετόν (threefold). Characteristically, Aristotle wins the determination of *continuity* not within geometry but within *physics*, where he strikes upon the task of laying apart the primary phenomena of *being-with-one-another* of the φύσει ὄντα (*Phys.* V, 3).

**αα) The phenomena of the being-with-one-another of the φύσει ὄντα (*Phys.* V, 3).** Heidegger stresses these determinations must not be taken as self-evident and primitive; their fundamental worth is that Aristotle proceeded, *against any theoretical constructions*, from what one *at first sees*. (1) ἅμα, "together" — not temporal here but concerning *place*: together is what is *at one place*. (2) χωρίς, "separated" — what is at *another* place. (3) ἅπτεσθαι, "touching" — ὧν τὰ ἄκρα ἅμα (226a23), whose ends, tips, are at one place. (4) μεταξύ, the "between" — εἰς ὃ πέφυκε πρότερον ἀφικνεῖσθαι τὸ μεταβάλλον (226b23), into which what turns over comes, by its being, *first, before* it has come to an ἔσχατον in continuous turning-over (for a ship moving in the river, the river is the μεταξύ, the *medium* within which the movement proceeds, distinguished by *letting-out least*, hanging together in itself). (5) ἐφεξής, the "following-upon" — in such connection with what it follows that there is *no between* of the same γένος (τῶν ἐν ταὐτῷ γένει, 227a1): the houses of a street are ἐφεξής; what lies between them is not of the same being-character as what makes up the row. (6) ἐχόμενον, "what holds itself" — an ἐφεξής determined by ἅπτεσθαι: ἐχόμενον δὲ ὃ ἂν ἐφεξῆς ὂν ἅπτηται (227a6), a succession whose ends are at one place, the objects abutting, touching in their outermost boundaries. (7) συνεχές, *continuum* — a very complicated formation, since it *presupposes* the others though for αἴσθησις it is primarily given. τὸ συνεχὲς ἔστι ὅπερ ἐχόμενόν τι (227a10): the συνεχές is *already, quite certainly, an ἐχόμενον* — so there is here *no between*; it is *more originally* an ἐχόμενον than the ἐχόμενον itself, because it is still more as regards the manner of its ἔχειν: it is a συνεχόμενον — λέγω δ' εἶναι συνεχὲς ὅταν ταὐτὸ γένηται καὶ ἓν τὸ ἑκατέρου πέρας οἷς ἅπτονται (227a11): it is συνεχές when the boundary of each of the touching becomes *the same and one* — not merely that the boundary of one house abuts the other, but that the boundary of the one is *identical* with the other's: ταὐτὸ καὶ ἕν. The συνεχές is the principal structure of μέγεθος, distinguishing every extension. Of these, the *ἐφεξής* has a priority: φανερὸν δὲ καὶ ὅτι πρῶτον τὸ ἐφεξής ἐστιν (227a17), it is first *as regards constitution*, ἐν προτέροις τῷ λόγῳ, everywhere already co-meant and unspokenly said in addressing — for τὸ μὲν ἁπτόμενον ἐφεξῆς ἀνάγκη εἶναι, τὸ δ' ἐφεξὲς οὐ πᾶν ἅπτεσθαι (227a18): every touching is necessarily a succession, but not every succession a touching. Hence ἐφεξής is *the first*.
