We had come to recognize φρόνησις as the mode of uncovering a determinate being that has the character of *being-able-to-be-otherwise* — human Dasein. φρόνησις stands in a *twofold* possibility of pure uncovering, simple perceiving: (1) insofar as in φρόνησις there *simply shows itself, φαίνεται* (1144a34), the ἀγαθόν for which I resolve in the προαίρεσις; (2) insofar as in φρόνησις the *ἔσχατον* of the deliberation shows itself in an *αἴσθησις* — in the moment-of-vision (*Augenblick*) I survey the concrete situation of the action, out of which and for which I resolve. So φρόνησις, taken as a whole and above all seen in connection with βουλεύεσθαι, results as *that being-true which relates to Dasein itself*. One would suppose that, insofar as for the human his own being, his own existence, is the decisive thing, that being-true which relates to Dasein itself would be the highest — that φρόνησις would be the highest and decisive uncovering. *Yet Aristotle says*: σοφία, pure understanding, is — with respect to its ἀληθεύειν, and insofar as ἀληθεύειν is the being-mode of the human, with respect to the possibilities in which the human can exist — the *highest*.

If φρόνησις, though it goes to the being of the human, is nonetheless *not* the highest possibility of uncovering, this difficulty can lie only in this: that φρόνησις is *not fully self-standing*, but remains, in its structure, referred to *another* comportment of the human. And Aristotle shows exactly this: for φρόνησις the ἀγαθόν shows itself *only for such an existence as is itself already good, ἀγαθόν*. τοῦτο δ' εἰ μὴ τῷ ἀγαθῷ, οὐ φαίνεται (1144a34): "the ἀγαθόν does not show itself except to the ἀγαθός." διαστρέφει γὰρ ἡ μοχθηρία καὶ διαψεύδεσθαι ποιεῖ περὶ τὰς πρακτικὰς ἀρχάς (1144a34): bad disposition, and bad constitution generally, brings a Dasein to the point that the ἀγαθόν *gives itself out as something it is not*. ὥστε φανερὸν ὅτι ἀδύνατον φρόνιμον εἶναι μὴ ὄντα ἀγαθόν (1144a36): so one can be φρόνιμος only if he is *already ἀγαθός*. The possibility of φρόνησις' ἀληθεύειν is *bound* to this: that the one who enacts it is, in his being, from himself already ἀγαθός. Here shows itself, from the *other side*, a peculiar belonging of φρόνησις to πρᾶξις. Not only, as we saw at the outset, does a certain orientation and guidance belong to πρᾶξις — not only is πρᾶξις led by the circumspection, the sight of φρόνησις — but it shows that *this sight, the anticipation of the ἀγαθόν, is as enactment-mode of uncovering possible only in an ἀγαθός himself*. φρόνησις is nothing insofar as it is not enacted in the πρᾶξις that is as such determined by ἀρετή, by the πρακτόν as ἀγαθόν. By the mere *having* of the τέλος of an action, by mere disposing over φρόνησις, we are not yet πρακτικώτεροι; we are not thereby brought to act ethically better, insofar as we are not already good: εἴπερ ἡ μὲν φρόνησίς ἐστιν περὶ τὰ δίκαια καὶ καλὰ καὶ ἀγαθὰ ἀνθρώπῳ, ταῦτα δ' ἐστὶν ἃ τοῦ ἀγαθοῦ ἐστιν ἀνδρὸς πράττειν, οὐθὲν δὲ πρακτικώτεροι τῷ εἰδέναι αὐτά ἐσμεν, εἴπερ ἕξεις αἱ ἀρεταί εἰσιν (1143b21). The mere self-standing ἀληθεύειν of φρόνησις contributes nothing to the action itself, unless this φρόνησις is enacted by an ἀγαθός himself. Just as οὐθὲν πρακτικώτεροι τῷ ἔχειν τὴν ἰατρικήν ἐσμεν (1143b26): just as little as we become more fit for acting, for intervening, by *mastering the ἰατρική*, possessing the healing art purely theoretically, if we have not really learned to deal with it, if we are not really *physicians*. By the mere having of orientation and guidance we are not at the *level of being* that properly corresponds to the sense of ἀληθεύειν. Insofar as φρόνησις, as regards the possibility of its right enactment, remains dependent on being enacted by an ἀγαθός, it is itself *not self-standing*. With this the priority of φρόνησις is *shaken*, though it relates to human Dasein.

On the other side the question still stands: how shall σοφία be the highest possibility, since it does not *occupy itself with the Dasein of the human*? ἡ μὲν γὰρ σοφία οὐθὲν θεωρεῖ ἐξ ὧν ἔσται εὐδαίμων ἄνθρωπος (οὐδεμιᾶς γάρ ἐστιν γενέσεως) (1143b18). σοφία is indeed self-standing, but what stands in its theme is the ἀεί — what has nothing to do with γένεσις — while human Dasein has its being precisely in *being γένεσις, πρᾶξις, κίνησις*. The pure understanding of the philosopher does *not* take into view whence the human could properly *come into being*. What is regarded in philosophy contributes, by its sense, *nothing further for the existence of the human*. From this sentence alone one sees that Aristotle is as far as possible from a *religious worldview* or the like. So the following difficulty results: (1) φρόνησις goes to the Dasein of the human, but since it remains dependent on the being of the human as ἀγαθός, it is *not self-standing*; (2) σοφία is self-standing, insofar as it goes purely to the ἀρχαί, but since it goes to the ἀεί, it *contributes nothing for the Dasein of the human*. At bottom the difficulty lies in this: that *both*, φρόνησις and σοφία, are *no [mere] ἕξεις* — neither is a settled possession one simply has; each is a way Dasein *is*.

##### b) The criteria of the decision. The rank of ἀληθεύειν as such. The self-standingness of the "accomplishment" (ποιεῖν); σοφία as ὑγίεια of the ψυχή. The ontological priority according to the Greek concept of being

*[The editor notes that the exposition from here to the σοφία/ὑγίεια comparison was delivered freely by Heidegger; only sparse notes survive in the manuscript, and the reconstruction rests on the transcripts of Jonas, Schalk, and Weiß.]* For understanding this important decision one must keep in view that Aristotle *casts the whole question back onto a purely ontological regard*. πρῶτον μὲν οὖν λέγομεν ὅτι καθ' αὑτὰς ἀναγκαῖον αἱρετὰς αὐτὰς εἶναι, ἀρετάς γ' οὔσας ἑκατέραν ἑκατέρου τοῦ μορίου, καὶ εἰ μὴ ποιοῦσι μηδὲν μηδετέρα αὐτῶν (1144a1). First: the question which of the two modes is the more decisive is *inadequate* so long as one does not regard these being-modes *as being-modes*. So long as one asks, at the ἀρετή, what it *contributes and profits* (ποιεῖ), one is not yet at the adequate question. The adequate question is whether the *being-mode of ἀληθεύειν* is higher or lower. Even if neither of the two could accomplish anything, one must ask after their proper *ἀρετή-character*. For ἀρετή is itself something like a *τελείωσις*; it is what brings a being *at itself* into the *properness of its being*. With this Aristotle places the whole discussion into a *purely theoretical regard*.

ἔπειτα καὶ ποιοῦσι μέν (1144a3). But then the same regard of the being *at itself* sees that φρόνησις and σοφία *do* in fact accomplish something, ποιεῖν — where ποιεῖν means: to *produce, contribute, bring to being*. And precisely this ποιεῖν of φρόνησις and σοφία gives, more closely seen, the ground for delimiting σοφία and setting it *higher* than φρόνησις. At *this* ποιεῖν the priority of σοφία will decide itself *ontologically*. For the principle is: ἡ γὰρ ποιοῦσα ἄρχει καὶ ἐπιτάττει περὶ ἕκαστον (1143b35). "That possibility of human Dasein which *in itself* ποιεῖ, accomplishes something — which accomplishes *more properly* than another — *rules and leads* all the rest." Accordingly, if this principle is to apply here, we must attend to discovering in σοφία, despite all that has been laid out about it, *still a ποίησις*. And Aristotle says: the pure regarding of the philosopher does in fact contribute something, ποιεῖ — namely τῷ ἔχεσθαι καὶ τῷ ἐνεργεῖν (1144a6), "by being *had* and being *enacted*" — thus not through *results*, but merely in that *I live in this θεωρεῖν*. This uncovering accomplishes, *as such*, something.

Aristotle draws a comparison one understands only after securing its ground. He compares the theoretical regarding of philosophy with *health*: καὶ ποιοῦσι μέν, οὐχ ὡς ἡ ἰατρικὴ δὲ ὑγίειαν, ἀλλ' ὡς ἡ ὑγίεια, οὕτως ἡ σοφία εὐδαιμονίαν (1144a3):

> ὑγίεια — σοφία
> ἰατρική — φρόνησις

To understand the ground of the comparison, one must lay down a human who *is* a physician. If a physician who is *sick* heals himself on the ground of the knowledge he possesses *as physician*, that is a peculiar way of caring for one's own Dasein *from oneself*, of making one's own Dasein healthy again. But a *higher* mode of being-healthy is *health itself*. The healthy human does not *need* the knowing-one's-way-in-healing at all, in order to be healthy; he is healthy *without the detour* — i.e., he *is* what he is. Being-healthy is itself a *being-mode* that holds the human in the properness of his bodily being. So too with φρόνησις and σοφία. φρόνησις leads and guides every human acting, but is *dependent* on something else — the action itself. The θεωρεῖν of σοφία, by contrast, does *not*, like the ἰατρική, still have a *further purpose*, but is enacted *purely as such* by the human who lives in it. θεωρεῖν is a *being-mode in which the human has his highest being-mode: his proper spiritual being-healthy*.

But a *gap* still remains in understanding σοφία's priority. That σοφία accomplishes, as it were, *immediately*, merely by being there, while φρόνησις accomplishes with a view to something other than itself — this is structurally clear. But it is still not intelligible *how far σοφία can be compared with the being-healthy of the human*, i.e. how far the comportment that is the *uncovering of the always-being* makes up the *proper being of the human*. This we understand *only from the sense of the Greek concept of being*. Precisely because the being to which σοφία relates is *always-being*, and σοφία is the *purest mode of comporting-oneself-toward, of tarrying-alongside* this being, therefore σοφία, as the *genuine being-positioned toward this highest being*, is the *highest possibility*. The decision of σοφία's priority is thus ultimately *taken from the being itself to which it relates*. (ἐπιστήμη is here excluded, since it *cannot disclose the ἀρχαί* but presupposes them. The constant tarrying-alongside the always-being is accomplished by *pure νοεῖν*, which Aristotle also compares with αἴσθησις.)

So we win a fore-glimpse into the *basic conception of human Dasein* that was guiding for Aristotle: **human Dasein is *proper* (eigentlich) when it always is as, in the highest sense, it *can* be — when it tarries, in the highest measure, as long as possible and *always*, in the pure regarding of the always-being.** Insofar, however, as the human is *mortal*, insofar as he needs recreation and relaxation in the widest sense, the *constant tarrying-alongside the always-being* — the ultimately adequate comportment to the always-being — is *denied* him. This is the tragic structure the decision leaves standing: the highest possibility of human existence is one the human, *as mortal*, cannot sustain; he can only approximate the divine θεωρεῖν, never abide in it.

We close this regard of σοφία by making the same phenomenon present *from the opposite side*. If σοφία is the highest mode of ἀληθεύειν, but is on the other hand a *ἕξις τῆς ψυχῆς*, i.e. of the being of the human, then the question arises how far in σοφία lies the possibility of the *εὐδαιμονία* of the human. It is a matter of grasping σοφία and its ἀληθεύειν *as being-mode of human Dasein*. Since for Aristotle σοφία is the highest possibility of human Dasein, he must see in it too the *εὐδαιμονία* — and this is the theme *Nic. Eth.* X takes up.

#### §25. σοφία's priority in view of εὐδαιμονία (*Nic. Eth.* X, 6–7)

##### a) The idea of εὐδαιμονία (*Nic. Eth.* X, 6). The ontological sense of εὐδαιμονία as the completed being of the ψυχή

Aristotle grasps εὐδαιμονία *strictly ontologically*, as *τέλος*. This ontological sense is to be held fast: λοιπὸν περὶ εὐδαιμονίας τύπῳ διελθεῖν, ἐπειδὴ τέλος αὐτὴν τίθεμεν τῶν ἀνθρωπίνων (1176a31). "Of what concerns the human's being, we set down as that which makes up its *being-finished* the εὐδαιμονία." It makes up the *properness of the being* of human Dasein — which says nothing other than: *being-present, pure being-present alongside what always is*. But now εὐδαιμονία, insofar as it makes up the being *completely*, cannot be a mere *ἕξις*, a mere possibility one disposes over but lacks the occasion to actualize — for then it could belong even to one who *sleeps his whole life away*, who lives the life of a plant. It cannot be a mere property that now wakes and now sleeps. Rather εὐδαιμονία, insofar as it concerns the being of the human *as a being-finished, as the being-proper of the highest being-possibilities*, must be a being of the human that *every moment, constantly, is what it is*. It concerns not a mere being-able-to-be but this being-able-to-be *in its presence, ἐνέργεια*: μᾶλλον εἰς ἐνέργειάν τινα θετέον (1176b1). Accordingly εὐδαιμονία, as the proper being, must be set into *ἐνέργεια* — and ἐνέργεια says nothing other than *presence, pure immediate presence-at-hand*. τῶν δ' ἐνεργειῶν αἱ μέν εἰσιν ἀναγκαῖαι καὶ δι' ἕτερα αἱρεταὶ αἱ δὲ καθ' αὑτάς (1176b2): of the ἐνέργειαι, some are δι' ἕτερα, oriented toward another; others are καθ' αὑτάς αἱρεταί, graspable *at themselves* — καθ' αὑτὰς δ' εἰσὶν αἱρεταὶ ἀφ' ὧν μηδὲν ἐπιζητεῖται παρὰ τὴν ἐνέργειαν (1176b6): those modes of ἐνέργεια, of the pure being-present of the living, from which *nothing further is sought beside the being-present simply*. Insofar as εὐδαιμονία is the τέλος, it cannot be an ἐνέργεια oriented on another (δι' ἕτερα), but only one graspable *at itself* (καθ' αὑτήν). So εὐδαιμονία is in itself *completed and self-sufficient, αὐτάρκης*: οὐδενὸς γὰρ ἐνδεὴς ἡ εὐδαιμονία ἀλλ' αὐτάρκης (1176b5) — what makes up εὐδαιμονία is *οὐκ ἐνδεής*, needs nothing other.

In human Dasein there are various possibilities of actual acting, related among themselves and graded. εὐδαιμονία as τέλος simply is, in the purest sense, *self-standing being-present of the living in the world*. It is the *pure presence of the living with respect to its brought-to-end being-possibility*: ψυχῆς ἐνέργειά τις κατ' ἀρετὴν τελείαν (1102a5). Herein lies an *intensification* of the τέλος-character: κατ' ἀρετὴν τελείαν means properly κατὰ τελείωσιν τελείαν — for in the expression ἀρετή already lies the determination of τελείωσις. εὐδαιμονία is thus the *finished-being-present of the living with respect to its highest being-possibility*. It is the *τελείωσις of the being of the being as In-being* (*In-Sein*). From this idea Aristotle determines, in *Nic. Eth.* X, 7, the structure of εὐδαιμονία more concretely, by seven viewpoints.

##### b) The structural moments of εὐδαιμονία and their fulfillment through the θεωρεῖν of σοφία (νοῦς) (*Nic. Eth.* X, 7)

What brings Dasein into the properness of its being must be:

(1) the *κρατίστη ἕξις* (1177a13), the being-mode in which the human *most properly disposes over what he can be*. This highest being-determination is *νοῦς*.

(2) This highest being-determination *in us, ἐν ἡμῖν* — νοῦς, the pure being-able-to-perceive the being as such — is related to the γνωστά, with which I become familiar in pure regarding; and specifically it relates to a being that is itself *κράτιστον*, to the *always-being*: καὶ γὰρ ὁ νοῦς <τὸ κράτιστον> τῶν ἐν ἡμῖν, καὶ <τὰ κράτιστα> τῶν γνωστῶν, περὶ ἃ ὁ νοῦς (1177a20). The highest in us relates to the highest of the knowable.

(3) This being-mode is *συνεχεστάτη* (1177a21), what *most holds together in itself*, is more uninterrupted than all else: θεωρεῖν τε γὰρ δυνάμεθα συνεχῶς μᾶλλον ἢ πράττειν ὁτιοῦν (1177a21). We are able, on the ground of our human being, to live *more uninterruptedly* in the mode of pure regarding than in that of acting. For the action is, by its sense, *in each case another* according to circumstances, time, humans; the steadiness of acting across a determinate life-connection is constantly *interrupted* by the new settings-in that every resolve demands. Pure regarding, by contrast, is in itself an *even, uninterrupted abiding* that by its sense *cannot be otherwise* — for it is a *tarrying-alongside the being that in itself cannot be otherwise*. While the being of πρᾶξις can in each case be otherwise and in each case demands a resolve in the moment-of-vision, the pure regarding of the always-being *abides, as it were, in a lasting now*. This third moment, the συνεχέστατον, is ascribed to the comportment we know as the θεωρεῖν of σοφία.

(4) This θεωρεῖν of σοφία is the ἐνέργεια that is *ἡδίστη* (1177a22), most pleasant. We hold that to the proper being of the human a corresponding *finding-oneself*, a state-of-mind (*Befindlichkeit*) — namely *ἡδονή*, the being-glad — is admixed: οἰόμεθά τε δεῖν ἡδονὴν παραμεμῖχθαι τῇ εὐδαιμονίᾳ (1177a22). Generally, constitutive for the being of a living thing is the *so-and-so-being-attuned* (*Gestimmtsein*) in reference to that with which and for which the living thing is; this basic constitution belonging to life may not be absent at the highest being-level. ἡδίστη δὲ τῶν κατ' ἀρετὴν ἐνεργειῶν ἡ κατὰ τὴν σοφίαν ὁμολογουμένως ἐστίν (1177a23): agreed, to the being-present-alongside the being κατὰ τὴν σοφίαν, to pure regarding, corresponds also the *purest gladness*. This pure tarrying-alongside is in itself the purest *being-attuned* in the widest sense; and the purity and firmness of this attunement is intelligible only from its *theme*, the always-being — which has no possibility of bringing with it a disturbance, alteration, or confusion of the human's comportment as researcher, of destroying his mood from the root. The human, insofar as he *stays by the matter*, stays in the same mood. Therefore in the tarrying-alongside-the-always-being is given the possibility of *διαγωγή*, of pure lingering, which no longer has the *unrest of seeking* about it. Seeking is, for the Greeks, the uncovering of the concealed, the *λανθάνον*; seeking is the *not-yet-being-before-the-unconcealed*, whereas the pure lingering of being-knowing, of seeing, of having-in-view, is the tarrying-alongside the being *in its unconcealment*. Therefore Aristotle can say of the ancients, insofar as they were genuine philosophers: φιλοσοφήσαντες περὶ τῆς ἀληθείας (*Met.* I, 3; 983b2) — "they philosophized *about the truth*," which does *not* mean they philosophized about the *concept* of truth, but: they were *friends*, had *resolved* for the pure uncovering of being in its unconcealment.

(5) The fifth moment is *αὐτάρκεια*, the comportment of the human that is *set upon itself*: ἡ τε λεγομένη αὐτάρκεια περὶ τὴν θεωρητικὴν μάλιστ' ἂν εἴη (1177a27). Aristotle stresses: τῶν μὲν πρὸς τὸ ζῆν ἀναγκαίων καὶ σοφὸς καὶ δίκαιος καὶ οἱ λοιποὶ δέονται (1177a28) — the philosopher, exactly like every other human, is *dependent* on the urgencies necessary for living; he cannot free himself from them. But beyond that: ὁ μὲν δίκαιος δεῖται πρὸς οὓς δικαιοπραγήσει καὶ μεθ' ὧν (1177a30), "the one who as judge wills to act justly *needs the humans* against and with whom he can act justly." So too the σώφρων (the temperate) and the ἀνδρεῖος (the brave). Not only these, but *all* being-possibilities of the πρᾶξις of the pre-philosophical human are, by their sense, *dependent on being-with-one-another*. Therefore they cannot be the *proper* being-possibilities of the human — and this although each is an *ἀγαθὸν καθ' αὑτὸ αἱρετόν*. For here it is a matter precisely of the *proper being-present, presence of life*, of the radically-ontologically grasped *properness of being* that is itself the ontological basis of the human's factical, concrete existence. While the being-possibilities of πρᾶξις are dependent on being-with-one-another, the pure regarding of the always-being is *free of this bond*: ὁ δὲ σοφὸς καὶ καθ' αὑτὸν ὢν δύναται θεωρεῖν, καὶ ὅσῳ ἂν σοφώτερος ᾖ, μᾶλλον (1177a32). The philosopher, to whom it comes purely and only on understanding, on the uncovering of the being, can be who he is *even when, and precisely when, he is καθ' αὑτὸν ὤν, only alongside himself*. And the more he is alongside himself and strives only toward uncovering, the *less* he needs others. βέλτιον δ' ἴσως συνεργοὺς ἔχων, ἀλλ' ὅμως αὐταρκέστατος (1177a34): perhaps it is still *better* if he has *co-strivers*, such as are with him at the work and abide with him in this bearing. But even then he is only what he is when he in each case *sees the matters from himself*, as they are. No one can see the matters *for another*, uncover them and so have-them-there. Pure seeing is the affair of the *single one* — although precisely he who sees for himself, when he sees the *same* as the others, *is with the others*, in the mode of *συμφιλοσοφεῖν*, philosophizing-with-one-another.

(6) So this being-mode of pure regarding is the *only* one that can be loved *for its own sake*: δόξαι τ' ἂν αὐτὴ μόνη δι' αὑτὴν ἀγαπᾶσθαι· οὐδὲν γὰρ ἀπ' αὐτῆς γίνεται παρὰ τὸ θεωρῆσαι, ἀπὸ δὲ τῶν πρακτικῶν ἢ πλεῖον ἢ ἔλαττον περιποιούμεθα παρὰ τὴν πρᾶξιν (1177b1). For in this being-mode of pure regarding we *bring about nothing further*, look around for nothing further, as we do in πρᾶξις, where it always comes to something else. So this being-mode is marked by *ἐν τῇ σχολῇ ἐστιν* (1177b4), "is *in leisure*" — i.e. in pure lingering, in genuine being-present-alongside.

(7) This kind of human Dasein is a *genuine* one only when it λαβοῦσα μῆκος βίου τέλειον (1177b24): ἡ τελεία δὴ εὐδαιμονία αὕτη ἂν εἴη ἀνθρώπου, λαβοῦσα μῆκος βίου τέλειον (1177b24). It is a genuine one only "when it has grasped a *completed length of life*," when it in fact stretches over the *whole duration of human existence*, when this comportment determines human existence not merely at times but *holds through as the proper one*. For the always-being, which is the theme of this comportment, *constantly pre-sketches itself*, so that the *presence* of Dasein alongside it is determined as a *constant, self-maintaining* one. Herein lies the *peculiar tendency of the measuring-up (Anmessung) of human Dasein, as regards its being-temporal, to the always-being of the world*. This tarrying-alongside-the-always-being, θεωρεῖν, shall not be arbitrary, at-times, but shall *hold through uninterruptedly through the duration of life*. Herein consists, for the human, a certain possibility of *ἀθανατίζειν* (1177b33) — a being-mode of the human in which he has the *highest possibility of not coming to an end*. That is the *outermost position onto which human Dasein was borne by the Greeks*.

Only from here — from the wholly determinate and clear *predominance of the sense of being as always-being* — does σοφία's priority become intelligible. Now it becomes clear *why* the pure regarding contributes something for the human's existence, why in the Greek sense it is the highest. Understanding the last sense of human existence among the Greeks hangs on our seeing how the regard of the ethical lies *from the outset outside* the viewpoints we today know from traditional philosophies. For the Greeks, the regard of human existence is *purely oriented at the sense of being itself* — at how far human Dasein has the possibility of *being always*. This sense of being — being as *absolute being-present* — is *read off the being of the world* by the Greeks. One cannot therefore force the question-posing of Greek ethics into the modern alternative whether the Greeks had an *ethics of success* or an *ethics of disposition* (*Gesinnung*). Dasein is here seen *solely with respect to its being-possibility as such*, wherein neither disposition nor practical success plays a role. To this grasp of the human's being corresponds also the expression *ἦθος* — bearing, proper being. Holding fast this viewpoint of the primarily *ontological* question, one understands the peculiar fact that σοφία can be compared with *ὑγίεια*, being-healthy. From this idea of the human's being the sense of εὐδαιμονία is from the outset determined, which Aristotle defines as *ψυχῆς ἐνέργεια κατ' ἀρετὴν τελείαν*. The ψυχή is the proper of a being that lives; this living being is in εὐδαιμονία insofar as it is *simply present with respect to the being-possibility that is in it the highest*. This highest being-possibility of a living thing called human is *νοῦς*. The νοεῖν as *ἐνέργεια θεωρητική* most satisfies the ἐνέργεια of the living, its pure being-present simply. Insofar, νοεῖν *most properly satisfies εὐδαιμονία*. Therein consists human life in the properness of its being — a properness grasped *radically ontologically*, such that as such it is the *ontological condition of the factical, concrete existence of the human*.

With this the entire priority-question is settled, and settled from a single root: the Greek understanding of being as constant presence. The seven moments of εὐδαιμονία — highest ἕξις, relation to the κράτιστον, uninterruptedness, purest gladness, self-sufficiency, being-lovable-for-its-own-sake, and completed length of life — all measure human existence by its capacity to *approximate the always-being*, culminating in the ἀθανατίζειν, the mortal's highest possibility of not-coming-to-an-end. It remains only to clarify how νοῦς stands to λόγος — the question that turns the whole Introductory Part toward its final theme and toward Plato.

#### §26. The reach and limit of λόγος

##### a) λόγος and νοῦς. νοεῖν and διανοεῖν. The grasping of the πρῶτα and ἔσχατα through νοεῖν

νοῦς is the highest determination of the human, so that it must even be grasped as *the divine*; life in νοῦς is a *θεῖον* (1177b30). *However*, human comportment moves for the most part, and above all *at first*, *not* in pure νοεῖν but in *διανοεῖν*. Because the being of the human is determined as ζῷον λόγον ἔχον — because the human *speaks*, discusses the things he sees — the pure perceiving is *always a through-speaking*. Pure νοεῖν is enacted as *θιγεῖν* (touching, immediate contact: *Met.* IX, 10; 1051b24). But the νοεῖν enacted *within* a being that *has λόγος* is a *διανοεῖν*. So there is a *διαφορά* between the pure νοῦς and the *νοῦς σύνθετος* (composite νοῦς): the νοῦς of the human is always enacted in the mode of *speaking*. The νοῦς of the human is *not the proper one*, but the *ὁ καλούμενος νοῦς*, the "so-called" νοῦς (*De An.* III, 9; 432b27). It must be held fast that λόγος belongs to the being of the human and that with it, at first and for the most part, the *meaning* is enacted: it is *νοεῖν μετὰ λόγου*. So it is justified that Aristotle characterizes the modes of ἀληθεύειν discussed above — ἐπιστήμη, τέχνη, φρόνησις, σοφία — as *ἕξεις μετὰ λόγου* (VI, 6; 1140b31). The through-regarding, διανοεῖν, is a *speaking, λέγειν*. But this meaning, insofar as the *ἀρχή* is to be grasped, must *leave λόγος behind itself*, must be *ἄνευ λόγου*, in order to have the possibility of grasping an *ἀδιαίρετον*. The character of λέγειν is precisely to *address something as something*; but what is *simply simple, ἁπλοῦν*, can no longer be addressed *as something other*. All ἔσχατον and all πρῶτον can be *properly* grasped only when the νοεῖν is *no διανοεῖν but pure looking-toward*. The uncovering in the enactment-mode of λόγος *fails here and steps back*.

That λόγος *can* here step back is grounded *in λόγος itself*. For λόγος *as* λόγος is, by its sense, *not already set on uncovering beings, ἀληθεύειν, on being true*. Speaking as such does *not* have primarily the sense of *ἀποφαίνεσθαι*, of letting the being be seen; only a *quite determinate* λόγος is *λόγος ἀποφαντικός*. This fundamental fact is to be kept in view in order to understand what we have fundamentally to make out about the *concept of truth* among the Greeks.

##### b) λόγος and ἀλήθεια

**α) λόγος σημαντικός (speech) and λόγος ἀποφαντικός ("judgment") (*De Int.* 4; *De An.* II, 8).** It does *not* belong to λόγος as such to be true, to uncover beings. *Not every* λόγος is ἀποφαντικός. But every λόγος *is σημαντικός*. Aristotle treats this in *De Interpretatione* 4: ἔστι δὲ λόγος ἅπας μὲν σημαντικός, […] ἀποφαντικὸς δὲ οὐ πᾶς, ἀλλ' ἐν ᾧ τὸ ἀληθεύειν ἢ ψεύδεσθαι ὑπάρχει (16b33). Every speech is, as speech, σημαντική; σημαίνειν means: to *signify*; every speech thus *signifies* something, is understandable — every speech has in itself an *ἑρμηνεία*, an understandability. But to signify something in this way *and at once, in this signifying, to let the signified matter be seen, ἀποφαίνεσθαι* — that is *not* the case with every speech. A speech that by its sense is σημαντική becomes ἀποφαντική only in that in it is present the *uncovering, ἀληθεύειν*, or the *distorting, ψεύδεσθαι*. For not only the uncovering but also the *distorting* is a letting-see — though the uncovering is the *proper* letting-see. So in *not every* speech is the ἀληθεύειν or ψεύδεσθαι present; therefore speech is, by its sense, *at first neither true nor false*: οὐκ ἐν ἅπασι δὲ ὑπάρχει, οἷον ἡ εὐχὴ λόγος μέν, ἀλλ' οὔτ' ἀληθὴς οὔτε ψευδής (17a3). A *request*, e.g., is neither true nor false — understood in the Greek sense: a request has, as request, *not* the sense of letting-something-be-seen. Aristotle points out that the manifold of speeches that are understandable, that communicate and orient, but *let nothing be seen*, belong to *rhetoric and poetics*: ῥητορικῆς γὰρ ἢ ποιητικῆς οἰκειοτέρα ἡ σκέψις, ὁ δὲ ἀποφαντικὸς τῆς νῦν θεωρίας (17a5). The λόγος ἀποφαντικός, by contrast, is the object of the present investigation.

**β) The rejection of λόγος as the proper site of truth. νοεῖν as ἀληθεύειν without λόγος. The λόγος ἀποφαντικός as the site of ψεῦδος. The synthetic structure of the λόγος ἀποφαντικός as the condition of ψεῦδος.** Traditional logic, precisely with appeal to this analysis, let itself be seduced into a *ground-misunderstanding*: that Aristotle had said the *proper bearer of truth is the judgment*. And when on closer study one met investigations where a being-true is spoken of and yet *not* of the judgment, one said Aristotle was, as regards his concept of truth, *in contradiction with himself*. Against this: Aristotle at first does not speak of the *judgment* at all, but of *speech*, and speech is *aufzeigend, ἀποφαντικός*, only when in it the *ἀληθεύειν occurs*. Speech is *not the primary and sole bearer* of the ἀληθές; it is something *in which* the ἀληθές *can* occur but *need not* occur. **λόγος is not the site in which ἀληθεύειν is at home, native, ground-standing.**

The λόγος, insofar as it has the structure of ἀποφαίνεσθαι, of "something as something," is so little the site of truth that it is rather, conversely, the *proper condition of the possibility that there is falsehood*. Because this λόγος is such an aufzeigen that it lets what it speaks about be seen *as* something, there is the possibility that through the "as" it is *distorted*, that there is *deception*. Something can be distorted only when it is grasped *from another*; only where the ἀληθεύειν is enacted in the mode of the *as-something*, only where the *As* is structurally present, can it happen that something is *given out as something it is not*. In the *simple* uncovering — in αἴσθησις as in νοεῖν — there is *no λέγειν* any longer, no addressing of something as something; therefore there is here *also no deception*.

Aristotle determines the structure in which λόγος is uncovering: taking the κατάφασις, "this is that" — at the setting-in of the speaking there is given, *unarticulated*, the *whole*. In the κατάφασις, insofar as it is a λέγειν τι κατά τινος, lies that the καθ' οὗ λέγεταί τι, that in reference to which something is said, is *in advance already there, in advance already unarticulatedly objective*. The λόγος — e.g. "the board is black" — is enacted such that I have *in advance in view the unarticulated whole*: black-board, an *ὄν*. If this is now to be uncovered *as such*, if a speaking about it is to let it *expressly* be seen, this is enacted in the *as-saying*. And this as-saying is enacted so: I have in view the *whole board* and *articulate* what is so seen: board — black; the *νοήματα*, the perceived (board, black) are *lifted out* and the one *ascribed to the other*: the board *as* black. In this λόγος lies a *σύνθεσις* of the νοήματα, a certain *co-positing* of the meant: σύνθεσίς τις ἤδη νοημάτων ὥσπερ ἓν ὄντων (*De An.* III, 6; 430a27). I posit the one *with* the other, "as if they were One." I posit board with black, so that they are seen *as One* — for this One I already have in advance in view. The speaking about it first makes the seen *properly visible*: the board expressly *as black*. The pre-given is *lifted out* in the As, such that precisely in passing *through* the breaking-open articulation it is understood and seen *as One*. So the grasping in the sense of the letting-see through λόγος has the structure of *σύνθεσις*. And only where there is such a σύνθεσις, only where the character of the As occurs, is there falsehood. The distorting of something is possible only so, that *another* (grey) — which supposedly and presumably could point up the being (board) — is *placed before* it. τὸ γὰρ ψεῦδος ἐν συνθέσει ἀεί· καὶ γὰρ ἂν τὸ λευκὸν μὴ λευκόν, τὸ μὴ λευκὸν συνέθηκεν (430b1): "the deceptive is always only where a σύνθεσις is; for even when I address the white *as non-white*, the non-white is *co-posited*." One might suppose that in the μή lies a *separation*; but in addressing the λευκόν *as* μὴ λευκόν lies precisely a *σύνθεσις*. Even the giving-out of something *as* something it is not structurally includes a *σύν* in itself, the co-meaning of the one νόημα with the other, *as ἕν*.

**γ) Critique of the traditional doctrine of judgment. σύνθεσις and διαίρεσις as the basic structures of the λόγος ἀποφαντικός in general.** One says: Aristotle divides judgments into positive and negative, κατάφασις and ἀπόφασις; the ascribing is the *joining* of two representations, σύνθεσις, the denying the *separating*, διαίρεσις. One takes joining and separating of representations as the structure of the positive and negative judgment. **This is a complete inversion of what Aristotle says in measuring-up to the phenomena.** Both κατάφασις and ἀπόφασις have the character of *σύνθεσις*, and both have the character of *διαίρεσις*. σύνθεσις and διαίρεσις are *originary structures* that lie, as *founding*, *before* the κατάφασις and ἀπόφασις. ἔτι πᾶν τὸ διανοητὸν καὶ νοητὸν ἡ διάνοια ἢ κατάφησιν ἢ ἀπόφησιν […] ὅταν μὲν ὡδὶ συνθῇ φᾶσα ἢ ἀποφᾶσα, ἀληθεύει, ὅταν δὲ ὡδί, ψεύδεται (*Met.* IV, 7; 1012a2): "everything that is theme of a through-meaning and meaning, the thinking means, perceives in the mode of ascribing or denying. When it, so ascribing or denying, posits the meant *in one* — i.e. posits and means as *νοῦς* — then the thinking is true, then it uncovers; when otherwise, then it is false, then it distorts." Here it becomes clear that κατάφασις and ἀπόφασις are *ordered into* the σύνθεσις. All ascribing and denying, whether true or false, is *in advance a σύνθεσις*.

But conversely, both ascribing and denying, κατάφασις and ἀπόφασις, are also *in advance a διαίρεσις*: ἐνδέχεται δὲ καὶ διαίρεσιν φάναι πάντα (430b5). The ascribing and denying is equally to be interpreted *as διαίρεσις*, taking-apart. The taking-apart is a mode of enactment of perceiving, of νοεῖν, i.e. of the holding-in-view of the ὄν, of the *whole*; it is a *safe-keeping letting-see of the whole*, a positing-of-the-one-with-the-other. **σύνθεσις and διαίρεσις make up the full enactment-mode of νοεῖν**, and this itself can, insofar as it is that of the λόγον ἔχον, be κατάφασις or ἀπόφασις. The essential in both enactment-forms of νοεῖν — σύνθεσις and διαίρεσις — is the *primary unitary holding-in-view of the ὑποκείμενον*, of that about which is spoken, of what is in question. In *σύνθεσις* comes to appearance the moment that the addressing sees the one *with* the other and so the *whole together*. In *διαίρεσις* lies that λόγος, because it lets something be seen *as* something, *takes apart* the in-advance-seen whole (black board → board – black) — but *not* so that the νοήματα are set side by side; rather ὥσπερ ἓν ὄντων (430a28), so that one sees them *as One*. One understands the whole doctrine of λόγος when one holds fast the basic structure of *ἀπόφανσις*, of letting-see and seeing; in this basic bearing the ascribing and denying is enacted.

**δ) The ἀληθές as the encountering-character of being (*Met.* VI, 2 and 4).** Being-true, uncovering, is a *being-mode of human life* and relates at first *to the world*. From this arises the problem: in what connection stands the being, insofar as it is *uncovered*, with the *other characters of being*? For, free of every epistemology and its prejudices, one sees that *unconcealment is in a certain way a character of the being of the being itself*. Therefore Aristotle speaks of an *ὄν ὡς ἀληθές*, of the being insofar as it is unconcealed, and correspondingly of a *μὴ ὄν ὡς ψεῦδος* — and this in connection with a fundamental establishing of ontological research through the distinction of the various *respects* in which being can be spoken of. These are (1) the ὄν of the *categories*; (2) the ὄν *κατὰ συμβεβηκός* (incidentally); (3) the ὄν *δυνάμει and ἐνεργείᾳ* (potentially and actually); (4) the ὄν *ὡς ἀληθές* (*Met.* VI, 2; 1026a33). Here the phenomenon of the ἀληθές appears in connection with the question of the *basic determinations of the being itself*. Nevertheless Aristotle says this ὄν ὡς ἀληθές does *not properly fall into the theme of ontology*, insofar as the character of the ἀληθές yields nothing of the being *that accrues to it as such*, but only insofar as *it is there, insofar as it encounters for an uncovering meaning* (*Met.* VI, 4; 1027b25).

But it is *wrong* to suppose that this ὄν ὡς ἀληθές says something like *being-true in the sense of the validity of judgment*, because Aristotle *excludes* the ὄν ὡς ἀληθές from ontological regard. Of that there is no talk. The ὄν ὡς ἀληθές is *not* a being that is *assumed in consequence of a merely factically occurring thought-process*. It is rather *the being of the same being of which the categories too are being-determinations*. Only it stands so with the categories, that they belong to the being itself *as being*, while the ἀληθές is a character of the being of the being only insofar as *it is there and present for a grasping*. So there is here no talk at all of a *logical* being, of the validity or invalidity of the judgment. The ὄν ὡς ἀληθές is rather *the same being that is also theme in ontology: the being of the world*. On closer inspection we will strike on the fact that Aristotle, in the last sense, *assigns even this character, this being, to ontological regard* (*Met.* XI, 10). The ὄν ὡς ἀληθές will show itself as *a character of being insofar as it encounters*.

With this we gain a *look-through* into the dimension of what being-true means for Aristotle. It will show: *being-true, unconcealment, is not at home in λόγος*. But if not in λόγος — then the positive question runs: *where then?* From here we win again the orientation for the *central question of the "Sophist,"* the question of the *being of the ψεῦδος* — whether there is such a thing as a *μὴ ὄν*, whether the non-being *is*. The regard of the problem of the ἀληθές will be carried only so far that we grasp the *setting-in of the "Sophist" from Aristotle*.

This closes the Introductory Part. Its whole labor has been to win, from the *clear* (Aristotle), the ground on which Plato's *obscure* (the *Sophist*) can be read. The decisive result — reached against the entire subsequent logical tradition — is that **λόγος is not the site of truth**. Truth (ἀλήθεια, unconcealment) is a being-mode of Dasein enacted first in the *simple* seeing of νοεῖν and αἴσθησις, which have no "as" and so admit no falsehood; the assertoric λόγος (λόγος ἀποφαντικός) is a *derivative* determinate form of the σημαντικός, and its very structure — the synthetic-dihairetic "something as something" — is what first makes *ψεῦδος*, distortion, possible at all. This is the direct source of *Being and Time* §33 (assertion as a derivative mode of interpretation) and §44 (the existential-ontological foundations of truth). And it hands the course its bridge to Plato: since falsehood is possible, the μὴ ὄν "is" in some manner; the *Sophist* must show *how* — and that showing will demand the revolution of the concept of being that the Transition now announces.

## TRANSITION: Fixing the thematic field, setting out from ἀληθεύειν

*[Continuation of the lecture after the Christmas break, 1924/25. From here the edition draws not only on Heidegger's manuscript notes and the transcripts of Jonas, Schalk, and Weiß, but also on the typed copy of S. Moser's stenographic transcript, which Heidegger reviewed and authorized with marginal remarks.]*

#### §27. What has so far been accomplished, and the further task. First indication of the theme: the revolution of the concept of being; the being of the non-being (= ψεῦδος)

What we have so far regarded had the sense of a *preparation* for understanding a Platonic *scientific* dialogue. Heidegger stresses "scientific": not all Platonic dialogues bear this height of scientific regard in themselves, though all in a certain way aim at cognition. And he restates the hermeneutic principle at its sharpest: *there is no scientific — i.e. historical — going-back to Plato without going through Aristotle*. Aristotle at first, as it were, *blocks every access to Plato*. This is self-evident once we recall that we always come *from the later* and, as latecomers, go *backward* to the earlier, and that in the field of fundamental philosophical regard there is *no arbitrariness*. The selection of a philosophy or a philosopher is *never arbitrary*. Whereas one may, from the most various motives and intellectual predilections, choose from history existence-possibilities, ideas, models — may rummage about in history at will — this does *not* hold for *philosophical* research, if this is to uncover Dasein in its fundaments, and if this Dasein, *we ourselves*, *are* history. Going through Aristotle, whether express or not, is at bottom self-evident, all the more since in Aristotelian research nothing else lies than a *more radical grasp* of the problems with which Plato and the earlier thinkers wrestled. A Plato-interpretation not only cannot leap over Aristotle; *every one must prove itself on him*. Per the hermeneutic principle we go from the clear into the obscure, from the relatively *unwrapped* to the *confused* (*verworren*) — "confused" not as a *disparaging valuation* but meaning that the various directions of seeing and questioning in Plato *still run through one another*, not on the ground of a subjective intellectual incapacity but on the ground of the *difficulty of the problems*. The confused, un-unwrapped can be understood only when *guiding threads* for its immanent tendencies are available — and these cannot be arbitrary philosophical questions. The *fundamental question* of Greek philosophical research is the *question of being, of the sense of being*, and — characteristically — the *question of truth*.

We are, in one direction, sufficiently prepared: through the regard of ἀληθεύειν we have appropriated the *basic position* out of which, in the dialogue, it is seen and questioned — the manner in which the steps of the dialogical transaction themselves run. *[Heidegger's marginal note: the first part of the lecture is an interpretation of Aristotle, Nic. Eth. VI.]* But not only the *manner of the researching regard* is to be fixed in this preparation; equally the *thematic field* of this regard itself. And this field bears, in the dialogue we take up first *[Heidegger's note: the Philebus was also planned]*, a *peculiar double character*.

In the *Sophist* it is asked and transacted *what the sophist is* — and this with a view to making out *what the philosopher is*. The sophist is at first made visible in the *manifold of his comportments*; and out of this manifold, and the corresponding interpretation, there becomes visible at once *that to which he comports himself*. The manner of sophistical speaking and dealing-with all matters lets at once become clear the *with-what* of this dealing. The comportment of the sophist is, taken in the widest sense, a *τέχνη*. Heidegger recalls that in Plato the expressions τέχνη, ἐπιστήμη, σοφία, φρόνησις still partly run through one another *[his note: cf. Theaetetus 207c, τεχνικός as ἐπιστήμων against the mere δοξαστικός]*. For Plato τέχνη has the breadth of meaning the expression still shows in Aristotle's *Metaphysics* I: knowing-one's-way in the widest sense in some comportment. Here, with the sophist, it is a matter of *knowing-one's-way in speaking about everything there is* — a knowing-one's-way in *discussing beings*. In the course of the further characterization the peculiar determination is added that this knowing-one's-way is a *deceiving* about that which is spoken of. The sophist gives out, in his speech, what he speaks about *as something which at bottom, on proper regard, it is not*, or is not as he shows it.

The manifold characteristic of the sophist — which springs to the eye at once on first reading, setting in again and again from wholly different sides — has the sense of bringing the *concrete Dasein of the sophist within Greek life* quite concretely near. But out of this *unavoidable factical Dasein* of the sophistical comportment — which within the Greek intellectual world was a distinguished *power* — out of this incontestably *powerful being* of the sophistical comportment, it becomes at once clear that *that to which he comports himself, that with which he as sophist has to do, is deception and fraud (Täuschung und Trug)*. But insofar as deception and fraud are something *which at bottom is not* — a being that gives out a *non-being* as being — out of the Dasein of the sophist himself the *being of the non-being* becomes clear. So it shows, out of the *concretion and facticity* of the sophistical being, out of the *existence of such a thing as a sophist* — for a regard standing on a higher level — that the *non-being — deception, fraud — is*.

This insight, that the non-being *is*, means at once a *revolution of the previous conception* — the sense of being *still represented by Plato himself*. The interpretation of the sophist's being-mode aims thus ultimately at the *demonstration of the being of the non-being*. This demonstration is nothing other than the *more radical grasp of the sense of being itself* and of the characters of the *Not* enclosed in it. But this means a *more originary appropriation of the theme* of philosophical research — not in the sense of a program, but in the course of the dialogue, in the manner of a *concrete working-through* of the question of being. With this more radical grasp and grounding of being-research is at once given a *more fundamental interpretation of this research itself*, i.e. of *philosophizing*. So, by way of the *matter-regard* of the theme of the being of the non-being, the regard *returns to a new proper existence — that of the philosopher*. Characteristic is that thereby *no determinate type of human* is treated, no *typology* of human kinds pursued, but *matter-research*, out of which the sense of the philosopher *springs of itself*, without Plato expressly speaking of it. **With the question of the sense of the sophistical existence, that of the philosopher is *indirectly* co-answered.**

Placing the weight of the question-posing on the *matter-question of the concept of being* and the *re-laying of the previous concept of being*, we are set before the task of appropriating the *position* of regard on which the *presence of the non-being* first becomes present and evident. It comes to *phenomenologically pointing up the states of affairs*. We will have to look: on *what way* does the being of the non-being become present and evident? Where and how is the presence of the non-being *no longer to be evaded*? And we will have to ask: what does this way *say*? How is enacted, in the face of the being of the non-being, the *re- and out-forming of the concept of being*? How was this concept won *hitherto*, in *Parmenides*? Whence does Plato come *into* his questioning after being? The theme of the regard is thus *the being in its being*; it is a matter of the *characters of the being insofar as it is*.

This being treated in the dialogue is theme of a *speaking-about-it* — and specifically such a speaking, *διαλέγεσθαι*, that in it the being becomes visible *as uncovered*. Therefore Plato always speaks of the *ὄν ἀληθινόν* — this is the being *as uncovered at itself*. About ἀληθεύειν, the manner of access to the uncovered-being, we are sufficiently oriented. *[Heidegger's marginal note: The first part of this lecture is an interpretation of Aristotle, Nic. Eth. VI.]* Among the possible modes of ἀληθεύειν we have come to know a *distinguished* one — the theme the Transition now develops as it turns to Plato's dialectic (διαλέγεσθαι) as itself a mode of ἀληθεύειν, the "justified preliminary stage" of philosophizing announced at the close of §23.

Among the possible modes of ἀληθεύειν we came to know a distinguished one to which it comes solely and alone on *purely uncovering*: *θεωρεῖν* — and specifically the θεωρεῖν of σοφία, which has the sense of making the being visible *in its ἀρχαί*, out of what it as being already is in advance: the *ὄν ἀληθινόν*, or the *ἀληθές of the ὄν*. On the ground of this inner connection of being and being-uncovered the Greeks can say, in shortened speech, that philosophy goes to *ἀλήθεια* (*Met.* I, 3; 983b3). ἀλήθεια means at once the *un-concealment simply* of something, but also — corresponding to the meaning of λόγος — *the unconcealed itself, the unconcealedly-being*. The simple use of ἀλήθεια says nothing other than *the being in its being, insofar as it is properly uncovered*.

With ἀληθεύειν we have clarified the *manner of access* and the manner of regarding and uncovering, but *not* correspondingly the *thematic field* — not being-research itself, i.e. the theme of the being as Aristotle treats it in his ontology. Presenting Aristotelian ontology is out of the question even with more than a semester available. Only in a quite shortened procedure will we win in advance an orientation about what is treated in the dialogue — and, since the thematic field is determinable *through the manner of access and dealing*, most briefly so that we bring the manner of regarding in the dialogue, the ἀληθεύειν, still nearer with reference to the characteristic manner in which it lies *in Plato*: with reference to *διαλέγεσθαι*.

#### §28. First characterization of dialectic in Plato

##### a) διαλέγεσθαι as ἀληθεύειν. The basic sense of "dialectic": the *breaking-through* of idle talk, tendency toward seeing (νοεῖν)

If the preparation through the explication of ἀληθεύειν is grounded and is to be a genuine preparation, then out of it the *manner of regarding of the dialogue*, the διαλέγεσθαι, must become intelligible. With the clarification of διαλέγεσθαι's sense we win at once an understanding of *why the discussion happens in the manner of "dialogue" at all — why Plato philosophizes in dialogues*. This happens *not*, as one trivially says, because Plato was an artist and wanted to present such things beautifully too, but out of an *inner necessity of philosophizing itself* — out of the radical taking-up of the impulse and shock Socrates dealt him: *out of λόγος as idle talk* (*Gerede*), the at-first-given, the spoken-about and talked-around concerning all things, *to go through genuine speaking* to a λόγος that really, *as λόγος ἀληθής*, says something about what is discussed. διαλέγεσθαι is a *going-through the speaking*, setting out from something spoken, with the goal of arriving at an assertion, at a λόγος in the genuine sense, *about the being itself*. In this sense διαλέγεσθαι is — as it is later called in Plato's *Sophist* — a *διαπορεύεσθαι διὰ τῶν λόγων* (253b10), a *running-through of what is said*, such that it points up what in it could be *meant as being*.

διαλέγεσθαι has accordingly, like λόγος, the *function of uncovering* — an uncovering in the mode of *through-speaking*. This through-speaking sets in at what one at first and already beforehand *speaks* about what is to be discussed, goes *through* this, is *directed toward* and finds its *end* in a speaking that properly asserts something about the theme: in the assertion, in the genuine λόγος.

When we say that λόγος, here as διαλέγεσθαι, is uncovering — taken at any rate *in this facticity* — there lies in this that to λόγος an ἀληθεύειν belongs. More exactly seen: λόγος itself *as λόγος* does not without further ado make up the enactment-mode of ἀληθεύειν; the uncovering is in λόγος *as λόγος not ground-standing*. Rather λόγος *can* take over the accomplishment of uncovering, but *need not*. Factically, however, it is precisely *λόγος* that at first and for the most part *rules through all modes of uncovering* — so that all the earlier forms of ἀληθεύειν we came to know in Aristotle, except that of νοῦς, are determined by the character of *μετὰ λόγου*; they are enacted in the mode of *discussing*. λόγος, the discussing addressing, is the *nearest enactment-mode of ἀληθεύειν*, while νοῦς, pure perceiving, is as such *no possibility for the human*, the ζῷον λόγον ἔχον. νοεῖν is at first and for the most part a *διανοεῖν*, because the dealing-with is *dominated by λόγος*.

λόγος can thus *take over* the ἀληθεύειν; it has it *not from itself* but from the respective νοεῖν and διανοεῖν, or the respective αἴσθησις. **By its originary sense, and also by its originary facticity, λόγος is not uncovering at all but — speaking extremely — downright *concealing*.** λόγος is at first the *idle talk* that has the facticity of *not* letting the matters be seen, but of forming a peculiar *self-satisfaction* in stopping at what one so says. **The dominance of idle talk *closes off* the being precisely for Dasein and so makes it blind against the uncovered and the possible uncovering.** But if λόγος in this facticity as idle talk at first rules through Dasein, then the pressing-forward to the uncovered-being must go *precisely through it*. It must be such a speaking that, in speaking-for and speaking-against, it *leads more and more up to* what is spoken of and *lets this be seen*. διαλέγεσθαι thus has in itself the *immanent tendency toward a νοεῖν, toward a seeing*.

But insofar as the regard *remains in λέγειν*, holds itself as διαλέγεσθαι in the through-speaking, such a through-speaking can indeed *leave idle talk* but can only make the *attempt* to press forward to the matters themselves. **διαλέγεσθαι remains in discussing; it does not come to pure νοεῖν.** It does not have the proper means for that at which it is properly to be at an end: for *θεωρεῖν itself*. Although διαλέγεσθαι does not reach its goal, does not uncover the being simply, so long as it remains in λέγειν, it need be *no game*: it has a *genuine function*, insofar as it *breaks through idle talk*, controls idle talk, in speaking as it were *lays the finger* on what is meant, and so pre-gives the discussed matters in a first indication, for the first time and *in their nearest look*. **That is the basic sense of Platonic dialectic.** It has in itself the tendency toward a *seeing*, toward an *uncovering*. One cannot therefore grasp dialectic by making a distinction between *intuiting and thinking* and striking dialectic to the side of thinking. Dialectic is *not* a higher stage of so-called thinking over against so-called mere intuition — but the reverse: **its sole sense and sole tendency is, through the merely-discussed, to *prepare and form the genuine originary intuition*.** *[Heidegger's marginal note: Knowing — cf. Being and Time — and intuiting. Hegel at bottom likewise.]* That Plato did not press forward to *seeing the being itself* and, as it were, *overcoming dialectic* — this insufficiency, enclosed in his own dialectical procedure, determines certain moments of his dialectic, e.g. the much-discussed *κοινωνία τῶν γενῶν*, the community, the holding-itself-with-one-another of the kinds. **These characters are *no advantages* and no determinations of a superior philosophical method, but signs of a fundamental confusion and unclarity** which, as said, lies grounded in the *difficulty of the matters themselves*, in the difficulty of such first fundamental researches.

##### b) Critique of the traditional conception of dialectic. Dialectic: no thought-technique, but a preliminary stage of νοεῖν. Aristotle's position on dialectic

The dominance of λόγος later brings forth — as still today — a *retroactive effect*, in the "theoretical" generally and in the "logical." The history of philosophy and dialectically-oriented philosophical regard took *this* Platonic dialectic as its first ideal and saw in it a *superior* kind of philosophizing. In connection with this one contrived a *wonder-work of philosophical thought-technique*, a thought-technique of dialectical to-and-fro, a method that runs best when it is as *unburdened by matter-acquaintance* as possible, and to which nothing belongs but an *idling, wild-grown understanding*. **What for Plato was an inner necessity — to come to the matters — is here made into a principle of *playing* with the matters.** The Platonic exertion in dialectic runs precisely the opposite way: *to see the ὄν ἀληθινόν, what is*.

The reverse side of this misunderstanding is the *disparaging judgment* of Aristotle's position on dialectic. It has become a commonplace to say Aristotle *no longer understood* Platonic dialectic and dragged it down to a mere technique of inferring thought; more recently it is again stressed that Aristotle *deposed* the word "dialectic" from its high Platonic dignity. Such dicta, which philosophically mean little, spring from a *romantic conception of philosophy*. The sentence is *correct* if one only adds the right grounding — but not if behind it hides a *romantic regret*. **Aristotle deposed dialectic from its dignity not because he no longer understood it, but because he understood it *more radically*** — because he grasped Plato himself as, with his dialectic, *underway to θεωρεῖν*, because he succeeded in *actually making real* what Plato strove for. Aristotle *saw the immanent limits of dialectic because he philosophized more radically*. Through this delimiting he was at once put in a position to *give dialectic back its relative right*. He could do this only because he understood *what function λόγος and διαλέγεσθαι have within scientific regard and within human existence generally*. Only from a positive understanding of the phenomenon of λέγειν within life — as we find it in his *Rhetoric* — did Aristotle win the ground to interpret λέγεσθαι quite concretely and thereby see διαλέγεσθαι more sharply. **Aristotle could not drag dialectic *down* at all, because for him it is by its sense *already below*, i.e. a preliminary stage of θεωρεῖν** — and as preliminary stage not some acute operating and thinking, but by its sense *always already a wanting-to-see*, insofar as λόγος has precisely the sense of ἀποφαίνεσθαι, of letting-see. **Dialectic is no art of persuasion; it has rather, conversely, the sense of *bringing the fellow-conversant to seeing* and opening his eyes.**

##### c) The meanings of the expression "λόγος" in Plato

Plato speaks of λόγος in quite various meanings — not arbitrarily, but in an *indeterminacy that always has a certain foundation in the matters*. λόγος means:

1. *λέγειν* (the speaking itself).
2. *λεγόμενον* — and this has a double sense: it can mean the *discussed*, i.e. the *content*; but also
3. the *being-said*, the *being-spoken-out* of this content — so-and-so *has said it* — a mode of being of λόγος that rules precisely in *everyday Dasein*, so that, as Aristotle says, often the mere *being-said* suffices to awaken a *πίστις*, a conviction about what is said, without one's expressly appropriating the said content and the manner of saying.
4. λόγος means as much as *εἶδος*. This meaning hangs together with λόγος being able to mean λεγόμενον, the said — insofar as λέγειν means ἀποφαίνεσθαι, to let-see: what one *lets be seen* of the being in saying, the being thus *in how it looks*, in how it is pointed up in λόγος as ἀποφαίνεσθαι — so that λόγος can often be *identified with εἶδος* or Idea.
5. An *identification of λόγος with νοῦς, νοεῖν*. λόγος is the phenomenon grasped as the basic determination of what makes up the being of the human: the human as a living thing that *speaks*. But insofar as this speaking is the *enactment-mode* of seeing, of perceiving — of αἴσθησις as of νοεῖν — λόγος as basic character of the human's being becomes at once *representative* for the other determination of human ζωή, for *νοῦς*. On the detour of this phenomenal interlocking it then comes about that one *translates λόγος as "reason."* **But λόγος does not mean reason, and does not in itself have the sense of νοεῖν; it can only be the *enactment-mode* of perceiving.** One must be clear, in this usage, about what *unexplicated states of affairs* underlie it. *[Heidegger's marginal note: λόγος — ratio.]*
6. λόγος means *relation* (*Beziehung*). This becomes intelligible from the basic meaning of λέγειν: λέγειν τι κατά τινος, to address something *as* something, or *with a view to* something. In λέγειν lies a *looking-toward*, from one to the other; therefore λόγος means also as much as relation. *[Marginal note: λέγειν — generally to take-together — to relate.]*
7. λόγος means *ἀνάλογον*, "cor-responding," the corresponding, the corresponding-to as a determinate kind of being-related.

Heidegger restricts himself to this circuit of meanings because these predominantly meet us — and often *several meanings are meant in one*. From here it becomes intelligible how one step of the dialogical regard follows from another; this remains dark so long as one wants to hold fast an *isolated* meaning of λόγος.

As transition to the dialogue itself comes the short orientation about *διαλεκτική*. Aristotle speaks of dialectic at two distinguished places: (1) in connection with determining philosophy's task as the *fundamental science of the being*, *Metaphysics* IV, 2; (2) in the doctrine of λόγος in the *Topics* and in the treatise on fallacies (which properly belongs to the *Topics* as its last book) — thus (1) in connection with *σοφία*, (2) in connection with the *theory of λέγειν* in the sense of theoretical discussing. *[The editor notes: in the lecture Heidegger presented dialectic only with reference to Met. IV, 2; the planned presentation via the Topics was not carried out.]*

#### §29. Supplement: The innovation regarding the ground of Greek being-research in Plato's *Sophist*

*[This is the hour-transition from the 19th to the 20th session, 8–9 January 1925 — an expanded and more determinate version of the beginning of the 19th session, given separately here because of its own line of thought.]*

##### a) The double guiding thread of being-research in Plato's *Sophist*: concrete Dasein (the philosopher, the sophist); λέγειν

Taking the *Sophist* as a whole and setting out from its title, there results on closer inspection a *remarkable innovation* within previous Greek philosophical regard: for the discussion of being and the being, there is now set as *ground* a *determinate kind of existence* — that of the *philosopher*. For the dialogue has no other goal than to *explicate this ground of a concrete Dasein-kind* and thereby, as it were, to *create the milieu* within which the being can show itself in its being. This new *founding* of the research into the being of the being is remarkable over against the *point of departure of Greek being-regard generally* — over against the *position of Parmenides*, where being is simply determined *in correlation to νοεῖν*. That is fundamentally the same, insofar as the philosopher is the one who in a distinguished sense νοεῖ, perceives, regards — but with the difference that in Parmenides this νοεῖν remains *wholly indeterminate*: it is not said whether it is the νοεῖν of a determinate region of being or of the being generally; being is spoken of only *indeterminately*, and likewise νοεῖν. **The turn — as regards the *research*, not the *result* — lies in this: that the *ground* on which the question of the sense of being is posed now becomes *concrete*.** The task of appropriating the ground becomes *harder*, but the result *richer*. This shows in that the *non-being too* is recognized in its being, or at least *put to us as a question*. In both cases, as generally, it shows that about the being something can be made out *with respect to its being* only insofar as the being *is there* — insofar as the being *can encounter at all*. It comes solely to holding fast the *encountering being in its nearest and most originary manner of encountering* and asking, *within this*, after that *as which* it shows itself.

The *other* direction goes together with this immediately for a more concrete being-research, insofar as the encountering being — in naive ontology at first *the world* — encounters and *is there in everyday Dasein, which speaks about the world*, so that at once the *discussing and addressing* becomes a further *guiding thread* by which the question of being orients itself. It is asked: *how does the being look, insofar as it is addressed, discussed, λεγόμενον?* **This question of being along the guiding thread of λέγειν is at once the *proper origin of logic*.** "Logic" in the Greek sense has at first *nothing to do with thinking*, but stands wholly *within the task of the question of being*. So the *Sophist* — and generally the dialogues grouped around it — is a remarkable *turn* between the position of Parmenides and that of Aristotle, who completes all these beginnings of Greek ontology. This significance of the *Sophist* shows itself, to be sure, only if we grasp it *originally enough* in what did *not* come to issue and *could not* come to issue on this position. There remain fundamental difficulties not to be removed on this position — *which are there for us*. So not only the *encountering* world, but also the world *insofar as it is addressed*, is given in this double sense as guiding thread of being-research.

##### b) λόγος as the guiding thread of being-research in Aristotle ("Onto-logy")

λόγος, the discussing of the world and the being, has the role of the *guiding thread*, insofar as *in the λεγόμενον the being is there*. Even where being-research, as in Aristotle, comes *beyond* dialectic — beyond remaining attached to the addressed being — to the *pure grasping of the ἀρχαί*, to θεωρεῖν, even there it can be shown that λόγος is still *fundamental* for the final grasp of being. Aristotle too, although he *overcomes* dialectic, remains in his whole question-posing after being *oriented at λόγος*. **This state of affairs is the origin of what today is called *formal ontology*, and has been absorbed into it.** διαλέγεσθαι is a way of asking about the being with respect to its being in which λόγος *is and remains* guiding thread. But for Aristotle λόγος shows itself in its peculiar *relation-structure*: λέγειν is always a *λέγειν τι κατά τινος*. Insofar as λόγος addresses something *as* something, it is *fundamentally unsuited* to grasp what by its sense can *no longer be addressed as something else* but is to be grasped only *at itself*. Here λόγος, in this nearest and predominant structure, as it were *fails*. There remains, if one goes beyond it, only a *new idea of λόγος*: the *λόγος καθ' αὑτό*, as Aristotle showed in *Met.* VII, 4.

On the ground of this sharper insight into λόγος' structure Aristotle succeeds in characterizing Platonic dialectic itself *in its preliminariness*. He accomplishes this in connection with the research-kind called *"First Philosophy,"* which regards the being in its being. In connection with the exposition of the idea of an originary and first *science of being*, Aristotle appeals also to the *dialecticians and sophists*, insofar as he says that these too *make the claim to be philosophers* (*Met.* IV, 2; 1004b17). Together with this claim to philosophy, their cognition and cognitive interest is directed *at the whole*, at the *ὅλον*, at the *ἅπαντα*, at *all* beings — not at a determinate being. Aristotle takes from the fact that there are dialecticians and sophists *as improper philosophers* a *confirmation* that philosophy aims at the whole. It aims at the whole, ὅλον, in a quite determinate sense: *not* such that the *content-determinacies* of all beings there are would be enumerated, that it would be recounted what beings there are and how things are constituted in particular; but it aims at *the being insofar as it is, and only insofar*. It goes therefore *not*, as we would say, at the *ontic* — at the being itself such that I am absorbed in the being — but it goes at the being such that *I address the ὄν as ὄν*, the *ὄν λεγόμενον ᾗ ὄν*: I address the being so that it is addressed *solely with respect to its being* and in no other respect. **This idea of "*Onto-logy*" — of λέγειν, of addressing the being with respect to its being — Aristotle first exposed in all sharpness.** In this connection he comes to the delimitation of dialectic and sophistry over against this idea of a First Philosophy, which the exposition in *Metaphysics* IV will now make concrete.

#### §30. Philosophy — dialectic — sophistry in Aristotle (*Met.* IV, 1–2)

*[For the following interpretation of Met. IV, 1–2 no notes survive in Heidegger's manuscript, only the pointer: "Met. Γ, 1 and 2, cf. interpretation."]*

##### a) The idea of First Philosophy. First Philosophy as science of the ὄν ᾗ ὄν. Its delimitation against the particular sciences. Being as φύσις τις. The στοιχεῖα-research of the ancients. First and Second Philosophy

*Metaphysics* IV begins, as it were quite dogmatically: ἔστιν ἐπιστήμη τις ἣ θεωρεῖ τὸ ὄν ᾗ ὄν καὶ τὰ τούτῳ ὑπάρχοντα καθ' αὑτό (1003a21). "There is a science that *regards* the being *as* being" — i.e. with respect to its *being*, the being not as something else, not as so-and-so constituted, but as being, insofar as it *is* — "and that which at this, namely at the being with respect to its being, *ὑπάρχει*, is already there in advance," belongs *with* it as to being, and specifically *καθ' αὑτό*, to it *at itself*. There is thus a science that regards the *being-characters* of the being.

Traditional interpretation found a difficulty here: Aristotle designates First Philosophy as *ἐπιστήμη*, though ἐπιστήμη is no originary science over against *σοφία* — since ἐπιστήμη is a theoretical knowing that *presupposes* determinate principles, axioms, basic concepts. Strictly it contradicts the sense of ἐπιστήμη that it could thematically grasp something originary *in its originariness*; here it should have run *ἔστι σοφία τις*. One sees at once that this is a *counter-sense*. Aristotle wants, precisely *without terminological usage*, to say: over against the concrete particular sciences there is a "science" that *regards, θεωρεῖ*, the being in its being. ἐπιστήμη has here the quite *wide* sense of θεωρεῖν; one may not press the expression in the epideictic sense.

This science "is not the same as any of the others" — ούδεμιᾷ τῶν ἐν μέρει λεγομένων ἡ αὐτή (a22). One mostly translates as if λεγομένων referred to *ἐπιστημῶν*; but the connection, and later passages, make clear that the *λεγόμενα* are meant as *the matters themselves* to which the sciences relate. There is a manifold of sciences relating to that being which is *ἐν μέρει*, "addressed section-wise": sciences that *cut out* determinate districts from the whole of the being and address these purely at themselves, each delimited, and work them over in λέγειν. Each such science has its determinate *field*. To each field corresponds a determinate *αἴσθησις*, an originary perceiving in which the *basic matter-character* of a field is grasped, expressly or not — in geometry the space- or position-relation (which is not at all given with being as such), in *φυσική* the being insofar as it is *moved*. The physicist does not first *prove* that the thematic being is moved; it is *seen so in advance*. Every stem, every self-standing district of beings has a determinate αἴσθησις mediating access to its primary matter-character: space, movement. This says that this *μία αἴσθησις*, with reference to what is seen, is *ἐν μέρει*, "section-wise," compared with the *ὅλον*, the whole. But the science that regards the *being of the being* "falls together with none of these." οὐδεμία γὰρ τῶν ἄλλων ἐπισκοπεῖ καθόλου περὶ τοῦ ὄντος ᾗ ὄν, ἀλλὰ μέρος αὐτοῦ τι ἀποτεμόμεναι περὶ τούτου θεωροῦσι τὸ συμβεβηκός (1003a23): "None of the other sciences regards *in the whole* the being in its being; each cuts out a part of it and sets its regard over this part" — more exactly, over what is *proper* to the so-cut-out being as such (geometry over the position-relations themselves).

ἐπεὶ δὲ τὰς ἀρχὰς καὶ τὰς ἀκροτάτας αἰτίας ζητοῦμεν, δῆλον ὡς φύσεώς τινος αὐτὰς ἀναγκαῖον εἶναι καθ' αὑτήν (a26). "Because we seek the ἀρχαί — the departure-points, that from whence the being of the being is what it is — and specifically the *highest, first* causes, it is clear that these determinacies, the ἀρχαί, are determinacies *ὡς φύσεώς τινος*, of such a thing as is there *from itself*." This expression is characteristic and clarifying for Aristotle's whole idea of this science of being. **He can no longer say, as Plato still did, that the being of the being is itself *a being*.** The being of the being is something of a wholly peculiar kind that may not be characterized again with what it itself *categorially determines*. **I may not grasp the being of the being again as a being; I can grasp it only by winning, for being itself, immanent determinations *out of it*.** Aristotle helps himself by saying that being and the manifold of being-characters accruing to it καθ' αὑτό are *ὡς φύσεώς τινος*, such a thing as "is already there from itself." He says *φύσις* to stress that these characters of being at the being are not something that accrues to the being *merely insofar as it is addressed*, but what is *already there for* the ἀποφαίνεσθαι, the pointing-up in λέγειν. φύσις means a being that has the ἀρχή of its being *in itself*, and not — the opposite, ποίησις — one that would be there by grace of human cognizing and making. With this expression φύσις τις for the ὄν, more exactly for the *characters of being*, Aristotle indicates that they are there *from themselves as determinacies*.

He then points out (a28) that the *ancients*, when they asked after the *στοιχεῖα*, the elements of the being, and gave various answers — water, air, earth — were with this question *not* asking after a determinate *district* of beings, did not want to recount how the being itself looks content-wise, but were properly led by the interest of *determining the being of the being*. They were only not yet at the level of regard that understands that the being *as* being cannot be clarified out of a determinate district of beings but only *from being*. By this appeal to this imperfect kind of question after the being of the being, Aristotle wants — as he always does — to bring his idea of First Philosophy into a *continuous connection with the previous tradition* of research.

This science falls into the philosopher's task-field in the distinguished sense: περὶ τούτων … καὶ τῆς οὐσίας … λόγον ἔχειν (1004a32) — about the determinations of the being, and above all about *οὐσία*, it is necessary to *have the being as pointed-up in addressing*, i.e. to *point up the being of the being*; καὶ ἔστι τοῦ φιλοσόφου περὶ πάντων δύνασθαι θεωρεῖν (1004a34): "and it is the peculiar right, the task of the philosopher, *δύνασθαι*, to bear in himself as knower the possibility of setting the regard *περὶ πάντων*, over everything." But we know from *Met.* I, 2 that *περὶ πάντων* does not mean "over everything" in the sense of the *sum*, but *over the whole with respect to its origins*.

Aristotle develops this idea further by pointing out that every being that is what it is, is an *ἕν*. The *unity* — that every something is a something — falls equally into this science. Further belong here questions like εἰ ἓν ἓν ἐναντίον (1004b3), "whether there is such a thing as is, as One, *set against* the One" (ἐναντίον: over against, as it were lying *in the face of* it); further τί ἐστι τὸ ἐναντίον, what this "against," this against-like, properly is, and *ποσαχῶς λέγεται*, in how many ways one can speak of the against-like ("opposition" already misses the connection). This is not mere *program*: Aristotle carried out concrete investigations of the ἓν ἐναντίον in *Met.* V. This question-posing after the *being-structures of the being as such* makes up this fundamental science.

The kind of questioning is *formally the same* as in *Second Philosophy*, in the other philosophies that regard determinate districts of beings with respect to their being-structure. These do not *describe* the being — e.g. the φύσει ὄντα — but ask after their being-structure, explicate e.g. the idea of *κίνησις*; likewise they regard the object-field characterized by the title *ἀριθμός*, number. Aristotle makes a *sharp distinction* between number and the *ἕν*: the ἕν belongs still to the *ὄν*, the ἕν is *not yet a number*. **In Plato, by contrast, these connections still run through one another — which shows in that the Ideas themselves are grasped as numbers.** Likewise other regions have their determinate structures: the *στερεόν*, the solid, solidity (we would say: materiality); the *ἀκίνητον*, the unmoved in its unmovedness; the *ἀβαρές*, the un-heavy, and the heavy. All these beings have, with respect to their being, *ἴδια*, peculiar categorial determinations. And so there is a science that regards the being *as* being: οὕτω καὶ τῷ ὄντι ᾗ ὂν ἔστι τινὰ ἴδια (1004b15), "so there are also for the being, insofar as it is being, τινὰ ἴδια, determinate structures proper only to it"; καὶ ταῦτ' ἐστὶ περὶ ὧν τοῦ φιλοσόφου ἐπισκέψασθαι τἀληθές (b16), "and these being-characters are those regarding which the philosopher must regard the truth" — more sharply taken: must *see these in their un-coveredness*.

##### b) Delimitation of dialectic and sophistry against First Philosophy. The commonality of object: the "whole." The difference: philosophy = γνωριστική; dialectic = πειραστική; sophistry = φαινομένη σοφία (εὖ λέγειν)

οἱ γὰρ διαλεκτικοὶ καὶ σοφισταὶ τὸ αὐτὸ μὲν ὑποδύονται σχῆμα τῷ φιλοσόφῳ (1004b17): "the dialecticians and sophists put on — as it were *slip into* — the same *shape* as the philosopher." ἡ γὰρ σοφιστικὴ φαινομένη μόνον σοφία ἐστί (b18) — here one sees that Aristotle knows very well that the science he speaks of *is σοφία* — "sophistry *φαινομένη μόνον*, merely *looks like* philosophy"; καὶ οἱ διαλεκτικοὶ διαλέγονται περὶ ἁπάντων (b19), "and the dialecticians make *everything* the theme of their through-speaking," i.e. they do not move within a determinate field but claim to be able to stand answer over everything — exactly analogous to the sophists, who in their manner of education claim to educate young people so that they are put in a position to *εὖ λέγειν* over everything, "to transact and speak well."
