To both, sophists and dialecticians, it is proper κοινὸν δὲ πᾶσι τὸ ὄν ἐστιν (b20), "to have the being *in the whole* as theme"; περὶ μὲν γὰρ τὸ αὐτὸ γένος στρέφεται ἡ σοφιστικὴ καὶ ἡ διαλεκτικὴ τῇ φιλοσοφίᾳ (b22): "sophistry and dialectic move within the *same field* of the being as philosophy" — according to their *claim*. All three — dialectician, sophist, philosopher — claim to transact *over the whole*.

But now the difference. ἀλλὰ διαφέρει τῆς μὲν τῷ τρόπῳ τῆς δυνάμεως (b23): "philosophy differs from the one, namely *dialectic*, by the *manner of the ability*" — with respect to how far each *suffices*. Dialectic does not reach so far, is not so sufficient for its task as philosophy: dialectic is *πειραστική* (b25), or as Aristotle paraphrases in the *Topics*, *πεῖραν λαβεῖν*, "to make the attempt with something." **Dialectic makes the *attempt* — with what? With the pointing-up of the being in its being. It is *underway* to this goal, but does not suffice.** With respect to the *breadth of sufficiency*, dialectic differs from proper philosophy; it remains *pre-ordered and sub-ordered* to it. τῆς δὲ τοῦ βίου τῇ προαιρέσει (b24): "from the other, namely *sophistry*, philosophy differs in the manner of the *anticipating of the mode of existence*," literally translated. That is to say: **the βίος of the philosopher is set upon *pure matter-directedness* (*Sachlichkeit*).** The philosopher has, as representative of this radical research, decided *absolutely* for pure matter-directedness. With the sophist too there is a *προαίρεσις*, but *another*. It comes to him on *educating* — and indeed to a determinate kind of existence: to put others in the possibility of *εὖ λέγειν*, "transacting well," about everything the philosopher also treats. Thereby it is *completely disregarded* whether this being-able-to-speak about the things really *speaks from the matters themselves*. In sophistry it comes solely to this: that about *anything whatever* standing in discussion it can be spoken *in a distinguished way*. **Sophistry has the ideal of an intellectual existence directed solely at the *formal being-able-to-speak*** — which for the Greeks had a distinguished significance. It has the ideal of the right and beautiful being-able-to-speak about all things, *apart from whether what is said is correct or not*. **The sophist decides for this *formal-aesthetic* ideal of human existence — i.e. properly for *un-matter-directedness* (*Unsachlichkeit*)** — while the philosopher has the προαίρεσις for the βίος of the pure θεωρεῖν of the ἀληθές, of the uncovered at itself.

What thus lies *in the distance* for dialectic, in whose direction it moves — regarding *this* the philosopher is not πειραστικός but *γνωριστικός* (b26): he *makes familiar* with it. He has the possibility, the δύναμις, which, when it makes earnest, *can let the whole be seen* in its being and being-structure. Sophistry, by contrast, is *φαινομένη*, it merely *looks so*, but at bottom has another ideal: *οὖσα δ' οὔ* — it *is not really* philosophy. So one sees from this orientation that **dialectic is by Aristotle not simply negated but characterized as *πειραστική*, and so has a determinate *positive sense*.** It has in common with philosophy that it speaks, as Aristotle says in the *Topics*, *κατὰ τὸ πρᾶγμα*, "with a view to the matters," while for the sophist it comes *not* to the matter-directedness of the speech but solely to the *εὖ*, to arguing and discussing beautifully and brilliantly and *in seemingly genuine proof-manner*.

#### §31. First characterization of sophistry, continued

##### a) The idea of παιδεία in sophistry and in Aristotle. εὖ λέγειν. Un-matter-directedness and matter-directedness. Pre-sketch of ἀληθεύειν as the ground of sophistry

Note that **Plato knows only the *simple* difference between dialectic and sophistry, whereas in Aristotle, through the sharper grasp of the sense of the dialectical, a *threefold* articulation springs out: philosophy, dialectic, sophistry.** Aristotle distinguishes dialectic and philosophy with respect to *reach*; and both against sophistry with respect to the *manner* in which the sophist on the one side, philosopher and dialectician on the other, *give themselves over to their matter*. Over against the sophist, dialectician and philosopher are determined by this: that they are *in earnest* with what they speak about, that in their speaking they have the *intention of bringing what they speak about matter-directedly to understanding*, while the sophist attends *not* to the content of the speech but solely to the *speech itself*, to *keeping the upper hand and shining*.

Therefore the idea guiding the sophists is *παιδεία*, a certain being-educated *with respect to speaking about all things*. This παιδεία has *formal* character, in the sense of being able to speak *εὖ*, well, about everything. Aristotle too knows this ideal of being-educated in the sense of scientific formation, and with him too it has in a certain respect a *formal* meaning: παιδεία is not confined to a determinate matter-field. **But in Aristotle παιδεία means being-educated with a view to the possibility of *measuring oneself in each case to the matter* spoken of — thus precisely the *opposite* of what παιδεία means with the sophists**: with them, being-educated in the sense of a fundamental and thoroughgoing *matter-unconcernedness*; with Aristotle, being-educated *to speak of the matter in each case adequately*. Since there are regionally many matters, this παιδεία cannot simply be characterized content-wise; it concerns a determinate kind of formation — the *methodical* formation of the scientific level with respect to questioning and preparing investigations. Through this delimitation sophistry is at once brought into the connection by which *philosophy itself* is determined: that of *ἀληθεύειν*, of uncovering the being.

Heidegger declines to go into the historical conditions and a historical characterization of sophistry (referring to Diels, *Fragmente der Vorsokratiker* II), noting that the main stock of the proper sources is taken *from Plato himself*, so that discussion of sophistry's historical standing is, given *Plato's prejudice against it*, bound up with certain difficulties. **Our regard goes in another direction: not to sophistry in its cultural significance, but to understanding, out of the idea of the sophist himself, that with which he as sophist gives himself over: *semblance, the false, the Not, and negation*.**

##### b) Critique of the traditional interpretation of sophistry

Sophistry as it historically formed itself is interpreted in the usual history of philosophy *as though the sophists were representatives of determinate philosophical directions* in knowing and living — so that one makes the sophists representatives of a *skepticism, relativism, subjectivism*, and whatever these terms are called. **This conception is untenable**, insofar as the sophists from the outset did *not* have the tendency toward a matter-directed treatment of scientific questions. They therefore also did not have the *concrete means* to philosophize scientifically, so that one cannot ascribe to them any determinate scientific conviction — *not even that of skepticism*. What one so interprets is in reality with them only *object of speech and of arguing*, not object of *regarding*. E.g. Protagoras' sentence "the human is the measure of all things" **is not the expression of a relativism or skepticism**, in which one then further seeks an epistemology.

This traditional interpretation is occasioned by one's trying to understand what became positively known of scientific-philosophical attempts *as a counter-movement against sophistry*. But in this attempt one takes what Plato, Aristotle, and Socrates worked *against* as itself on the *same level* as Plato and Aristotle. **One overlooks that scientific philosophy did not arise in a counter-movement against *doctrinal contents, schools*, and the like, but out of a *radical reflection on existence*** — an existence which in public Greek life was determined by the *educational ideal* of the sophists, and *not* by a determinate philosophical direction. Only in passing *through Plato* can one come to make the sophists representatives of determinate philosophical systems. This is a *perverted picture* of the intellectual development of the Greeks generally, and above all of scientific philosophy itself.

##### c) Sophistry and rhetoric. The differing position of rhetoric in Plato and in Aristotle. The commonality of their judgment of sophistry (φαινομένη σοφία)

Plato, in his fight against sophistry — which he, as in part still Aristotle, *identified with rhetoric* — condemned rhetoric along with it; i.e. he did not succeed in coming to a *positive understanding of rhetoric*. Only Aristotle comes to this, seeing that this kind of speech *has its sense in everyday life*, insofar as in everyday discussing and deliberating it does not come so much to disclosing the real, strict truth as merely to forming a *δόξα*, a *πίστις*, a conviction. Through the positive regard Aristotle exercised in the *Rhetoric*, rhetoric *separates itself off* from sophistry — which in Plato were still identified. This is clear from the Platonic dialogues titled after Greek sophists. *Gorgias*: ταὐτόν ἐστιν σοφιστὴς καὶ ῥήτωρ, ἢ ἐγγύς τι καὶ παραπλήσιον (520a6): "the sophist and the rhetor are the same, or at any rate come very near each other and are alike." The characteristic of the sophists — who as *paid teachers* of youth claim to work out this determinate formation — the rhetor has too, insofar as his goal too is to bring about the παιδεία in the sense of the *δεινότης of εὖ λέγειν*, to put one in a position to speak well. The sophist of the highest intellectual level, correspondingly esteemed by Plato and Aristotle, is *Protagoras of Abdera*: his work did not in fact stop at rhetoric, but in connection with reflection on speech he struck upon the formation of determinate *grammatical* basic concepts. Likewise *Prodicus of Ceos* engaged the question of *meaning-connections*.

Fundamentally the judgment of sophistry is *the same* in Aristotle and Plato. The determination we came to know in Aristotle — that σοφιστική is *φιλοσοφία φαινομένη, οὖσα δ' οὔ* (*Met.* IV, 2; 1004b26) — we find almost *word for word* in Plato's *Sophist*: πάντα ἄρα σοφοὶ τοῖς μαθηταῖς φαίνονται (233c6), "they *appear*, give themselves out to the pupils in every respect as such as know and understand" — σοφοὶ φαίνονται, thus φιλοσοφία φαινομένη; and Plato says οὐκ ὄντες γε (233c8), "in fact they *are* not." The sophists do not have the *ἀλήθεια*, such that they address the *uncovered matter*; rather they move in a *δοξαστικὴ περὶ πάντων ἐπιστήμη* (233c10), in a knowing that is only δοξαστική, that only *looks like* knowing and according to its claim extends to everything. δοξαστική is to be taken in a *double* sense: it means as much as *φαινομένη*, seeming — and at once therein lies the *ground why* the ἐπιστήμη is φαινομένη: because it gives not the ἀλήθεια but only *δόξαι*, views, *of* the matter, not the matter itself.

##### d) ἀληθεύειν as the ground of the question of the μὴ ὄν (= ψεῦδος)

With the reflection on ἀληθεύειν we have at once won the ground for understanding *why the sophist becomes theme for the question of the being of the non-being*. Insofar as ἀληθεύειν has the sense of *uncovering the being in its being* *[Heidegger's marginal note: ἀλήθεια — beingness]*, the *ψεύδεσθαι*, the distorting, the deceiving, is as its *opposite* the kind of comportment in which the being — this is where the transition stands, and it is the hinge on which the whole dialogue will turn. The sophist is not chosen as a cultural target or a philosophical opponent; he is chosen because his *existence* is the concrete place where deceiving, and therefore the *non-being*, factically *is*. To pin down what the sophist is, one must say what deception is; to say what deception is, one must say how the μὴ ὄν can be. The path from ἀληθεύειν to the ontological revolution of the *Sophist* is thus complete, and the dialogue itself can begin.

Deception is the kind of comportment in which the being is *covered over and distorted*, the kind in which something *shows itself* — or "is" — *as something it at bottom is not*, so that the non-being can, with the *facticity of error and deception*, at once be pointed up *as being*. That is the inner connection between ἀληθές–ὄν and ψεῦδος–μὴ ὄν. It comes to bringing *ψεύδεσθαι itself* nearer, in order to win the ground for making present the *μὴ ὄν* itself.

#### §32. Continuation: The idea of First Philosophy in Aristotle

##### a) First Philosophy as ontology (ὄν ᾗ ὄν) and theology. Explication of the doubling out of the Greek understanding of being (= presence)

The idea of First Philosophy as the originary science of the being is *crossed* in Aristotle by another fundamental science, which he designates *θεολογική*, so that we have: **πρώτη φιλοσοφία = the science that regards the ὄν ᾗ ὄν** (later called "ontology" — Aristotle does not know the expression), **and θεολογική** — both laid claim to *as πρώτη φιλοσοφία*.

This doubling can be traced further into the Middle Ages and into modern ontology. One has tried to *mediate* between ontology and theology in Aristotle, in order to win a "rounded world-picture" of Aristotle. **This way is unfruitful** for understanding the matters at issue. Rather the question is to be posed *why Greek science came onto this way*, so that it landed, as it were, at these two fundamental sciences. Theology has the task of clarifying the being as a whole — the ὅλον, the being of the world, nature, the heaven and all beneath it — *in its origins*, in that through which it properly *is*. It is to be noted that **this clarification of the being in the whole through the unmoved mover has *nothing to do with a proof of God* on the ground of a causal inference.** Theology has the whole, the ὅλον, as theme; and *ontology too* has the whole as theme and regards its ἀρχαί. Both take their departure from the being as whole, as ὅλον, and it comes to them to understand the ὅλον *as being*.

Why did Greek science and philosophy come to these two basic sciences? In *Plato* they are still wholly promiscuous, much less clarified than in Aristotle — but factically he already moves in these two dimensions. **This is intelligible only out of the sense being had for the Greeks: the being is *what is present in the proper sense*.** Theology regards the being in what it *already is in advance*, in what in the most proper and highest sense makes up the *presence of the world*: the **most proper and highest presence** of the being is theology's theme. **Ontology's theme is the being insofar as it is present *in all its determinations*** — not cut to a determinate field, not only the unmoved mover and the heaven but also what is under the heaven, everything that is there, mathematical being as well as physical. So: for theology the theme is *the highest and most proper presence*; for ontology, *what makes up presence as such in general*. Into these two originary dimensions of reflection on being the development of Greek science is *pressed*.

The proper difficulty lies *not* in the θεολογική, which within this approach is relatively clear both for the Greeks and for us, but in *ontology* — more exactly in the question: *what sense do the characters of being, which accrue universally to every being insofar as it is, have with a view to the respective concrete being?* Later this question was expressed school-fashion: whether the universal determinations given in ontology about the being in its being have the character of *genera* — whether ontology is the science of the highest genera of all that is — or whether these characters of being have structurally *another relation* to the being.

Surveying the development of this whole question-posing from the Greeks and Aristotle to the present, Heidegger delivers a stark verdict: **we have in fact not come a step forward — on the contrary, the position the Greeks had reached has been *lost* to us, so that we no longer even *understand* these questions. The whole Hegelian "Logic" moves in a complete non- and mis-understanding of all these questions.** Only *Husserl* again, in connection with his idea of logic, as it were *discovered* the question of the sense of the formal being-determinations — though also only in a first, admittedly very important, approach. And it is *no accident* that this question surfaced in connection with a clarification of the idea of *logic*: because this science is ultimately oriented *at λόγος* — because its theme is the being insofar as it is *ὄν λεγόμενον*, addressed being, insofar as it is theme *for λόγος*. *[Heidegger's marginal note: Being and thinking.]*

##### b) λόγος as guiding thread for the being-research of σοφία. Explication of the guiding-thread function of λόγος out of the Greek understanding of being

Aristotle strives, in the idea of σοφία, *beyond* λόγος to a νοεῖν free of λέγειν. But more exactly seen, **even his determination of what is last ἀρχή, what is ἀδιαίρετον, is won only in orientation at λόγος.** This shows in that the basic determination of the ὄν, *οὐσία*, has the character of *ὑποκείμενον* — of what already lies before in advance, the wholly primary *presence*; that is the formal determination of something that *is* at all. And this ὑποκείμενον, what is already there in advance, is seen *out of the view toward λέγειν*: what, in a speaking about something, in the discussing of a nexus of beings, is there *in advance before all speaking* for this speaking — namely *that about which is spoken* — that is the ὑποκείμενον, the ὄν, the οὐσία in formal sense. **The basic character of being is drawn out of the connection of λόγος itself.**

Therefore — because λόγος is guiding thread — with First Philosophy there stands, *as regards the question-posing* and not as regards the theory, again "logic" as we today say, the *λέγεσθαι*, dialectic, in connection. **That is the sense of the phrase one hears now and then, that in Aristotle metaphysics is logical and logic metaphysical.** It has the sense that even the ἀληθεύειν of σοφία, the uncovering in the simple sense, remains in a certain sense *μετὰ λόγου* — that for the explication of a pre-given theme (were it only the wholly bare *something* in general) the *addressing and discussing* is guiding thread. **This irruption of λόγος, of the logical in this strictly Greek sense, into the question-posing after the ὄν is motivated by this: that the ὄν, the being of the being itself, is primarily interpreted as *presence*, and λόγος is the way in which I *primarily make present to myself* something — namely that about which I speak.**

## MAIN PART: PLATONIC BEING-RESEARCH — INTERPRETATION OF THE *SOPHIST*

### Preliminary remarks

#### §33. The sense of the preparation so far: winning the ground for the matter-directed understanding of a specifically Greek dialogue. Its insufficiency

Passing over now to the regard of what the dialogue has as theme, it must be said — though the preparation may already have seemed to some too circumstantial and far-flung — that **it does not suffice**, and that the ideal of a preparation for an interpretation is far from reached. An ideal preparation would first be won if it put one in a position, presupposing a strict and composed reading, *really to appropriate the dialogue in one stroke without hindrance of understanding* — i.e. if it had accomplished that all the *matter-horizons* within which the conversation moves had become *completely awake and available* for the reader. This equipping our introduction has not reached and, under present circumstances, never will.

Nevertheless we must hold fast the ideal of an interpretation whose sole goal is **to let the dialogue speak purely for itself**. That is self-evident; today everyone claims to let the texts themselves speak. **That has today become a phrase.** But the *obligation* one takes on with this claim is mostly not understood. For it does not suffice that one adduces as large a text-material as possible and says nothing that does not stand in the text — that in no way guarantees that one has understood even the least. Rather, in this claim to let the text speak for itself lies the task of *not first nailing down* the matters spoken of, but of *letting these matters be pre-given from a further-pressing understanding*. **In this claim lies the obligation to be, in understanding of the matter-problematic, fundamentally *further along* than what is object of the interpretation.** Having understood this sense of the claim, one has at once the corresponding occasion for *modesty*. For this being-further cannot for us mean being *superior* to Greek scientific philosophy, but can only mean: having understood that **we have to place ourselves in a *relation of service* toward these researches**, in order, under their leading, first to make the attempt to hear out their immanent tendencies, to grasp and hold them in more originary working-through, and so to lay more firmly the ground on which the discussion of the matters has to unfold.

When one reads ἀλήθεια and ἐπιστήμη and so on, it does not suffice to speak in terminological echoes of word-concepts of truth, science, semblance, deception, proposition and the like; nor — what one likes to take for matter-directed interpretation — to leave everything in indeterminacy, to take the *un-understood end* to help out the *un-appropriated beginning*, or to explain one passage by another; nor to fetch in passages from other dialogues speaking on the same theme. **Understanding "Plato out of Plato, Aristotle out of Aristotle" — *there is no such thing*.** The decisive lies always again in the *confrontation (Auseinandersetzung) with the matters* spoken of. So long as we do not set out to point up and clarify *at the matters themselves* what is spoken of, so long is understanding Platonic and Aristotelian philosophy — and any philosophy — not to be thought of.

Grasping the interpretive task so, one knows at once, even without a history of philosophy, that there is a **continuity of radical questioning and researching** — a continuity that does not show itself in the least in the aspect of what one knows of philosophical directions, problems, systems, works, persons, but lies *behind* all that. **The past in this sense becomes living only when we have understood that *we ourselves are it*.** We are, in the sense of our intellectual existence, *what we were*; we will be what we *appropriate and administer* of what we were, and *how* we do it. From these simple *time-relations* of human and especially intellectual existence one sees the proper sense of real research as *confrontation with history* — which becomes existent only when a research is *historical*, i.e. understands that *it itself is history*. Only so is there the possibility of the *historiological*. Such research then needs, for its justification, no appeal to supra-temporal and eternal values and the like.

#### §34. Recapitulation: First characterization of sophistry. Delimitation against dialectic and philosophy. The esteem of εὖ λέγειν: un-matter-directedness — matter-directedness

Sophistry is characterized by *Unsachlichkeit* (un-matter-directedness) — but in a quite determinate sense: not an accidental, arbitrary, occasional one, but a *fundamental* one, which yet **may not be understood as though in the sophists there lived a *disposition* of fundamentally wanting to twist and cover over the matters, as though they wanted only to deceive.** Better determined, it is *Sachlosigkeit*, matter-lessness: an un-matter-directedness grounded in something *positive* — in a determinate *esteem of the dominance of speech and of the speaking human*. The spoken word in its dominance, in individuals as in the community, is what makes up the decisive for the sophist. Insofar as this stiffening upon the word — upon the beautifully and strikingly spoken word — means the obligation, as speaking, always to speak *about something*, the interest in speaking itself, *from itself*, is already un-matter-directed simply in that it places the accent solely on the *formal* of speech and argumentation. Since every speech goes about something, and since the sophist speaks, he *must* speak about something, whether the matter interests him or not. But precisely in that it does *not* interest him — insofar as he is not *bound* to the matter he speaks about — he becomes, by placing the sense solely in *beautiful speaking*, un-matter-directed: un-matter-directedness as *unburdenedness by the matter-content of what is spoken*.

Now insofar as **speech is the basic kind of access to and dealing with world** — insofar as it is the way in which the world is at first *there*, and not only the world but also the other humans and in each case the individual himself — **the matter-lessness of speech is equivalent to the *un-genuineness and uprooting of human existence*.** That is the proper sense of sophistry's un-matter-directedness as matter-lessness. And it is to be considered that existence is seen by the Greeks as *existence in the πόλις*. The counter-pole to this uprooted existence — the proper sense of existence — lies in *matter-directedness*, in the uncovering and fundamental understanding of the being: in the *idea of scientific philosophy* as it became living first through Socrates and then in concrete execution through Plato and Aristotle.

**The difficulty of the dialogue lies neither in the specifically ontological treatise on non-being and negation and the like, nor in the complicatedness of the divisions with which the regard sets in.** The proper difficulty lies in *bringing rightly into view the connection of the whole* — and with it the matter of which properly and ultimately is spoken — so that from it, as from a *unitary source*, the understanding of every single sentence is fed.

#### §35. Construction and articulation of the *Sophist*

##### a) General characterization of the construction. The traditional division: introduction, shell, kernel. Adoption and critique

The dialogue is in its construction and articulation relatively transparent. Heidegger adopts the articulation given by H. Bonitz (*Platonische Studien*, 3rd ed., Berlin 1886), which is also mostly taken over — but stresses that **no particular value is to be placed on this articulation; it says nothing for the understanding, but has only the sense of an *external orientation*.**

Roughly spoken, the dialogue consists of an "introduction," an *enclosing shell*, and a *kernel*. **This image is at once characteristic of the way one takes such a dialogue.** The introduction accomplishes the preparation of the conversation; the enclosing shell, one says, is the question after the *essence of the sophist*, treated first but then *interrupted* by the question after the *being of the non-being*; in this question one sees the *kernel-piece* of the dialogue; at the end of this question the conversation leads back over to the first-taken-up question of the sophist's essence, so that this *encloses as shell* the question of the being of the non-being.

**Such a harmless division as that into "introduction," enclosing shell, and kernel already indicates that one remains stuck *externally, literarily*, at material occurrences and themes of the conversation, and dispenses oneself from asking after the *articulation of the matter itself*** — i.e. after what it is that is transacted. Out of the external articulation one has posed equally external *problems*. Following this pictorial orientation one made for oneself the difficulty that *the title* concerns only what makes up the *shell* and precisely not the inner piece: for what is properly Plato's intention, the question of the being of the non-being, does not come to expression in the title; and what the title indicates is only a playful imitation of sophistry. **This division into shell and kernel is a classical example of how a pictorial separation of *material and form*, without any orientation at the proper questions, can bring forth *pseudo-problems*** — such as why the dialogue is called *Sophist* when the main thing in it treats the being of the non-being.

We will try from the outset, already in regarding the preparation of the conversation, to *free ourselves from this external division*. That says nothing other than that we strive from the outset to bring out the *connection* in which the dialogue moves — i.e. the *matter-connection of the phenomena* that are theme of the *whole* dialogue, and not something within the inner piece or the shell. **This connection between what is pictorially characterized as kernel and as shell must yield itself *matter-directedly*.**

##### b) The articulation of the *Sophist* (after H. Bonitz)

*Introduction*: chs. 1–2, 216a–218b.

**I. Seeking the definition of the sophist** (chs. 3–24, 218b5–237b7):
1. An example of the method of defining: the definition of the *ἀσπαλιευτής* (angler), chs. 4–7, 219a4–221c4.
2. The first six definitions of the sophist, chs. 8–19, 221c5–231c9.
3. The preparation of the ground-standing or proper definition, chs. 20–24, 232b–236c.

The single definitions of the sophist (chs. 8–24): (a) *preparatory* definitions, chs. 8–19 — 1st def. 221c–223b; 2nd def. 223b–224d; 3rd and 4th def. 224d–e; 5th def. 224e–226a; 6th def. 226a–231c; summary 231d–232a. (b) *ground-standing* definition, chs. 20–24 — 7th def. 232b–236c (cf. continuation 264c). The 7th definition of the sophist as *ἀντιλογικός* gives the point of departure for regarding the basic problem:

**II. The being of the non-being** (chs. 25–47, 237b9–264b9):
1. The difficulties in the concept of the non-being, chs. 25–29, 237b9–242b5.
